The practitioner does not “go” anywhere; rather, layers of identification are seen through and released.</p>
<p>Since it involves intentional movement through levels of reality, it belongs to śāktopāya. Yet as the final veil thins, the method dissolves into śāmbhavopāya—the spontaneous recognition of the Supreme (<em>parā</em>) as one’s own nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 54 offers a map of interiority—not as cosmology, but as liberation through refinement. The "elements" (tattvāni) are not just physical substances, but modes of self-identification: the body, emotions, thoughts, the sense of "I am," even the luminous witness. Each is subtler than the last, yet each is still a veil over pure awareness.</p>
<p>To "merge one after another" is to inquire: Is this me?<br />
—The body? No.<br />
—The feelings? No.<br />
—The thoughts? No.<br />
—The witness of thoughts? Still not final.</p>
<p>This is not rejection, but recognition. Each layer is honored, then released—not as false, but as incomplete. The dream state is more subtle than waking, deep sleep subtler than dream, and the silent witness subtler still. But even this witness is transcended where subject and object collapse.</p>
<p>"Revealed" does not mean the Supreme appears as a new experience. She is what remains when all that is not-Self has been seen through. The world and body are not denied; they are recognized as expressions of her play—temporary, luminous, and empty of separate existence.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not a journey upward, but a deepening inward—until the last ripple of self-reference stills, and what was always whole stands revealed.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>55</h1>
<h3>Freedom Within Dreams</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
पिण्डं च दुर्बलं शक्तिं ध्यात्वा द्वादशगोचरे।<br>
प्रविश्य हृदये ध्यायन् स्वप्नस्वातन्त्र्यमाप्नुयात्॥५५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
piṇḍaṃ ca durbalaṃ śaktiṃ dhyātvā dvādaśa-gocare |<br>
praviśya hṛdaye dhyāyan svapna-svātantryam āpnuyāt ||55||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Meditating on the breath-energy<br />
as resonant and slow,<br />
held within the dvādaśānta—<br />
and, entering the heart while falling asleep,<br />
one who continues this awareness<br />
gains freedom within the dream.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This method works with the subtle rhythm of breath at the threshold of sleep. The practitioner sustains awareness of <em>śakti</em>—the energy of breath—as <em>piṇḍaṃ ca durbalaṃ</em>: “resonant” (producing a soft inner sound, as in ujjāyī) and “slow” or “prolonged” (<em>durbalam</em>, here meaning extended, relaxed). This breath is anchored in <em>dvādaśa-gocara</em>, the field of the dvādaśānta, which manifests in three experiential centers: the heart, the throat, or the space between the brows—all regarded as expressions of the true <em>hṛdaya</em>, the non-local heart of awareness.</p>
<p>Maintaining this focus while transitioning into sleep, the practitioner carries conscious presence into the dream state. As a result, <em>svapna-svātantryam</em>—mastery or freedom in dreaming—arises: the dreamer recognizes the dream as dream and moves within it with full creative autonomy. This is not mere lucidity, but the expression of <em>svātantrya</em>, the divine freedom of consciousness, now active even in the subtle realm of sleep.</p>
<p>Though it uses breath and posture (placing it initially in āṇavopāya), the moment awareness permeates the dream, it becomes śāktopāya—awareness itself guiding the subtle body.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 55 bridges waking discipline and nocturnal liberation. The “resonant, slow breath” is not forced; it is the natural breath of deep relaxation, accompanied by a faint inner hum—a thread of awareness woven into the fabric of sleep.</p>
<p>The <em>dvādaśa-gocara</em>—the “reach of the twelve”—is not a fixed point, but the expanse of inner space where attention stabilizes. Whether felt at the heart (emotional center), throat (expression), or brow (insight), it is the same openness: the doorway between waking and dreaming.</p>
<p>To “enter the heart while falling asleep” is to fall <em>into</em> awareness, not out of it. Most lose consciousness at sleep’s edge; here, one surrenders the body but retains the thread of presence. As dream images arise, they are no longer mistaken for reality. The dream becomes a field of spontaneous awareness—where consciousness freely manifests whatever it wills: visions of deities, landscapes of insight, or symbolic resolutions of waking knots.</p>
<p>This “freedom in dreaming” (<em>svapna-svātantryam</em>) is more than control—it is recognition. The dreamer knows: <em>This is me. This is my awareness dreaming itself.</em> And in that knowing, the boundary between waking, dreaming, and deep sleep begins to dissolve.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about manipulating dreams, but about waking up within them—and in doing so, revealing that all states, all worlds, are nothing but the spontaneous expression of the one, unbound awareness.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>56</h1>
<h3>Gross, Subtle, Supreme</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
भुवनाध्वादिरूपेण चिन्तयेत्क्रमशोऽखिलम्।<br>
स्थूलसूक्ष्मपरस्थित्या यावदन्ते मनोलयः॥५६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
bhuvanādhvādi-rūpeṇa cintayet kramaśo 'khilam |<br>
sthūla-sūkṣma-parasthityā yāvad ante manolayaḥ ||56||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Meditate successively on the entirety of manifestation—<br />
through the sequential stages of worlds and principles—<br />
in their gross, subtle, and supreme dimensions—<br />
until the mind dissolves into its source.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice unfolds through the three adhvans (paths of manifestation). The bhuvanādhva, or path of worlds, includes the gross experiential universe from Pātāla to Satyaloka, perceived as tangible form. The tattvādhva, or path of principles, reveals the 36 tattvas that structure experience—from earth to Śiva—subtle frameworks normally invisible to ordinary perception. The kalādhva, or path of powers, discloses the five kalās (nivr̥tti, pratiṣṭhā, vidyā, śānti, śāntyatīta) that pulse as the supreme flow of consciousness beyond all division.</p>
<p>The practitioner doesn't intellectualize these layers but experiences them as descending refinement. Starting with the gross world (sthūla), attention moves to the subtle architecture of the tattvas (sūkṣma), and finally recognizes that even these arise from the indivisible play of Śakti (parā).</p>
<p>This sequential refinement (kramaśaḥ) isn't mechanical—it unfolds naturally as awareness deepens. When conceptual scaffolding dissolves at the culmination (yāvad ante), the mind has no object to grasp and dissolves into its source (manolayaḥ). While it begins in śāktopāya (using contemplation as support), it culminates in śāmbhavopāya: direct, objectless recognition of one's true nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse offers a cosmic homecoming, not an escape. It reverses the usual flow of manifestation—not building reality from One to many, but tracing the many back to the One from which they arise.</p>
<p>The "entirety of manifestation" (akhilam) includes everything we perceive—mountains and thoughts, emotions and galaxies. The "sequential stages" (kramaśaḥ) aren't a ladder to climb, but layers to see through: the gross world of objects; the subtle principles that structure them; and the supreme dimension where all distinction collapses.<br />
What does this mean in immediate experience? When you see a tree, the gross (sthūla) is its physical form. The subtle (sūkṣma) is the network of life-force, consciousness, and elements that sustain it. The supreme (parā) is the luminous awareness in which both tree and perceiver arise. To "meditate successively" is to see all three at once—not as analysis, but as a single flash of recognition that collapses the illusion of separation.</p>
<p>"Mind dissolves" (manolayaḥ) is not unconsciousness. It is the moment when the mind stops dividing reality into "me" and "not-me." When you gaze at a sunset without the subtle commentary of "I am seeing beauty," the sunset doesn't vanish—it intensifies. The mind that named and claimed has dissolved, and only seeing remains.</p>
<p>This is not rejection of the world but its consummation. When the last veil of separation burns away, what remains is not absence but the one taste of reality: Bhairava, not as a distant ideal, but as the space in which all experience arises, dances, and dissolves. In that dissolution, you don't lose the world—you find it as your own heart.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>57</h1>
<h3>Contemplating Shiva Throughout</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अस्य सर्वस्य विश्वस्य पर्यन्तेषु समन्ततः।<br>
अध्वप्रक्रियया तत्त्वं शैवं ध्यात्वा महोदयः॥५७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
asya sarvasya viśvasya paryanteṣu samantataḥ |<br>
adhva-prakriyayā tattvaṃ śaivaṃ dhyātvā mahodayaḥ ||57||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Contemplating the Śaiva Reality<br />
throughout this entire universe—<br />
at its furthest reaches in every direction—<br />
by the method of the adhvan,<br />
one awakens to the Great Dawn.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana invites expansive, non-dual vision. The practitioner stands or sits immersed in the world—garden, sky, earth—and perceives the totality of manifestation (<em>asya sarvasya viśvasya</em>) not as external, but as the very field of Śiva’s being. Using <em>adhva-prakriyā</em>, the Tantric understanding of reality’s layered unfolding (bhuvanādhva, tattvādhva, kalādhva), one sees that every atom, every world, every dimension—from the 118 lokas below to the highest śakti realms above—is nothing but the pulsation of one’s own consciousness.</p>
<p>There is no separation between “me” and “the universe.” The vastness is not outside; it is the natural radiance of <em>aham</em>, the “I” of pure subjectivity. This is <em>prakriyā-jñāna</em>: knowledge not as information, but as direct recognition that all is Self. In that seeing, the <em>mahodayaḥ</em>—the Great Awakening—dawns: not as an event in time, but as the irrevocable clarity of the ever-present state of Bhairava.</p>
<p>This is pure śāktopāya: awareness alone, unmediated by breath or form, reveals the universe as the living body of Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 57 turns cosmology into liberation. The “ultimate limit in every direction” (<em>paryanteṣu samantataḥ</em>) is not a boundary to reach, but an invitation to boundlessness. Whether gazing at stars or feeling the earth beneath your feet, you recognize: this is not “my world”—this <em>is</em> me.</p>
<p>The <em>adhva-prakriyā</em> is not a map to study, but a lens to see through. The 118 worlds are symbolic of all possible states of experience—gross, subtle, causal—none of which exist apart from awareness. To “imagine” the universe as your own consciousness is not fantasy, but the undoing of the primal forgetfulness that says “I am small.”</p>
<p>“Great Awakening” (<em>mahodayaḥ</em>) is the collapse of that contraction. It is not enlightenment as a distant peak, but the simple, staggering realization: <em>There is no outside.</em> The garden, the sky, the breath, the thought—all are waves in the ocean of your being.</p>
<p>This is the essence of <em>aham sarvam</em>—“I am all.” Not as egoic inflation, but as humility before the infinite. In that recognition, every step is a dance, every sight a revelation, and the ordinary world—seen without division—becomes the radiant body of Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>58</h1>
<h3>Universe As Transparent Emptiness</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
विश्वमेतन्महादेवि शून्यभूतं विचिन्तयेत्।<br>
तत्रैव च मनो लीनं ततस्तल्लयभाजनम्॥५८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
viśvam etan mahā-devi śūnya-bhūtaṃ vicintayet |<br>
tatraiva ca mano līnaṃ tatas tallaya-bhājanam ||58||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Great Goddess,<br />
this entire universe<br />
is already transparent emptiness—<br />
not as concept, but as your immediate experience.<br />
There, the mind dissolves not by effort,<br />
but by recognition—<br />
and in that dissolution,<br />
you are not "fit to merge" into the supreme Void—<br />
you are the Void itself,<br />
awake to itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>The instruction to "imagine the universe as void" is not to fabricate a new reality, but to recognize what is already the case. The cosmos is śūnya-bhūtam—"empty in essence"—not because it lacks appearance, but because all forms are transparent to the awareness in which they arise. This is not nihilism, but the direct apprehension that phenomena have no independent substance apart from consciousness itself.</p>
<p>The dissolution of mind (mano līnam) in this emptiness is not a process to achieve, but the natural collapse of the subject-object structure when seen through. There is no "becoming fit" to merge with the supreme Void (para-śūnya)—as if one must qualify through practice. Rather, the moment the illusion of separation ends, what remains is recognized as one's true nature: the unbounded awareness in which both universe and void appear as passing expressions.</p>
<p>Though it begins with contemplation (vicintayet), this is not a technique of the individual mind. It is the mind's own intelligence turning back upon itself and recognizing its source. What appears as śāktopāya—the power of discriminative awareness—reveals itself as the spontaneous recognition of non-dual reality.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse does not ask you to make the world disappear—it asks you to see through what was never solid. The universe is not being denied; its true nature is being revealed. When you look at your hand right now, is it truly a solid thing? Or is it a shimmering constellation of sensation, light, and space appearing within awareness? This is not philosophy—it is direct perception.</p>
<p>The "void" is not absence. It is the unconditioned space in which all experience arises—luminous, responsive, and free. Every atom of your body, every star in the sky, every thought in your mind—all are already transparent to this living emptiness. There is no need to "dissolve" the mind into this void, for the mind is itself a ripple in this same emptiness. The apparent dissolution is simply recognition coming home to itself.</p>
<p>The phrase "fit to merge" (<em>tallaya-bhājanam</em>) is not about qualification or attainment. It points to what happens when the illusion of being separate evaporates: you don't "enter" the supreme Void—you recognize you were never outside it. The wave need not become ocean; it need only recognize it was ocean all along. Even the sense of "I am recognizing" dissolves in this seeing, leaving only pure awareness—self-luminous, self-knowing, and free.</p>
<p>Try this now: look at the room around you. See the forms, the colors, the objects—but don't stop there. Look deeper. Feel the space in which they appear. That space is not empty of awareness; it is awareness itself, already whole, already free. There is no distance between you and this recognition. It is not a future attainment but the ever-present ground beneath all appearance.</p>
<p>This is not about creating emptiness through imagination. It is about removing the filters of solidity we impose on experience. When you see a mountain, you are not seeing solid matter—you are witnessing awareness appearing as mountain. When you hear a bird, you are not hearing sound as separate entity—you are witnessing awareness appearing as sound. In this seeing, there is no "you" separate from what is seen. There is only seeing—luminous, boundless, and awake.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not a method to follow but a recognition to allow. You do not become the Void by effort—you are already the Void, recognizing itself through every sensation, every breath, every moment of being. This is not philosophy to understand but reality to inhabit. And in that inhabiting, the universe does not vanish—it shines with the radiance of your true nature.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>59</h1>
<h3>Space Within A Vessel</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
घटादिभाजने दृष्टिं भित्तिस्त्यक्त्वा विनिक्षिपेत्।<br>
तल्लयं तत्क्षणाद्गत्वा तल्लयात्तन्मयो भवेत्॥५९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ghaṭādi-bhājane dṛṣṭiṃ bhittis tyaktvā vinikṣipet |<br>
tal-layaṃ tat-kṣaṇād gatvā tal-layāt tanmayo bhavet ||59||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Fix your gaze upon the space within a jar<br />
or any vessel,<br />
discarding its walls as mere illusion.<br />
Instantly merging into that emptiness,<br />
you become one with it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is a direct, immediate gateway of śāmbhavopāya. Place before you a simple vessel—a pot, a cup, a bowl—and look not at its material form, but at the space it contains. Then, let go of the perception of the container itself (<em>bhittis tyaktvā</em>—“abandoning the walls”). See only the openness, the void, unbounded by shape or substance.</p>
<p>In that unmediated seeing—eyes open, mind ungrasping—the boundary between inner and outer dissolves. There is no “you” looking at “space.” Awareness simply rests as space. And in that instant (<em>tat-kṣaṇāt</em>), one merges (<em>tal-layam</em>) and becomes fully identified with that void (<em>tanmayo bhavet</em>).</p>
<p>This void is not inert. It is the living emptiness of Śiva—pure, self-aware, and all-pervading. The vessel is merely a pointer; the true space is the boundless <em>cid-ākāśa</em>, the ether of consciousness in which the entire universe appears and vanishes.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 59 strips meditation to its essence: seeing without naming. The jar is arbitrary—a stand-in for any form. The instruction is to see <em>through</em> it, not <em>at</em> it. The “walls” (<em>bhitti</em>) symbolize all conceptual boundaries—identity, time, separation. To discard them is not to deny their appearance, but to refuse their authority.</p>
<p>This is not visualization. It is de-visualization. You do not imagine emptiness; you stop overlaying solidity. The moment you cease reinforcing the “clay” of perception, only space remains—and you are that space.</p>
<p>“Instantly merging” (<em>tat-kṣaṇād gatvā</em>) reveals the immediacy of recognition. No preparation, no stages—only the willingness to see what is already true. The void in the pot is the same void in your chest, in the sky, in the silence between thoughts.</p>
<p>To “become one with it” (<em>tanmayo bhavet</em>) is not union of two things, but the end of the illusion that there were two. There is no “I” merging with emptiness. There is only emptiness, awake to itself.</p>
<p>Thus, the humble pot becomes a mirror of the absolute. In its hollow core, the universe is revealed—not as object, but as the open, luminous ground of all that is, was, and will be.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>60</h1>
<h3>Bare, Featureless Expanse</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
निरुर्क्षगिरिभित्त्यादिदेशे दृष्टिं विनिक्षिपेत्।<br>
विलीने मनसे भावे वृत्तिक्षयः प्रजायते॥६०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nir-urkṣa-giri-bhitti-ādi-deśe dṛṣṭiṃ vinikṣipet |<br>
vilīne manase bhāve vṛtti-kṣayaḥ prajāyate ||60||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Cast your gaze upon a place<br />
free of trees, mountains, walls—<br />
open, unmarked, bare.<br />
When the mind settles there,<br />
all mental fluctuations cease.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice uses the outer landscape as a mirror for inner stillness. One goes to a vast, featureless expanse—desert, plain, open sky—where the eye finds no object to cling to. In that visual emptiness, the habitual scattering of the mind loses its anchors. There is nothing to name, nothing to desire or reject.</p>
<p>By simply gazing without fixation (<em>dṛṣṭiṃ vinikṣipet</em>), awareness naturally relaxes. The mind, deprived of its usual food—form, boundary, story—turns inward not by effort, but by exhaustion of outward impulse. In that settling (<em>vilīne manase bhāve</em>), the <em>vṛttis</em>—thought-waves, emotional currents, mental noise—subside of their own accord (<em>vṛtti-kṣayaḥ prajāyate</em>).</p>
<p>This is śāmbhavopāya in its purest form: no mantra, no breath, no visualization—only open-eyed abidance in boundless space. The outer void reflects the inner; and in that reflection, the seer vanishes.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 60 reveals that stillness is not cultivated—it is uncovered. The “bare place” (<em>nir-urkṣa-giri-bhitti-ādi-deśa</em>) is not merely physical; it is any moment in which perception is not hijacked by form. Even in a city, one can find such space—in the gap between buildings, in the sky above rooftops, in the pause between thoughts.</p>
<p>The instruction is not to <em>do</em> anything, but to <em>stop doing</em>. The gaze is soft, not focused on any point. The mind, accustomed to chasing objects, begins to unravel when there is nothing to chase. Like a river entering the ocean, it loses its name and form.</p>
<p>“Thoughts dissolve by themselves” (<em>vṛtti-kṣayaḥ prajāyate</em>) is key. This is not suppression. It is the natural quieting that occurs when attention is no longer fragmented. The desert does not silence the mind—the mind silences itself in the presence of unbounded openness.</p>
<p>This practice is especially potent because it requires no special condition beyond what is already available. In any open space—literal or metaphorical—freedom is revealed not as something to attain, but as what remains when the noise of separation ends.</p>
<p>Thus, the empty field becomes a throne of awareness. And the one who gazes? He is not separate from the space he sees—for in that seeing, subject and object melt into the one, luminous void.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>61</h1>
<h3>The Space Between Dualities</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
उभयोर्भावयोर्ज्ञाने ध्यात्वा मध्यं समाश्रयेत्।<br>
युगपच्च द्वयं त्यक्त्वा मध्ये तत्त्वं प्रकाशते॥६१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ubhayor bhāvayor jñāne dhyātvā madhyaṃ samāśrayet |<br>
yugapac ca dvayaṃ tyaktvā madhye tattvaṃ prakāśate ||61||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>In the awareness of any two states or objects,<br />
rest in the space between them.<br />
Then, letting go of both at once,<br />
the Truth reveals itself in the center.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice works with duality as a threshold to unity. One selects any pair—two objects, inhalation and exhalation, sound and silence, thought and gap—and holds awareness at the point <em>between</em> them (<em>madhyaṃ samāśrayet</em>). This “middle” is not spatial, but the instantaneous gap where one perception ends and the next has not yet begun.</p>
<p>Initially, the two poles serve as supports: seeing object A, then object B, and attending to the fleeting neutrality between. With repetition, this midpoint stabilizes. At that moment, one simultaneously releases both (<em>yugapac ca dvayaṃ tyaktvā</em>)—not by force, but by natural disinterest. In that release, the <em>tattva</em>, the essential Reality, shines forth (<em>prakāśate</em>) not as an object, but as the self-luminous ground that was always present beneath the play of pairs.</p>
<p>Though it begins with concentration on perceptual dyads (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment both poles are abandoned and only the center remains, the method opens into śāmbhavopāya—direct, non-dual recognition.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 61 reveals the secret of all thresholds: the truth is not in the opposites, but in the seam between them. The “two things” can be anything—breath in and out, joy and sorrow, self and world. The practice is not to resolve the tension, but to rest in the pause where tension has not yet arisen.</p>
<p>This “center” (<em>madhya</em>) is not a midpoint in time or space. It is the timeless now—the gap between thoughts, the stillness between heartbeats, the silence between words. In that gap, there is no “this” or “that,” only pure awareness, uncolored by content.</p>
<p>The instruction to “abandon both simultaneously” is pivotal. As long as one pole is subtly held, duality persists. But when both are released—not sequentially, but <em>yugapat</em>, in one motion—awareness snaps back to its source.</p>
<p>This is not intellectual. It is experiential. You do not “find” the center; you stop obscuring it. The moment you cease leaning toward either side, balance reveals itself—not as achievement, but as your natural state.</p>
<p>Thus, every pair in life becomes a doorway. The breath, the glance, the thought—each dyad contains the seed of unity. And in the center, what shines is not new. It is Bhairava, the silent witness behind all coming and going.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>62</h1>
<h3>The Gap Between Perceptions</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
भावे त्यक्ते निरुद्धा चित् नैव भावान्तरं व्रजेत्।<br>
तदा तन्मध्यभावेन विकसत्यतिभावना॥६२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
bhāve tyakte niruddhā cit naiva bhāvāntaraṃ vrajet |<br>
tadā tan-madhya-bhāvena vikasaty ati-bhāvanā ||62||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When awareness releases one object<br />
and does not move to another,<br />
then, abiding in the gap between,<br />
supreme realization unfolds.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse presents two valid approaches, both centered on the <em>madhya</em>—the interval between perceptions.</p>
<p><strong>First interpretation</strong>: Fix attention unwaveringly on a single object—a pot, a flame, a syllable—without allowing the mind to drift to any other (<em>naiva bhāvāntaraṃ vrajet</em>). The key is <em>niruddhā cit</em>: awareness held firm, yet alive, not dull or mechanical. When concentration deepens to the point of identity—<em>tanmaya-bhāva</em>—the boundary between subject and object dissolves. The object’s consciousness merges with the perceiver’s, and both open into universal awareness.</p>
<p><strong>Second (preferred) interpretation</strong>: After fully perceiving an object, release it (<em>bhāve tyakte</em>)—but do not grasp another. Rest in the luminous gap where perception has ceased, yet no new object has arisen. This “between” (<em>tan-madhya-bhāvena</em>) is not empty; it is the fertile void from which all manifestation springs. In that stillness, <em>ati-bhāvanā</em>—supreme realization—blossoms spontaneously.</p>
<p>Both paths belong to śāktopāya: awareness, guided by intention, reveals the truth. But the second is more direct, for it bypasses identification with form and rests in pure subjectivity.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 62 captures the essence of meditative precision. The “gap” is not a void to be filled, but the natural resting place of awareness when freed from compulsive object-seeking.</p>
<p>In the first method, one object becomes a doorway. By attending to it with total presence—without boredom, without anticipation—the object ceases to be “other.” It becomes transparent, revealing the awareness that holds it. This is not absorption <em>into</em> the object, but recognition <em>through</em> it.</p>
<p>The second method is even subtler. It works with the natural rhythm of attention: perceive, release, abide. Most minds leap from one object to the next, never touching the silence between. But here, one pauses—in the moment after a thought ends and before the next begins, after a breath finishes and before the next starts. In that pause, there is no “me,” no “world”—only open, unconditioned presence.</p>
<p>“Supreme realization” (<em>ati-bhāvanā</em>) is not a new state. It is what remains when the habit of grasping ceases. The gap is not empty; it is full of potential—the same potential that births galaxies and thoughts alike.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about concentration as control, but as release. Whether through deep focus on one thing or abiding in the space between things, the goal is the same: to let awareness return to its source. And in that return, what shines is not an experience, but the ever-present ground of all experience.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>63</h1>
<h3>Pure Consciousness</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सर्वं देहं चिन्मयं हि जगद्वा परिभावयेत्।<br>
युगपन्निर्विकल्पेन मनसा परमोदयः॥६३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sarvaṃ dehaṃ cinmayaṃ hi jagad vā paribhāvayet |<br>
yugapan nirvikalpena manasā paramodayaḥ ||63||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Contemplate—whether your whole body or the entire world—<br />
as nothing but Consciousness itself.<br />
With a simultaneous, thought-free mind,<br />
the supreme awakening dawns.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice unifies body and cosmos in one recognition: both are <em>cinmaya</em>—made entirely of Consciousness. One does not imagine this as a mental exercise alone, but holds the conviction—<em>paribhāvayet</em>—that every cell, every atom, every star is a pulsation of Śiva’s luminous awareness.</p>
<p>The key is <em>yugapan nirvikalpena manasā</em>: “with a simultaneous, thought-free mind.” This means the contemplation is not discursive; it is immediate and non-conceptual. There is no sequence of reasoning—“this is Consciousness, therefore that is too”—but a single, unified perception in which the distinction between body, world, and awareness collapses.</p>
<p>Though it involves an initial act of recognition (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the mind abides in this truth without fragmentation, the <em>paramodayaḥ</em>—the supreme dawn of realization—arises not as an event, but as the natural state.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 63 offers the most direct inversion of ordinary perception. Instead of seeing consciousness as in the body, one sees the body as in consciousness—and not just the body, but the entire cosmos. This is not metaphor. It is ontological truth: matter is condensed awareness; space is its openness; time is its rhythm.</p>
<p>"Simultaneously" is crucial. You do not shift from body to world or world to body. You see them as one seamless field—like waves and ocean. The toe is not separate from the mountain; both are expressions of the same living light.</p>
<p>"Thought-free" does not mean blankness. It means perception without the overlay of naming, judging, or separating. In that clarity, the body is no longer a prison of flesh, but a temple of radiance; the world is no longer a field of threat or desire, but a dance of divine energy.</p>
<p>The "supreme awakening" is not a future achievement. It is what happens when you stop seeing form as solid and recognize it as the play of Consciousness. In that seeing, there is no "you" awakening—there is only awakening, happening as you, as the world, as everything.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about changing reality, but about seeing it as it always was.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>64</h1>
<h3>Meeting Of Breaths As Gateway</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
वायुद्वयस्य सङ्घट्टादन्तर्वा बहिरन्ततः।<br>
योगी सामत्वविज्ञानसमुद्गमनभाजनम्॥६४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vāyu-dvayasya saṅghaṭṭād antar vā bahir antataḥ |<br>
yogī sāmatva-vijñāna-samudgamana-bhājanam ||64||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When the two breaths meet—<br />
either within or beyond the body—<br />
the yogi becomes ripe<br />
for the arising of the knowledge of oneness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse points to the pivotal moment in breath-awareness: the <em>saṅghaṭṭa</em>, the “collision” or juncture where exhalation ends and inhalation begins—or vice versa. At that precise threshold, prāṇa and apāna touch, and for a fleeting instant, breath pauses. This suspension may be felt internally—at the heart, between the brows—or externally, in the space twelve finger-widths above the crown (<em>bahirdvādaśānta</em>).</p>
<p>In that natural gap, mental activity subsides, and the dualistic sense of “inner” and “outer” softens. The practitioner, having trained in breath awareness (placing this in āṇavopāya), becomes <em>bhājanam</em>—“fit” or “receptive”—for <em>sāmatva-vijñāna</em>, the direct cognition of equilibrium: the recognition that all apparent opposites arise within one unbroken field of awareness.</p>
<p>Though the method uses breath as support, the insight it yields transcends technique. The “knowledge of harmony” is not intellectual; it is the lived experience of non-duality, where subject and object, self and world, merge in the silent fullness of the present.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 64 reveals that the breath is not merely a physiological process, but a subtle map of consciousness itself. The “meeting of the two breaths” is not a physical event, but a perceptual threshold—the hinge between giving and receiving, expansion and contraction, manifestation and withdrawal.</p>
<p>At that hinge, time seems to pause. In that pause, the mind—no longer carried outward by exhalation or drawn inward by inhalation—settles of its own accord. This is not forced retention (<em>kumbhaka</em>), but natural suspension arising from refined attention.</p>
<p>“Within or outside” (<em>antar vā bahir</em>) indicates that the locus is not fixed. For some, the gap is felt at the heart—the center of emotional and intuitive awareness. For others, it is at the brow—the seat of insight. For still others, it opens outward into the boundless space above the head, where individuality dissolves.</p>
<p>The “knowledge of oneness” (<em>sāmatva-vijñāna</em>) is not a belief, but a shift in identity. You no longer experience yourself as a separate observer watching breath; you <em>are</em> the space in which breath arises and vanishes. Duality collapses not through effort, but through recognition in the gap.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is a microcosm of the entire path: in the smallest pause between breaths, the whole of reality is revealed—not as something to attain, but as what has always been present, waiting only for attention to cease its wandering and rest in the centerless center.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>65</h1>
<h3>Recognizing Innate Bliss</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सर्वं जगत् स्वदेहं वा स्वानन्दभरितं स्मरेत्।<br>
युगपत् स्वामृतेनैव परानन्दमयो भवेत्॥६५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sarvaṃ jagat sva-dehaṃ vā svānanda-bharitaṃ smaret |<br>
yugapat svāmṛtenaiva parānanda-mayo bhavet ||65||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Contemplate the entire world or your own body<br />
as already overflowing with your innate bliss.<br />
In that very instant,<br />
drenched in your own nectar,<br />
you become one with supreme joy.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice is a direct invocation of <em>ānanda</em>—the bliss that is not emotional pleasure, but the essential nature of Consciousness itself. One does not generate bliss; one remembers (<em>smaret</em>) that it already fills every atom of the body and every corner of the universe. The word <em>bharitam</em>—“overflowing”—emphasizes that this bliss is not partial or conditional, but complete and uncontainable.</p>
<p>Crucially, this is not a sequential visualization. The instruction <em>yugapat</em>—“simultaneously”—means the recognition must be immediate and all-encompassing: no “first the body, then the world,” but a single, unified perception of totality saturated with joy.</p>
<p>Through this remembrance, one is immersed in <em>svāmṛta</em>—one’s own nectar, the self-secret elixir of awareness. In that immersion, the practitioner does not “attain” <em>parānanda</em>; he <em>becomes</em> it (<em>parānanda-mayo bhavet</em>), for bliss is not an experience added to the self—it is the self, revealed.</p>
<p>This belongs to śāktopāya: the power of recognition (<em>smṛti</em>) alone awakens what has never been absent.</p>
<p>Verse 65 completes the triad begun in verses 63 and 64:<br />
- Verse 63: the world is <em>cinmaya</em>—made of Consciousness (prakāśa, luminosity)<br />
- Verse 64: the world reveals <em>sāmatva</em>—the knowledge of oneness and harmony where all dualities merge<br />
- Verse 65: the world is <em>ānandamaya</em>—made of Bliss (vimarśa, self-aware joy)</p>
<p>Together, they affirm the non-dual nature of reality in its three inseparable dimensions: awareness as luminosity, awareness as unity, and awareness as bliss. These aren't sequential stages but simultaneous aspects of one reality—like light, warmth, and radiance from a single flame.</p>
<p>Verse 64's insight is essential to this triad: consciousness (verse 63) doesn't remain abstract light but naturally reveals itself as unbroken wholeness in the "gap" between all apparent opposites. It's in this unity that bliss (verse 65) isn't emotional reaction but the inherent fullness of being itself.</p>
<p>"Already filled" is the key. You do not pour bliss into the world; you remove the veil that made you think it was empty. Ordinary perception sees lack—"I need this to be happy." The yogi's perception, having recognized both consciousness and unity, sees abundance—"This is already whole."</p>
<p>The "nectar" (<em>amṛta</em>) is not a substance, but the taste of freedom—the sweet certainty that nothing is missing. When you rest in that taste, the boundary between "my bliss" and "supreme bliss" vanishes. There is only one joy, vibrating as galaxies and heartbeats alike.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not aspiration, but affirmation. In one flash of simultaneous recognition—holding consciousness, unity and bliss together—the world is transfigured—not because it changed, but because you remembered: you are the bliss that fills it. And in that remembering, Bhairava shines—not as other, but as your own unbroken heart.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>66</h1>
<h3>Bliss Arising Through Playful Ruse</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
कुहनेन प्रयोगेण सद्य एव मृगेक्षणे।<br>
समुदेति महानन्दो येन तत्त्वं प्रकाशते॥६६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kuhanena prayogeṇa sadya eva mṛgekṣaṇe |<br>
samudeti mahānando yena tattvaṃ prakāśate ||66||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O gazelle-eyed one,<br />
by a playful ruse—<br />
sudden, boundless bliss arises,<br />
and through that, Reality reveals itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse introduces an unconventional, spontaneous method: <em>kuhana</em>—a “trick” or playfully startling touch, such as tickling under the armpits, which triggers uncontrolled laughter. In that moment of spontaneous outburst, the usual mental constructs collapse. The laughter is not mere reflex; it is an eruption of unconditioned joy, free from personal narrative or emotional residue.</p>
<p>The key is not the act itself, but the immediate inquiry: <em>Where does this laughter arise from?</em> In the split second before the mind labels it “mine” or “funny,” there is pure, unmediated delight—a flash of <em>mahānanda</em>, the great bliss that is the very texture of Śiva’s being.</p>
<p>Because this bliss arises without effort, mantra, or breath control—and because it is accessed through direct observation of a natural, bodily-emotive event—it belongs to śāktopāya. The practitioner does not create the experience; they recognize it as the spontaneous expression of their true nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 66 is a masterstroke of wisdom: liberation is not confined to silence, stillness, or solemnity. It can erupt in laughter—in the unguarded, involuntary burst that shatters the armor of self-seriousness.</p>
<p>The “trick” (<em>kuhana</em>) is not manipulation; it is a skillful disruption of habitual identity. Tickling bypasses the thinking mind and triggers a raw, pre-conceptual response. In that gap—between sensation and interpretation—the sense of “I” vanishes, and what remains is pure, bubbling joy.</p>
<p>Most dismiss such laughter as trivial. But the text asks: <em>Why do you laugh uncontrollably, even when you’re suffering?</em> Because beneath sorrow, beneath control, there is a wellspring of joy that cannot be extinguished. That joy is not psychological—it is ontological. It is the vibration of consciousness delighting in itself.</p>
<p>“Reality reveals itself” (<em>tattvaṃ prakāśate</em>) not as a vision, but as the ground of that laughter. When you trace it to its source—not the nerves under the arm, but the space from which the impulse to laugh springs—you touch the heart of Bhairava: playful, fearless, and utterly free.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about tickling—it is about readiness. In any moment of spontaneous release—laughter, awe, tears—the door opens. The only task is to not close it with thought. To rest, just for an instant, in the naked delight before the mind says “me.” And in that instant, all is revealed.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>67</h1>
<h3>Withdrawing Through Upward Breath</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सर्वस्रोतोनिबन्धेन प्राणशक्त्यूर्ध्वया शनैः।<br>
पिपीलिकास्पर्शवेलायां प्रथते परमं सुखम्॥६७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sarva-srotō-nibandhena prāṇa-śakty ūrdhvayā śanaiḥ |<br>
pipīlikā-sparśa-velāyāṃ prathate paramaṃ sukham ||67||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When all senses naturally withdraw<br />
through the gentle current of breath<br />
flowing upward,<br />
in the moment the subtle sensation<br />
of ants crawling arises—<br />
there, supreme bliss reveals itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This meditation is not about forcing the senses to close, but allowing attention to gather in the central axis of awareness. The "upward-flowing prāṇa-śakti" is not a physical energy moving in the spine, but the natural orientation of awareness turning toward its source.</p>
<p>As this inner gathering occurs, the outward pull of the senses—sound, touch, sight, taste, and smell—naturally subsides, not through suppression but through the magnetism of presence. In this collected state, a delicate tingling (pipīlikā-sparśa) may arise—not as a sought-after experience, but as the somatic expression of awareness dissolving its identification with gross form.</p>
<p>At that precise moment (velāyām), when sensation is felt without the overlay of "mine," supreme bliss (paramaṃ sukham) reveals itself not as an emotional pleasure, but as the intrinsic joy of consciousness recognizing its own unbounded nature. Though it begins with attention to breath and body (āṇavopāya), at the moment of recognition it transcends method and becomes pure śāmbhavopāya—the natural abidance in what has always been present.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Don't think about controlling your senses—feel the space in which this text appears right now. That space is not an object; it is awareness itself, already whole, already free. This verse simply points to what is already happening when attention becomes total.</p>
<p>The "restraining of sensory currents" (<em>sarva-srotō-nibandhena</em>) is not a practice to master. It is what naturally occurs when you stop chasing experience and simply rest as the awareness that knows it. The eyes may remain open, the world still present—but your attention no longer leaks outward in grasping. This is not withdrawal from life; it is deeper participation in it.</p>
<p>The "upward movement" of breath is not literal. It is the inner orientation toward source—the same way a flame naturally rises not by effort, but by its nature. When awareness is undivided, the body becomes transparent to this movement. That tingling sensation ("ants crawling") is not proof of spiritual advancement; it is the body's natural response to unbroken presence.</p>
<p>Most importantly, "supreme bliss" is not a state to achieve. It is the quiet satisfaction of being what you are. Not the pleasure of sensation, but the recognition that sensation—however subtle—arises within you, the unchanging ground of all experience. The bliss is not in the tingling; it is in the space that holds the tingling without being touched by it.</p>
<p>This is not about producing special experiences. It is about meeting ordinary experience with total presence. When you feel the breeze on your skin, don't label it "pleasant" or "unpleasant"—let awareness rest in the sensation itself, before interpretation. In that resting, the boundary between "feeler" and "felt" dissolves, and what remains is not a state to cultivate, but the ever-present ground of being.</p>
<p>The path is not "up the spine" but "into the heart of this moment." The sensation of crawling ants is not a signpost to follow, but a mirror reflecting your own nature—luminous, responsive, and free. In that mirror, there is no path, no practitioner, no goal. Only the simple, unadorned fact: awareness is, and in its recognition, bliss is not found. It is remembered.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>68</h1>
<h3>The Space Between Fire And Poison</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
वह्निविषस्य मध्ये तु चित्तं सुखमयं क्षिपेत्।<br>
केवलं वायुपूर्णं वा स्मरानन्देन युज्यते॥६८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vahni-viṣasya madhye tu cittaṃ sukha-mayaṃ kṣipet |<br>
kevalaṃ vāyu-pūrṇaṃ vā smar-ānandena yujyate ||68||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Place the mind—overflowing with bliss—<br />
in the space between fire and poison.<br />
Or simply fill awareness entirely with breath.<br />
In either case, merge with the eternal joy of union.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dhāraṇā reveals the non-dual heart of all apparent opposites. "Fire" (vahni) symbolizes icchā—the upward impulse of creation, will, desire; "poison" (viṣa) represents jñāna—the downward movement of dissolution, discernment, stillness. The practitioner rests in the interval between them—not in the surge of wanting nor in the resolution of knowing, but in the silent threshold where both arise.</p>
<p>This middle ground (madhye) is not spatial but experiential: the pre-conceptual moment before perception crystallizes into thought. When mind abides here, suffused with inherent joy (sukha-mayaṃ), it recognizes its own nature as the space in which all duality plays.</p>
<p>Alternatively, one may simply saturate awareness with the natural flow of breath (vāyu-pūrṇaṃ)—not through force, but through total presence to its unbroken rhythm. In this fullness, all separation dissolves.</p>
<p>Whether through the symbolic middle or through breath's continuity, the result is the same: union with smar-ānanda—the bliss that recognizes itself. This is not an emotional state but the self-recognition of consciousness as it is: whole, unbroken, and eternally fulfilled.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Don't think about fire and poison—feel the space between any two opposites right now. The pause between thoughts, the stillness between heartbeats, the openness between inhale and exhale—this is the living reality of madhye.<br />
The text offers two entry points, not as techniques to master, but as mirrors reflecting the same truth. The "space between fire and poison" is the threshold where the mind surrenders its addiction to resolution. In ordinary life, we rush to name experience: "This is joy," "That is sorrow." But in the in-between, before labeling, reality shines naked and unowned.</p>
<p>The alternative path—filling awareness with breath—is not about controlling inhalation or exhalation. It's about recognizing that every breath is already whole. When you stop measuring breath as "mine," it reveals itself as the pulsation of the universe itself—unbroken, effortless, complete.</p>
<p>"Smar-ānanda" is often translated as "the bliss of recollection," but this misses its essence. It is not memory of past joy, but the ever-present joy of awareness remembering itself. In the gap between opposites, in the fullness of breath, there is no separate "you" experiencing bliss. There is only bliss—conscious, alive, free.</p>
<p>This is not sexuality, nor is it asceticism. It is the recognition that all life—whether in the heat of passion or the coolness of stillness—is already saturated with the one taste. The body may tremble, the mind may dance, but the space that holds them remains untouched, radiant, whole.</p>
<p>Try this now: feel the space between your ribs as you read these words. Don't manipulate your breath—simply sense the openness there. That space is not empty; it is full of awareness. That awareness is not yours; it is you. In that recognition, fire and poison are seen as one dance, and what remains is not an experience to attain, but the ground of all experience—already free, already whole.</p>
<p>There is no moving toward union, but stopping the movement of fragmentation. In that stop, the world is not denied—the marketplace, the kitchen, the bedchamber all become altars. But the offering is not objects; it is the surrender of the illusion that you were ever apart from what you seek.</p>
<p>And in that surrender, Bhairava is not found beyond fire or poison, beyond breath or stillness. He is the unbroken space in which all opposites arise, play, and dissolve—recognizing itself as the eternal joy that needs nothing added, nothing removed.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>69-70</h1>
<h3>Union Bliss As Pedagogical Mirror</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
शक्तिसङ्गमसङ्क्षुब्धा शक्त्यावेशवसानिकम्।<br>
यत्सुखं ब्रह्मतत्त्वस्य तत्सुखं स्वाक्यमुच्यते॥६९॥<br>
लेहनामन्थनाकोशैः स्त्रीसुखस्य भरत्स्मृतेः।<br>
शक्त्यभावेऽपि देवेशि भवेदानन्दसम्प्लवः॥७०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
śakti-saṅgama-saṅkṣubdhā śaktyāveśa-vasānikam |<br>
yat sukhaṃ brahma-tattvasya tat sukhaṃ svākyam ucyate ||69||<br>
lehanāmanthanākośaiḥ strī-sukhasya bharat-smṛteḥ |<br>
śakty-abhāve ’pi deveśi bhaved ānanda-samplavaḥ ||70||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The bliss that arises<br />
when Śakti is stirred in union<br />
and absorption in her is complete—<br />
that joy is the very essence of Brahman,<br />
known as the bliss of the Self.</p>
<p>O Goddess, even without a consort,<br />
by intensely recalling<br />
the joy of kissing, embracing, or caressing,<br />
a flood of supreme delight arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses do not prescribe practice. They are vivid analogies—pedagogical mirrors, not methods. The sexual union (śakti-saṅgama) is used not as a spiritual technique, but as the closest human experience to the non-dual ecstasy of Śiva-Śakti merging. At the peak of mutual absorption—when personal boundaries dissolve in shared rapture—one tastes a fleeting reflection of cidānanda, the self-luminous joy of pure being.</p>
<p>Similarly, verse 70 illustrates that even the intense recollection (bharat-smṛteḥ) of such intimacy—without physical enactment—can trigger a surge of inner bliss (ānanda-samplavaḥ). This is not fantasy, but the power of remembrance (smaraṇa) to awaken the same energy that, in realization, flows unimpeded.</p>
<p>However, this is not a meditation technique in the technical sense. Abhinavagupta and other authorities cite it as metaphor, not method. The Vijñāna Bhairava itself clarifies that such references are illustrative, not instructional. Their purpose is pedagogical: to point the seeker toward the depth, immediacy, and totality of bliss by invoking the most potent experience available in ordinary life.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verses 69–70 occupy a delicate threshold. They acknowledge that the human body—especially in moments of total surrender—can mirror the divine play of unity. But they do not elevate sexuality to a path. Instead, they use its intensity as a mirror: <em>This—this total loss of separation, this unguarded fullness—is a glimpse of what you truly are.</em></p>
<p>The key is <em>intensity without objectification</em>. In ordinary desire, pleasure is sought as a possession. In the metaphor of these verses, the “bliss” arises precisely when possession ends—when lovers cease to be two and become one field of sensation. That momentary collapse of duality is the echo of recognition.</p>
<p>Similarly, “recollection” (<em>smṛti</em>) is not nostalgic indulgence. It is a focused invocation of the <em>feeling-state</em> of unity—so complete that the mind forgets the image and rests in the resonance. In that resonance, the distinction between “remembering” and “being” softens.</p>
<p>Yet the text is careful: this is simile, not sadhana. The true “union” is not between bodies, but between awareness and itself. The real “kissing” is the touch of subject and object collapsing into one taste. The authentic “embrace” is the holding of all experience in the open arms of non-dual awareness.</p>
<p>Thus, these verses are not licenses, but lenses. They do not invite you to chase ecstasy, but to recognize that the deepest joy you have ever known—even in fleeting human moments—was never separate from the eternal bliss that is your own nature.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>71</h1>
<h3>Merging Into Spontaneous Joy</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
आनन्दे महति प्राप्ते दृष्टे वा बन्धुवे चिरात्।<br>
आनन्दमुद्गतं ध्यात्वा तल्लयस्तन्मना भवेत्॥७१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ānande mahati prāpte dṛṣṭe vā bandhave cirāt |<br>
ānandam udgataṃ dhyātvā tal-layas tan-manā bhavet ||71||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When great joy arises—<br />
whether in profound delight<br />
or upon seeing a beloved after long separation—<br />
meditate on the very upsurge of that bliss;<br />
merging into it, the mind becomes one with it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is the actual <em>dharāṇā</em> relating to the prior verses. It does not prescribe sexual union, but utilizes any intense moment of spontaneous joy—whether from intimacy, reunion, or sudden wonder—as a gateway to realization.</p>
<p>At the instant of overwhelming delight (<em>ānande mahati prāpte</em>), before the mind labels, claims, or narrates the experience, there is a pure upsurge (<em>ānandam udgatam</em>)—a flash of unconditioned bliss that is none other than the self-radiance of consciousness. The instruction is to <em>dhyātvā</em>—attend directly to that upsurge itself, not to its cause.</p>
<p>By resting in the source of the joy—not the friend, not the sensation, but the naked arising of bliss—the practitioner dissolves into it (<em>tal-layaḥ</em>) and becomes fully absorbed (<em>tan-manā bhavet</em>). This is not emotional indulgence; it is recognition: the bliss was never “yours,” but the intrinsic nature of the Self.</p>
<p>Because it relies on awareness attending to an inner event without mantra or breath, this is pure śāktopāya. The emotion is not rejected; it is seen through to its luminous core.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 71 transforms ordinary human joy into a path of awakening. The key is timing: the moment joy <em>arises</em>, before “I am happy” solidifies into identity. In that gap—between the surge and the story—lies the door.</p>
<p>This applies universally. The embrace of a long-lost friend, the climax of passion, the awe at a sunset—all contain, for an instant, the unfiltered pulse of <em>ānanda</em>. Most miss it, rushing to possess or interpret the feeling. But here, one pauses—not to analyze, but to <em>dwell in the rising itself</em>.</p>
<p>“Merging into it” (<em>tal-layaḥ</em>) means the boundary between “me” and “joy” collapses. You do not <em>have</em> bliss; you <em>are</em> bliss—temporarily unobscured. That “you” is not the ego, but awareness prior to separation.</p>
<p>This is not about cultivating pleasure, but about recognizing that all joy—however fleeting—is a ripple from the ocean. The beloved, the body, the moment—they are not the source. The source is the silent awareness in which both longing and fulfillment appear.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is radical inclusion: nothing is rejected, not even desire. Instead, every peak of human feeling becomes an invitation to dive beyond the wave into the depth from which it rose—and discover that you were the ocean all along.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>72</h1>
<h3>Abiding In Fullness</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
जग्धि-पान-कृतोल्लासरसानन्दविजृम्भणात्।<br>
भावयेद्भरितावस्थां महानन्दस्ततो भवेत्॥७२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
jagdhi-pāna-kṛtollāsa-rasānanda-vijṛmbhaṇāt |<br>
bhāvayed bharitāvasthāṃ mahānandaḥ tato bhavet ||72||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When joy swells<br />
from the delight of eating or drinking,<br />
abide in the state of fullness—<br />
from that, supreme bliss arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana transforms sensory pleasure into spiritual insight. One partakes of food or drink—especially what is deeply enjoyed—and at the very moment when pleasure blooms (rasānanda-vijṛmbhaṇāt), one does not cling to the taste or the act, but rests in the feeling of fullness (bharitāvasthā) that arises with it.</p>
<p>This fullness is not physical satiety, but the expansive, uncontracted state of awareness that underlies all genuine enjoyment. The practitioner holds the conviction—not "I am enjoying," but "I am fullness itself." In that recognition, ordinary pleasure becomes a portal to mahānanda, the great bliss that is the intrinsic nature of consciousness.</p>
<p>Because it uses a sensory experience as support (jagdhi-pāna), this belongs to śāktopāya. The bliss is not rejected as worldly; it is seen as a localized expression of universal joy. The support is temporary; the recognition is eternal—what appears as enjoyment is actually the mirror reflecting your own boundless nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 72 sanctifies the ordinary. Eating and drinking—daily acts often dismissed as mundane—are revealed as opportunities for awakening. The key is not what you consume, but how you meet the experience.</p>
<p>When flavor floods the senses, the mind usually narrows into craving or judgment: "This is good—I want more," or "This is gone—I miss it." But here, one pauses in the peak of delight, before desire or memory intervenes. In that pause, the joy is not "mine"; it is pure, unowned radiance.</p>
<p>"Abide in the state of fullness" means to stop identifying as a separate consumer and instead recognize: This joy is not coming from the food—it is my own nature, temporarily unveiled. The sweetness of honey, the richness of wine, the warmth of broth—all are mirrors reflecting the inherent fullness of awareness.</p>
<p>This is not hedonism, but deconstruction through delight. By meeting pleasure with total presence, its power to bind dissolves. The taste remains, but the hunger for it as a source of happiness vanishes. What emerges is not satisfaction of desire, but the end of seeking itself.</p>
<p>Thus, the kitchen becomes a temple, the meal an offering, and the act of tasting a direct recognition: every flavor is a ripple in the ocean of bliss.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>73</h1>
<h3>Music Dissolving Mind</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
गीतादिविषयास्वादसमसौख्यैकतात्मनः।<br>
योगिनस्तन्मयत्वेन मनोरुद्धेस्तदात्मता॥७३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
gītādi-viṣayāsvāda-sama-saukhyāikatātmanaḥ |<br>
yoginas tanmayatvena manoruddhes tad-ātmatā ||73||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When the yogi’s mind becomes one<br />
with the incomparable joy of music and such aesthetic delights,<br />
and is fully absorbed in that essence,<br />
he realizes identity with it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana turns aesthetic experience into a gateway to non-dual realization. The yogi listens not to individual notes or rhythms, but to the <em>collective resonance</em> (<em>sama-saukhya</em>) that underlies all sound—the unified vibration (<em>nāda</em>) that flows through the seven svaras as one luminous stream. In that listening, the mind does not chase succession; it merges into the singular current of beauty.</p>
<p>When absorption is total (<em>manoruddheḥ</em>), and the listener becomes <em>tanmayatvena</em>—“of that very essence”—the boundary between subject and object dissolves. The yogi no longer hears music; he <em>is</em> the music, not as personal emotion, but as the self-radiant joy of consciousness itself. This is <em>tad-ātmatā</em>: identity with the Self, revealed through aesthetic rapture.</p>
<p>Because it uses sensory beauty as a support, this is śāktopāya. Yet for the mature practitioner, even ordinary conversation, poetry, or the sound of wind can trigger this recognition—provided the mind is refined enough to perceive the unity beneath multiplicity.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 73 reveals that art is not diversion, but revelation. The “collective sound” is not the sum of notes, but the silent harmony that holds them—the same harmony that holds galaxies, breaths, and thoughts. To hear it is to hear the pulse of reality itself.</p>
<p>Most listen fragmentarily: “This note is sharp,” “That phrase is sad.” But the yogi listens with the whole being, perceiving the <em>one taste</em> beneath all variation. In that perception, the sense of “me listening” vanishes, and only listening remains—vast, unowned, and luminous.</p>
<p>Abhinavagupta’s warning is precise: those who cannot feel this unity remain <em>jada</em>—not dead, but spiritually inert—not because they lack intelligence, but because they lack the openness to be dissolved by beauty. For them, sound is noise; for the yogi, even discord reveals the underlying silence.</p>
<p>This is not reserved for concert halls. The “music” may be birdsong, rain, a lover’s voice, or the hum of traffic. What matters is not the source, but the depth of listening. When attention is total, every sound becomes a mantra, every moment a rite of recognition.</p>
<p>Thus, aesthetic joy is not escape—it is homecoming. In the heart of beauty, the yogi finds not pleasure, but the Self.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>74</h1>
<h3>Mind Finds Natural Contentment</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यत्र यत्र मनस्तुष्टिर्मनस्तत्रैव धारयेत्।<br>
तत्र तत्र परानन्दस्वरूपं सम्प्रवर्तते॥७४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yatra yatra manas-tuṣṭir manas tatraiva dhārayet |<br>
tatra tatra parānanda-svarūpaṃ sampravartate ||74||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Wherever the mind finds contentment,<br />
let it rest there.<br />
In that very place,<br />
the nature of supreme bliss reveals itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is the essence: Natural, effortless realization. The practitioner does not seek a “holy” place or a “spiritual” experience. Instead, wherever the mind settles spontaneously in peace—whether in the garden, at work, in silence, or amid sound—one simply abides there (<em>manas tatraiva dhārayet</em>).</p>
<p>There is no hierarchy of activities. The act that brings stillness <em>is</em> the sacred act, regardless of convention. To force attention toward a “pure” object while the mind is at ease elsewhere is not devotion—it is resistance. True worship is alignment with what already calms the mind.</p>
<p>In that alignment, without effort or projection, <em>parānanda-svarūpaṃ</em>—the very form of supreme bliss—arises not as a new state, but as the natural radiance of awareness freed from seeking. As Abhinavagupta affirms: whatever delights the mind becomes a vessel for Brahman.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya at its most inclusive: the world itself, in its ordinary fullness, is the path.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 74 dismantles the sacred-secular divide. Peace is not found by escaping life, but by meeting it exactly as it is. If the mind is calm while washing dishes, that sink is the altar. If stillness arises while walking in rain, that street is the temple. The location is irrelevant; what matters is the quality of presence.</p>
<p>“Let it rest there” (<em>dhārayet</em>) is not passive. It is active surrender—refusing to chase a “better” state, and instead honoring the grace already present. This requires humility: to accept that enlightenment might wear gardening gloves or typing fingers, not just prayer beads.</p>
<p>The phrase “supreme bliss reveals itself” (<em>sampravartate</em>) is key. It does not come <em>because</em> of the activity, but <em>through</em> the non-resistance to it. When the mind stops rejecting the present, the veil thins—and what shines through is not joy <em>from</em> the garden, but joy <em>as</em> the ground of the garden, the gardener, and the act itself.</p>
<p>Thus, this teaching is not permission for distraction, but an invitation to intimacy with what is. In the ordinary moment—fully embraced—lies the extraordinary truth: that bliss is not elsewhere. It is here, now, wherever the mind is at rest. And in that rest, Bhairava is not sought. He is recognized.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>75</h1>
<h3>Between Waking And Sleep</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अनागतायां निद्रायां प्राणे स्ते बह्यगोचरे।<br>
सावस्था मनसा गम्या परा देवी प्रकाशते॥७५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
anāgatāyāṃ nidrāyāṃ prāṇe ste bahya-gocare |<br>
sāvasthā manasā gamyā parā devī prakāśate ||75||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When sleep has not yet come,<br />
and outer awareness has ceased—<br />
that state, approached by the mind,<br />
reveals the supreme Goddess.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana points to the threshold between waking and sleep—<em>anāgatā nidrā</em>, “sleep not yet arrived”—the liminal space where external perception has withdrawn (<em>bahya-gocare</em>), yet full unconsciousness has not set in. The breath is naturally suspended (<em>prāṇe ste</em>), and the mind, unanchored from sense objects, hovers in pure subjectivity.</p>
<p>To “approach this state with the mind” (<em>manasā gamyā</em>) is not to visualize or concentrate, but to rest in luminous vigilance as the world recedes. In that vigilance—free of drowsiness, free of imagery—<em>parā devī</em>, the supreme power of awareness, reveals herself not as an object, but as the self-shining ground of being.</p>
<p>This is pure śāmbhavopāya: no support, no technique, no object. It is the natural abidance in <em>turīya</em>, the fourth state, which underlies waking, dreaming, and deep sleep. Here, the body may appear asleep to others, but consciousness remains awake to itself—unmoved, unbroken, and free.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 75 unveils one of the most accessible yet profound gateways to realization: the gap before sleep. Most slip through it unknowingly, lost in fatigue or anticipation of dreams. But the yogi meets it with wide-open awareness.</p>
<p>In that interval—after the last thought of the day, before the first dream image—there is no “me,” no world, no time. Only pure, unmediated presence. This is not unconsciousness; it is hyper-consciousness, unclouded by content. The breath often pauses spontaneously, not by force, but because the outward impulse of attention has ceased.</p>
<p>“Supreme Goddess reveals herself” means the dynamic face of the absolute—Śakti as pure knowing—shines forth not as a vision, but as the silent certainty: <em>I am</em>. There is no body, no senses, no memory—yet awareness remains, undiminished.</p>
<p>This state is not rare. It occurs nightly. The only difference is recognition. When you learn to abide there—not chasing it, not fearing it—you discover that true rest is not oblivion, but awakening. The body may lie still, but consciousness stands in its fullness: Bhairava, ever awake, ever free.</p>
<p>Thus, the threshold of sleep becomes the doorway to eternity—not by escaping life, but by meeting the moment between worlds with total presence.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>76</h1>
<h3>Radiant Space As Mirror</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
तेजसा सूर्यदीपादेराकाशे सवलीकृते।<br>
दृष्टिर्निवेश्या तत्रैव स्वात्मरूपं प्रकाशते॥७६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
tejasā sūrya-dīpāder ākāśe savalī-kṛte |<br>
dṛṣṭir niveśyā tatraiva svātma-rūpaṃ prakāśate ||76||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Fix your gaze on space<br />
filled with the variegated radiance<br />
of sun or lamp.<br />
There itself,<br />
your true nature shines forth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice uses luminous space as a mirror for awareness. One gazes steadily—not at the physical source (the sun or bulb), but at the light-infused atmosphere itself (<em>ākāśe savalī-kṛte</em>). The enclosure, the fixture, the form—these are ignored. Only the pervading glow is held in attention.</p>
<p>After sustained gazing, one may close the eyes and rest in the afterimage—not as memory, but as the inner counterpart of outer radiance. In that fusion of inner and outer light, the mind, unanchored from form, dissolves into pure luminosity.</p>
<p>In that dissolution, <em>svātma-rūpam</em>—the essential form of the Self—reveals itself not as an object seen, but as the self-luminous ground that sees. Though it uses light as a support (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment identification with the light ends and only awareness remains, the practice opens into śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 76 works with light not as physics, but as metaphor for consciousness. The sun or lamp is incidental; what matters is the space <em>filled</em> with light—boundless, inclusive, impartial. This is the quality of awareness itself: it does not reject darkness, but reveals everything without being stained by it.</p>
<p>The instruction to “ignore the glass” is symbolic: see through form to the radiance that animates it. Whether gazing at sunlight through leaves or lamplight on a wall, the point is to let the eyes soften, the mind empty, and attention become transparent.</p>
<p>Closing the eyes afterward is not withdrawal, but integration. The outer light becomes the inner light—not as visualization, but as the natural glow of presence. In that glow, thoughts may arise, but they are seen as shadows passing through light, not as truths.</p>
<p>“Your true nature shines forth” means the Self is not discovered as new, but recognized as what has always been illuminating experience. You are not the one who sees the light—you are the light by which seeing occurs.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about staring at brightness, but about becoming attuned to the ever-present clarity that is Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>77</h1>
<h3>Fullness Through The Five Mudras</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
करङ्किण्या क्रोधनाया भैरव्या लेलिहाया च।<br>
खेचर्यां दृष्टिकाले च परावाप्तिः प्रकाशते॥७७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
karaṅkiṇyā krodhanāyā bhairavyā lelihāyā ca |<br>
khecarīṃ dṛṣṭi-kāle ca parā-vāptiḥ prakāśate ||77||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>With steady gaze in Karaṅkiṇī,<br />
in Krodhanā, Bhairavī, Lelihā—<br />
and in the sky-traveling Khecarī,<br />
supreme fullness shines forth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These five mudrās represent five paths to realization, each expressing non-dual awareness through a specific bodily posture.</p>
<p>Karaṅkiṇī-mudrā: For the jñāna-siddha, realized through direct insight. The body lies utterly still like a corpse, free of all movement. This is the death of self-contraction, the stillness of pure awareness.</p>
<p>Krodhanā-mudrā: For the mantra-siddha, realized through repetition. The face is fierce—eyes wide, mouth open, teeth bared—yet free of emotional turmoil. All residual energy of practice dissolves into unwavering stillness.</p>
<p>Bhairavī-mudrā: For the melāpa-siddha, realized in the threshold between sleep and waking. Eyes remain unblinkingly open; breath suspends in the pause between in and out. This is the fusion of steadfast awareness and awestruck openness—where external perception has ceased but full unconsciousness has not begun.</p>
<p>Lelihā-mudrā: For the śākta-siddha, realized through immersion in bliss. There is subtle inner tremor—not from sensation's pleasure, but from the overflow of fullness itself.</p>
<p>Khecarī-mudrā: For the śāmbhava-siddha, established in thought-free awareness. This is not merely a tongue lock, but abiding in the boundless sky of consciousness while fully engaged in life's activities—speaking, eating, working—untouched yet thoroughly present, like an actor who knows the play is not real.</p>
<p>When any of these mudrās is held with unwavering gaze (dṛṣṭi-kāle), the sense of separation dissolves. The supreme pervasion (parā-vāptiḥ) does not arise as an achievement but is recognized as what has always been. Though all five converge on the same truth, only Khecarī fully embodies śāmbhavopāya—effortless abidance in pure awareness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>True mastery appears not in special postures, but in how we inhabit ordinary moments. The five gestures described aren't postures to perfect, but natural expressions of awareness itself—unfolding whenever we cease struggling against experience.</p>
<p>Karaṅkiṇī's stillness isn't corpse-like rigidity—it's what your body recognizes when thought subsides. Krodhanā's fierce expression isn't anger—it's the clarity that arises when you cease editing your experience. Bhairavī's unwavering gaze isn't strain—it's the natural openness that sees both thought and the space between thoughts. Lelihā's subtle tremor isn't excitement—it's the recognition that joy exists prior to objects. Khecarī's sky-traveling isn't a physical gesture—it's the ordinary miracle of awareness present in every act, untouched yet fully engaged.</p>
<p>These states reveal what's already true: awareness has no fixed address. When we cease struggling against experience—whether in stillness, fierce presence, open-eyed receptivity, tasting life's sweetness with full attention, or moving through activities with inner spaciousness—we discover that consciousness itself is already complete.</p>
<p>Try this now: let your eyes rest softly on these words. Don't force stillness or intensity. Simply be present—unmodified, undivided. In that simple presence, without adopting any special posture, what shines is already full. This completeness isn't found by adding special techniques, but by removing the veil of separation we impose between ourselves and the living moment.</p>
<p>The ultimate gesture is the one that leaves no trace—where action flows without a sense of someone acting, where awareness recognizes itself in every gesture of existence. There can be no moving toward realization, only the ceasing of movement away from what's already here. When attention is total—whether in stillness or movement—what emerges isn't a new state, but what remains when the fiction of separation falls away. These five doorways aren't about transforming yourself. They are about recognizing that you were never bound.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>78</h1>
<h3>Unsupported Sitting</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
मृद्वासने स्फिजैकेन हस्तपादौ निराश्रयम्।<br>
निधाय तत्प्रसङ्गेन परा पूर्णा मतिर्भवेत्॥७८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
mṛdv-āsane sphijaikena hasta-pādau nirāśrayam |<br>
nidhāya tat-prasaṅgena parā pūrṇā matir bhavet ||78||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Sit gently on a soft cushion,<br />
balanced on the sitting bones alone.<br />
Place hands and feet without support.<br />
In this simplicity,<br />
supreme fullness of awareness arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dhāraṇā reveals how physical alignment becomes direct recognition. The yogi sits on a moderately soft seat—not too firm, not too yielding—resting only on the sitting bones (<em>sphijaikena</em>). The hands and feet remain unsupported (<em>nirāśrayam</em>), neither touching floor nor knee, suspended in natural equipoise.</p>
<p>This is not about perfect posture but about releasing subtle dependencies. When no part of the body leans on anything external, the habitual spatial reference "I am here" dissolves. Without these physical anchors, awareness naturally settles into its unbounded nature (<em>parā pūrṇā matiḥ</em>).</p>
<p>Though it begins with bodily arrangement (āṇavopāya), the moment attention stabilizes in this unsupported presence, the method dissolves into pure recognition (śāmbhavopāya). The body is not a vessel to contain awareness—it becomes transparent to the awareness that animates it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse reveals how ordinary sitting becomes sacred when freed from subtle dependencies. Don't think about perfecting your posture—feel the space in which your body appears right now.</p>
<p>The soft cushion is not luxury but wisdom: enough comfort to prevent distraction, not enough to induce dullness. The "sitting bones" instruction is not anatomical precision but an invitation to find your body's natural axis—the vertical alignment where weight and lightness meet.</p>
<p>Most crucial is the unsupported hands and feet. This is not theatrical gesture, but the dissolution of the body's habitual conversation with the world. When your hand doesn't rest on your knee, when your foot doesn't press against the floor, the subtle dialogue of "me here, world there" ceases. In that cessation, awareness is no longer pulled outward through the limbs.</p>
<p>What emerges is not emptiness but fullness. Not the emotional plenitude of happiness, but the uncontracted radiance of awareness itself—unbounded because it no longer believes it needs support. This fullness isn't found in the posture; it's recognized when the posture reveals the space in which both body and support arise.</p>
<p>Try this now as you read: let your hands rest freely in your lap, palms open, without leaning them against anything. Feel your feet on the floor but don't push down. Sense the sitting bones bearing your weight. Notice how the mind becomes less divided—not because you've achieved a special state, but because you've stopped reinforcing the subtle supports that fragment attention. The cushion is not a tool to use but a threshold to cross. When you sit without bracing, without leaning, without subtle contractions of support—what remains is not a body in space, but space appearing as body.</p>
