<p>In that recognition, liberation is not something to attain by sitting correctly. It is the natural condition of being when you stop holding on—not just physically, but in the very architecture of your identity. The hands and feet are free not because they float in air, but because they are seen as expressions of the same awareness that knows them.</p>
<p>Thus, this simplest of instructions—"sit with limbs unsupported"—becomes a mirror reflecting the truth: you were never dependent on anything. The throne of awareness requires no pillars. In the very act of releasing the last support, you discover you were already seated on the unshakable ground of being—Bhairava, not beyond the body, but as the luminous space within which body and world appear as one seamless reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>79</h1>
<h3>Space Beneath Armpits</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
उपविश्यासने सम्यक् बाहू कृत्वर्धकुञ्चितौ।<br>
कक्षव्योम्नि मनः कुर्वन् शाममायाति तल्लयात्॥७९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
upaviśyāsane samyak bāhū kṛtvārdha-kuñcitau |<br>
kakṣa-vyomni manaḥ kurvan śāmam āyāti tal-layāt ||79||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Sit fully balanced on a seat,<br />
arms gently bent halfway.<br />
Rest the mind in the space beneath the armpits—<br />
not as anatomical location,<br />
but as boundless openness.<br />
There, merging with what has never departed,<br />
peace arises—not as achievement,<br />
but as the natural state.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dhāraṇā reveals how ordinary posture becomes direct recognition. The seated position (<em>samyak āsane</em>) is neither rigid nor lax—utterly ordinary, yet completely present. The arms are held in <em>ardha-kuñcitau</em>—half-bent, not forced, creating subtle openness beneath the armpits (<em>kakṣa</em>).</p>
<p>This is not anatomical focus. The <em>kakṣa-vyoman</em>—the void beneath the arms—is not a cavity in the body, but the transparent space in which the body itself appears. When the mind is placed (<em>manaḥ kurvan</em>) there—not through concentration, but through total relaxation of effort—it discovers this space is identical to the boundless sky of awareness (<em>cid-ākāśa</em>).</p>
<p>In that recognition (<em>tal-layāt</em>), <em>śāmam</em>—peace—arises not as a state to be attained, but as the inherent stillness of consciousness freed from the subtle contraction of "I am here." Though the posture belongs to āṇavopāya, the moment awareness rests in the space itself, the method dissolves into śāktopāya. And in that dissolution, what remains is not emptiness, but fullness: Bhairava, abiding in the world while untouched by it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Don't think about the armpits—feel the space beneath your own arms right now. That space is not empty; it is full of awareness. This verse reveals that liberation hides in plain sight, in the most ordinary places we never notice.</p>
<p>The "half-bent arms" (<em>ardha-kuñcitau</em>) create neither tension nor collapse. They simply allow what is already there to be seen: the openness that has never been confined by flesh or bone. This is not a technique to master; it is a mirror held up to the space that is always already present.</p>
<p>When you truly rest in the space beneath your arms—not as object, but as the open field in which sensation appears—the boundary between inner and outer dissolves. That space is not "yours." It is the same space that holds galaxies and thoughts, breath and stillness. The armpit is merely a doorway; the destination is the boundless awareness that was never located anywhere.</p>
<p>"Peace arises" (<em>śāmam āyāti</em>) not because disturbance is suppressed, but because the one who needed peace has dissolved. What remains is not a tranquil state to maintain, but the vibrant stillness that underlies all movement—the unbroken wholeness that is Bhairava.</p>
<p>This is not about bodily posture. It is about recognizing that every "gap" in experience—between breaths, between thoughts, between sensations—is the same opening through which awareness recognizes itself. The armpit is not special; it is ordinary. And in that ordinariness lies the extraordinary: the ever-present ground where the separate self forgets itself, and what remains is not emptiness as absence, but fullness without boundary.</p>
<p>Try this now: sit as you are, let your arms rest naturally, and feel the space beneath them. Don't manipulate your posture. Don't seek a special sensation. Simply rest as the awareness that knows this space. In that resting, there is no "you" finding peace. There is only peace—awake to itself.   The arms, the seat, the room—all remain as they are. But the one who sat down has vanished into the space that holds everything.</p>
<p>That space is not beneath your arms. It is what you are. And in that recognition, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the unbroken openness in which all practice—and all life—naturally dissolves.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>80</h1>
<h3>Unblinking Gaze</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
स्थूलरूपस्य भावस्य स्थब्धां दृष्टिं निपात्य च।<br>
अचिरेण निराधारं मनः कृत्वा शिवं व्रजेत्॥८०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sthūla-rūpasya bhāvasya stabdhāṃ dṛṣṭiṃ nidadhāti ca |<br>
acireṇa nirādhāraṃ manaḥ kṛtvā śivaṃ vrajet ||80||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Fix the gaze, unwavering and unblinking,<br />
on a vivid external form—<br />
and, in that very moment,<br />
let the mind become supportless;<br />
thus, swiftly, one enters Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses the immediate impact of beauty as a lightning path to realization. One sits before a tangible, vividly present form—a richly adorned vase, a blooming flower, or the radiant presence of a young child—and fixes the eyes on it without blinking (stabdhāṃ dṛṣṭim). The object must be real—not imagined or painted—and must emanate presence, not mere appearance.</p>
<p>The key is not in analyzing the object, but in surrendering to the bhāva—the living essence of beauty itself. In that unbroken gaze, thought ceases (nirādhāraṃ manaḥ), not through suppression, but through total captivation. The mind, no longer fragmented, becomes a mirror reflecting pure awareness.</p>
<p>In that instant of non-dual perception—where seer, seen, and seeing collapse—one enters the state of supreme consciousness, not as a destination, but as the ground already revealed. Though it begins with an external support, the moment beauty is perceived not as form but as luminous presence, the method becomes pure śāmbhavopāya: direct, instantaneous, and objectless.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Liberation can flash forth through the door of beauty. The instruction is precise—sthūla-rūpasya bhāvasya: "the essence of a gross form." Not the form itself, but the aliveness that makes it shine. A child's face, a lotus in full bloom, a crafted vessel—these are not objects to desire, but mirrors of the divine play.</p>
<p>"Unblinking gaze" is not physical rigidity, but total absorption—eyes open, mind empty. In that seeing, there is no "I am looking at beauty." There is only beauty, seen without separation. The moment the mind stops labeling—"this is a girl," "this is a vase"—and simply rests in the radiance, the door opens.</p>
<p>"Supportless mind" means no foothold in thought, memory, or identity. The world is not denied; it is seen so completely that the one who sees vanishes. In that vanishing, what remains is not void, but fullness: the same fullness that makes a flower unfurl or a child laugh.</p>
<p>The story of Swami Mehtab Kak illustrates this perfectly. He did not admire the girl; he was dissolved by the presence she carried. Tea was served, but he was already home—not in trance, but in recognition.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about gazing at objects, but about learning to see without the filter of self. In the heart of beauty, the world does not disappear—it reveals itself as the unbroken openness in which all experience arises.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>81</h1>
<h3>Tongue Centered With Soundless "Ha"</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
मध्यजिह्वे स्फारितास्ये मध्ये निक्षिप्य चेतनम्।<br>
होच्चारं मनसा कुर्वन्ततः शान्ते प्रलीयते॥८१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
madhya-jihve sphāritāsye madhye nikṣipya cetanam |<br>
hokāraṃ manasā kurvan tataḥ śānte pralīyate ||81||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>With the mouth gently opened,<br />
place the tongue centered in its space,<br />
fix awareness there,<br />
and mentally attend to the soundless “ha” of out-breath.<br />
In that, one dissolves into peace.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice belongs to āṇavopāya and uses the subtle articulation of the breath-sound “ha” as a focal point. The mouth is opened just enough internally—lips relaxed, jaw unhurried—so the oral cavity becomes a resonant chamber. The tongue rests at the center, neither touching palate nor teeth. Into this still space, the practitioner mentally utters “ha,” the exhalation sound that mirrors the natural release of breath.</p>
<p>This “ha” is not part of the <em>so’ham</em> mantra in this context. While “so’ham” pairs inhalation (“sa”) and exhalation (“ha”), here only the exhalatory “ha” is used—stripped of its inhaling counterpart—to symbolize complete dissolution into the universal. As awareness merges with this silent utterance, the mind, no longer scattered, settles into <em>śānta</em>, the appeased state of Śiva. Though it begins with breath and sound (āṇavopāya), its fulfillment is śāmbhavopāya: the spontaneous vanishing of the meditator into pure stillness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 81 turns the mouth into a microcosm of the cosmos. The “wide-open mouth” (<em>sphāritāsye</em>) is not a grimace, but an inner expansion—a soft opening that creates space without effort. The centered tongue becomes an axis, and the silent “ha” the vibration that dissolves the boundary between inside and out.</p>
<p>“Ha” is not merely a syllable; it is the sound of letting go. In ordinary life, we hold—opinions, breath, identity. Here, in the sanctuary of the mouth, one releases without force. The mental intoning is key: no audible sound, just the subtle inner resonance that accompanies the natural outflow of prāṇa.</p>
<p>“Dissolves into peace” (<em>śānte pralīyate</em>) does not mean unconsciousness. It is the quiet collapse of the sense of “I am doing.” The mouth remains, the breath flows, but the one who claimed them as “mine” has vanished. What remains is not emptiness, but the fullness of awareness resting in its own nature.</p>
<p>This practice is especially potent because it uses the body’s own architecture as a vessel of revelation. In the simple act of opening the mouth and intoning “ha,” the yogi enacts the great return: all movement, all identity, flowing back into the silent source from which they arose. And in that return, Bhairava is not found elsewhere—he is recognized as the peace that was always already here.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>82</h1>
<h3>Utterly Supportless</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
आसने शयने स्थित्वा निराधारं विभावयन्।<br>
स्वदेहं मनसि क्षीणे क्षणात्क्षीणदशायो भवेत्॥८२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
āsane śayane sthitvā nirādhāraṃ vibhāvayan |<br>
sva-dehaṃ manasi kṣīṇe kṣaṇāt kṣīṇa-daśāyo bhavet ||82||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Whether seated or lying down,<br />
contemplate the body as utterly supportless.<br />
When the mind dissolves into stillness,<br />
in that very instant, one enters the state beyond all mental conditioning.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice begins in śāktopāya and culminates in śāmbhavopāya. The yogi—whether sitting on a seat or reclining on a bed—holds the unwavering perception that the body rests on nothing (<em>nirādhāraṃ</em>). Not on earth, not on fabric, not on breath or thought—nothing supports it. This is not denial of physical contact, but the inner recognition that all apparent support is illusory.</p>
<p>As this contemplation deepens, the mind, deprived of its usual anchors, naturally thins and dissolves (<em>manasi kṣīṇe</em>). In that dissolution—<em>kṣaṇāt</em>, in a single moment—the practitioner enters <em>kṣīṇa-daśā</em>, the state beyond mental dispositions, where the sense of individuality vanishes and only pure, unconditioned awareness remains.</p>
<p>Though it starts with intentional focus (<em>vibhāvayan</em>), the moment the mind surrenders its grip, the method transcends technique. The body may remain in posture, but consciousness abides in the boundless: Śiva, ever free.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 82 reveals liberation in stillness, whether upright or at rest. The instruction “supportless” (<em>nirādhāraṃ</em>) is radical: it asks you to see through the very notion of ground. The chair, the bed, the earth beneath—they are all appearances within awareness, not foundations of your being.</p>
<p>This is not nihilism. It is freedom. When you stop believing the body needs something to hold it up, the contraction of “I am here” softens. The body becomes weightless, not physically, but existentially—floating in the open sky of awareness.</p>
<p>“Mind dissolves” (<em>manasi kṣīṇe</em>) does not mean unconsciousness. It is the end of the mind’s tyranny—the cessation of its constant narration, its need to locate, define, and control. In that quiet, what remains is not absence, but full presence.</p>
<p>“Instant liberation” (<em>kṣaṇāt</em>) is not hyperbole. When the last prop is relinquished—when even the subtle belief in “I am supported” drops—awakening is immediate. Not because something new arrives, but because the veil of separation is seen through.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice meets you exactly as you are—sitting, lying, awake, tired—and asks only one thing: <em>Can you rest without holding on?</em> In that letting go, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the space itself—in which body, bed, and breath all appear.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>83</h1>
<h3>Stillness Amid Motion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
चलासने स्थितस्याथ सनैर्वा देहचलनात्।<br>
प्रशान्ते मनसे भावे देवि दिव्यौघमाप्नुयात्॥८३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
calāsane sthitasyātha sanair vā deha-calanāt |<br>
praśānte manase bhāve devi divyaugham āpnuyāt ||83||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Whether seated on a moving seat<br />
or gently swaying the body,<br />
when the mind settles into stillness,<br />
O Goddess, one attains the divine flood—<br />
the boundless sky of consciousness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals motion itself as a path to stillness. One may ride upon a moving seat (<em>calāsana</em>)—a horse, a cart, or any conveyance whose rhythm gently rocks the body—or, without such support, slowly sway the torso from side to side. The oscillation is not erratic, but smooth and unhurried (<em>sanaiḥ</em>).</p>
<p>In this gentle movement, the key is not the motion itself, but the awareness that rests <em>between</em> the movements—between the rise and fall, the left and right. The mind, instead of following the swing, abides in the neutral axis where motion originates and dissolves. In that center, agitation subsides (<em>praśānte manase bhāve</em>), and the practitioner is flooded (<em>divyaugham</em>) with <em>cid-ākāśa</em>, the luminous expanse of pure awareness.</p>
<p>Since no mantra, breath control, or visualization is involved—only the natural rhythm of movement and the alertness to its midpoint—this belongs to pure śāktopāya. The support is transient; the recognition is eternal.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice dismantles the false boundary between movement and stillness. The "moving seat" (calāsane) is not a contrived method but the recognition that all life is already in motion—walking, breathing, heartbeat—the perpetual dance of existence. When the text speaks of the body "gently swaying" (sanair vā deha-calanāt), it does not prescribe a technique, but invites attention to rhythm already present in ordinary life.</p>
<p>Crucially, stillness is not achieved through motion, nor does it arise between movements. Stillness is the ever-present ground in which motion appears—like the depth of the ocean remaining unmoved while surface waves rise and fall. The mind does not "settle into stillness" (praśānte manase) as if stillness were a destination; it recognizes itself as stillness amid all agitation.</p>
<p>The phrase "divine flood" (divyaugham) is not a state to be attained, but the natural revelation of what has never been obscured. When the subtle division between "me" and "my experience" collapses—not through effort, but through the simple absence of grasping—the luminous expanse of awareness (cid-ākāśa) is revealed not as something new, but as what has always been present beneath the surface of apparent change.</p>
<p>This is not about mastering movement to reach stillness. It is about seeing that stillness was never absent—even in the midst of motion, even in the marketplace, even in the heart of chaos. The rocking cart, the swaying body, the rhythm of breath—none of these create stillness; they reveal what has always been whole. What appears as "settling of the mind" is simply the end of the illusion that awareness was ever fragmented.</p>
<p>The divine flood is not a reward for practice. It is the recognition that you were never separate from the ocean itself—even when you appeared as a wave.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>84</h1>
<h3>Cloudless Sky Revealing</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
आकाशं विमलं पश्यन् कृत्वा दृष्टिं निरन्तरम्।<br>
स्थब्धात्मा तत्क्षणाद्देवि भैरवं वपुराप्नुयात्॥८४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ākāśaṃ vimalaṃ paśyan kṛtvā dṛṣṭiṃ nirantaram |<br>
stabdhātmā tat-kṣaṇād devi bhairavaṃ vapur āpnuyāt ||84||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Gazing steadily at the clear sky—<br />
unbroken, unwavering—<br />
with body utterly still,<br />
in that very instant, O Goddess,<br />
one becomes Bhairava himself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is pure śāmbhavopāya—immediate, objectless, and effortless. One looks into a cloudless sky (<em>vimalam ākāśam</em>), not as an external object, but as the mirror of one’s own nature. The gaze is held without blinking or distraction (<em>nirantaram</em>), and the body is frozen in absolute stillness (<em>stabdhātmā</em>—literally, “the self made motionless”).</p>
<p>There is no mantra, no breath control, no conceptualization—only open-eyed abidance in boundless clarity. In that unmediated seeing, the distinction between inner and outer collapses. The sky is not “out there”; it is the very space of awareness in which both observer and observed arise.</p>
<p>At that precise moment (<em>tat-kṣaṇāt</em>), the practitioner does not “attain” Bhairava; he recognizes himself as Bhairava. The “form of Bhairava” (<em>bhairavaṃ vapuḥ</em>) is not a vision—it is the self-luminous reality that shines when all contraction ceases.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 84 is the essence of the practice: realization through naked perception. The clear sky is not chosen for its beauty, but for its emptiness—its refusal to hold any form, yet its capacity to reveal all forms. It is the perfect symbol of awareness: vast, unmarked, and self-evident.</p>
<p>“Unbroken gaze” (<em>nirantaram dṛṣṭim</em>) means total presence—no drifting into thought, no labeling “blue,” no remembering past skies. Just seeing, without a seer. “Body utterly still” (<em>stabdhātmā</em>) is not rigidity, but the natural settling that occurs when attention is not fragmented.</p>
<p>“In that very instant” (<em>tat-kṣaṇāt</em>) is the key. There is no journey, no accumulation, no waiting. The moment perception is free from the overlay of “me” and “mine,” the sky is no longer separate—and what remains is not a person looking at sky, but sky-awareness, awake to itself.</p>
<p>This is not metaphor. It is direct seeing. When you look into the cloudless expanse and stop reinforcing the boundary between “here” and “there,” the mind’s grip loosens—and in that release, you are not <em>in</em> space. You <em>are</em> space. And that space is Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>85</h1>
<h3>Sky Dissolved Into The Crown</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
लीनं मूर्ध्नि व्यात्सर्वं भैरवत्वेन भावयेत्।<br>
तत्सर्वं भैरवाकारं तेजस्तत्त्वं समाविशेत्॥८५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
līnaṃ mūrdhni vyāt-sarvaṃ bhairavatvena bhāvayet |<br>
tat sarvaṃ bhairavākāraṃ tejas-tattvaṃ samāviśet ||85||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Contemplate the entire sky—<br />
dissolved into the crown of your head—<br />
as the very essence of Bhairava.<br />
Then, all becomes Bhairava’s form,<br />
and one enters the radiant truth of His being.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses imaginative expansion to dissolve the boundary between inner and outer. One visualizes the entire sky (<em>vyāt-sarvaṃ</em>) as drawn into the cranial vault (<em>mūrdhni līnam</em>)—not as physical containment, but as the recognition that the boundless space above is identical to the space within. The skull is no longer a finite container; it becomes as vast as the cosmos itself.</p>
<p>Holding this vision, one <em>bhāvayet</em>—firmly regards it—as <em>bhairavatvena</em>, the nature of Bhairava: luminous, unbounded, and self-aware. In this unified perception, all experience—inner and outer—shines as <em>bhairavākāra</em>, the form of Bhairava. The practitioner then <em>samāviśet</em>, enters fully into <em>tejas-tattva</em>, the radiant essence of that reality.</p>
<p>Because it begins with a dual framework—the sky outside and the space within—and requires intentional visualization (<em>vikalpa</em>), this belongs to śāktopāya. It is a preparatory merging, not the spontaneous immediacy of śāmbhavopāya. Yet it serves as a powerful bridge: by expanding inner space to meet outer infinity, the sense of separation dissolves, preparing the ground for direct recognition.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice employs spatial expansion as a threshold between dualistic visualization and non-dual recognition. The invitation to draw the entire sky into the cranial vault serves not as an end, but as a strategic dismantling of perceived boundaries. The skull becomes a symbolic vessel—not containing the infinite, but revealing its illusion of containment.</p>
<p>Lakshmanjoo carefully places this method within śāktopāya, where awareness uses internal imagery as a temporary scaffold. The practitioner doesn't create unity between inner and outer space—they temporarily relax the contraction that maintains their separation. This momentary relaxation creates a crack in the architecture of duality through which spontaneous recognition may flash.</p>
<p>Crucially, the "radiant truth" (tejas-tattva) isn't entered as destination but glimpsed as the ever-present ground when conceptual barriers momentarily dissolve. The visualization of sky dissolving into crown isn't cultivation of experience but momentary suspension of the habit that divides experience.</p>
<p>When the commentary describes this as "preparatory merging," it doesn't imply linear progress toward a distant goal. Rather, it acknowledges that for minds still bound by spatial reference points, the collapse of "inside" and "outside" requires a transitional gesture—a final release of the handhold before freefall into immediacy. The sky isn't brought into the head; the illusion that they were ever separate evaporates.</p>
<p>This practice is the mind's last graceful bow before the heart's spontaneous uprising—honoring the journey while recognizing that what appears as path was always home. What remains when all visualization falls away isn't attainment, but the recognition that the sky was never outside, the head was never a container, and Bhairava was never other than this unbroken wholeness.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>86-87</h1>
<h3>Bhairava In All States</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
विश्वादि भैरवं रूपं ज्ञात्वानन्तप्रकाशबृत्।<br>
ब्रह्मलोकस्ततः पुनरुन्मीलनं समाविशेत्॥८६॥<br>
एवमेव दुर्निशायां कृष्णपक्षागमे चिरम्।<br>
तैमिरं भावयन्न्रूपं भैरवं वपुरेष्यति॥८७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
viśvādi bhairavaṃ rūpaṃ jñātvānantaprakāśabṛt |<br>
brahmalokas tataḥ punar unmīlanaṃ samāviśet ||86||<br>
evameva durniśāyāṃ kṛṣṇa-pakṣāgame ciram |<br>
taimiraṃ bhāvayann rūpaṃ bhairavaṃ vapur eṣyati ||87||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Having known the form of Bhairava<br />
in the states of waking, dreaming, and deep sleep,<br />
one enters the infinite light,<br />
then merges directly into open-eyed absorption.<br />
So too, on a dark night<br />
at the start of the moonless fortnight,<br />
meditating long on the form of darkness,<br />
one attains the form of Bhairava.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p><strong>Verse 86</strong>: This practice moves through the three ordinary states—waking (<em>viśva</em>), dreaming (<em>taijas</em>), and deep sleep (<em>prajñā</em>)—recognizing each as Bhairava’s expression. Waking is <em>viśva</em>, the realm of differentiation; dreaming is <em>tejas</em>, luminous with inner light; deep sleep is <em>prajñā</em>, concentrated wisdom. By seeing all three as inseparable from one’s own consciousness, the practitioner enters <em>ananta-prakāśa</em>, the infinite radiance beyond limitation. From there, one passes through <em>brahmaloka</em>—the internal world of divine consciousness—not as a final stop, but as a threshold to <em>unmīlana samādhi</em>, where eyes are open and the world is embraced as Bhairava’s body. Though it begins as āṇavopāya (sequencing through states), it ripens into śāmbhavopāya, the natural abidance in totality.</p>
<p><strong>Verse 87</strong>: On a moonless, rain-lashed night (<em>kṛṣṇa-pakṣa</em>), with all lights extinguished, one gazes into the absolute darkness (<em>taimiraṃ rūpam</em>). The steady drumming of rain silences external noise, deepening inward focus. Here, darkness is not absence, but the form of Bhairava himself—unmanifest, all-pervading. By holding this perception without fear or conceptualization, the boundary between inner and outer dissolves. In that dissolution, one <em>eṣyati</em>—attains—not a state, but the very essence of Bhairava. This is śāktopāya, for it uses darkness as a support, yet leads to the supportless.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p><strong>Verse 86</strong> dismantles the hierarchy of states. Waking is not superior to sleep; all are movements within one awareness. To "know Bhairava in viśva, taijas, prajñā" means seeing the same luminosity in the rustle of leaves, the flicker of a dream, and the depth of dreamless rest. This knowing is not intellectual—it is the collapse of the seeker who believed enlightenment lay beyond ordinary life.</p>
<p><em>Brahmaloka</em> is not a celestial realm, but the shift in perception where the world is recognized as divine play. It is a "pathway" only for the mind still clinging to stages. True realization leaps beyond it into <em>unmīlana</em>—eyes open, heart unguarded, dancing in the marketplace without losing the still center. The cart, train, and jet analogy clarifies the upāyas: all reach the same destination, but the depth of transformation differs. Āṇavopāya’s methodical path builds unshakable foundation; śāmbhavopāya’s immediacy risks bypassing integration. Both are grace.</p>
<p><strong>Verse 87</strong> sanctifies darkness. Modernity fears the unlit, yet here, darkness is the womb of awakening. The rain’s rhythm is not distraction—it is the pulse of the absolute, dissolving the edges of self. "Form of darkness" (<em>taimiraṃ rūpam</em>) is precise: darkness as formless form, like space holding galaxies. To "meditate long" is not duration, but depth—until the one who fears darkness realizes he <em>is</em> the darkness that holds fear.</p>
<p>Bhairava is not found by adding light, but by seeing through the illusion of separation in total blackness. The rain-washed night becomes a mirror: just as clouds veil the moon yet cannot touch its light, thoughts and forms arise within awareness but cannot stain it. In that recognition, the yogi does not "attain" Bhairava—he recognizes he was never other than the unbroken stillness beneath all storm.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>87-88</h1>
<h3>Darkness As The Form Of Bhairava</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
एवमेव दुर्निशायां कृष्णपक्षागमे चिरम्।<br>
तैमिरं भावयन्न्रूपं भैरवं वपुरेष्यति॥८७॥<br>
एवमेव निमील्यादौ नेत्रे कृष्णाभमग्रतः।<br>
प्रसार्य भैरवं रूपं भावयंस्तन्मयो भवेत्॥८८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
evameva durniśāyāṃ kṛṣṇa-pakṣāgame ciram |<br>
taimiraṃ bhāvayann rūpaṃ bhairavaṃ vapur eṣyati ||87||<br>
evameva nimīlyādau netre kṛṣṇābham agrataḥ |<br>
prasārya bhairavaṃ rūpaṃ bhāvayaṃs tanmayo bhavet ||88||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>So too, on a moonless night<br />
at the dark fortnight’s height,<br />
meditating long on the form of darkness,<br />
one attains Bhairava’s very body.</p>
<p>So too,<br />
first close the eyes gently<br />
and hold the darkness before you.<br />
Then, opening them,<br />
extend that dark form of Bhairava outward—<br />
and, abiding in it, become one with it.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 87 is śāktopāya: it uses the <em>impression</em> of darkness—dense, moonless, amplified by rain’s rhythm—as a support. The practitioner perceives this darkness as Bhairava’s form (<em>taimiraṃ rūpam</em>), a tangible entry point. But if the darkness were <em>absolute</em>—no sound, no sensation, only pure void with no object to perceive—it would be śāmbhavopāya. Śāmbhavopāya requires no support; it is awareness resting in its own soundless, objectless nature. Here, the rain, the night, the act of "meditating on darkness"—all are supports, placing this in śāktopāya.</p>
<p>Verse 88 deepens this by unifying inner and outer darkness. Close the eyes softly—no pressure, no phosphenes—only the blackness of <em>kṛṣṇābham</em> before you. Hold this as Bhairava’s presence. Then, <em>abruptly</em> open the eyes into dimness. The critical test is not whether forms appear, but whether the sense of separation between inner and outer arises. If, in that instant, awareness remains undivided—whether forms are seen or not—then the dark form of Bhairava is recognized as the seamless ground of all perception. In this non-dual continuity, you <em>bhāvayaṃs tanmayo bhavet</em>: dwell and become one with Bhairava. This too is śāktopāya, for it relies on the sequence of closing, stabilizing, opening, and recognizing. Yet its fruit—non-dual perception—echoes śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>The distinction between verses 87 and 88 is subtle but profound. Verse 87 works with <em>external</em> darkness as a mirror; verse 88 collapses the mirror itself. The critical test in 88 is perceptual purity: if opening your eyes reveals even a shadow, the practice is incomplete. True success is when the darkness inside the eyelids and the darkness outside are indistinguishable—not as visual sameness, but as the same boundless ground.</p>
<p>The teaching that "śāmbhavopāya is soundless, supportless void" clarifies the hierarchy:<br />
- <strong>Śāktopāya</strong> (87–88): Uses darkness as a <em>symbol</em> of the absolute. The rain’s sound in 87, the act of closing/opening eyes in 88—these are skillful means.<br />
- <strong>Śāmbhavopāya</strong>: Would be sitting in absolute silence and void, where no "darkness" is perceived—only awareness, prior to all objects, even the object "darkness."</p>
<p>Yet both paths converge. In verse 88, when the practitioner no longer distinguishes inner from outer darkness, the support vanishes <em>within the practice itself</em>. The "dark form of Bhairava" is no longer a form—it is the open space in which form and formlessness arise.</p>
<p>The yogi does not reject the world of rain, night, or closed eyes, but seeing through them to the luminous void that is Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>89</h1>
<h3>Self Shining After Sensory Impact</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यस्य कस्येन्द्रियस्यापि व्याघाताच्च निरोधतः।<br>
प्रविष्टस्याद्वये शून्ये तत्रैवात्मा प्रकाशते॥८९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yasya kasyendriyasyāpi vyāghātāc ca nirodhataḥ |<br>
praviṣṭasyādvaye śūnye tatraivātmā prakāśate ||89||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When any sense organ<br />
is struck by impact or self-restrained,<br />
and one enters the non-dual void—<br />
there, the Self shines forth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses sudden sensory interruption as a lightning path to realization. Whether an organ is struck accidentally (<em>vyāghātāt</em>)—a toe hitting a stone, an elbow knocking wood—or deliberately restrained (<em>nirodhataḥ</em>)—clenching the jaw, pressing the eyes—one does not dwell on the pain or sound, but on the <em>first instant</em> of sensation, before the mind labels it “pain,” “impact,” or “me.”</p>
<p>In that raw, pre-conceptual moment—<em>ālocana</em>, the bare perception—thought halts. The shock creates a gap where subject and object collapse into pure awareness. In that gap (<em>advaye śūnye</em>, the non-dual void), the Self (<em>ātmā</em>) reveals itself not as an object, but as the luminous ground of the sensation itself.</p>
<p>Since it uses a physical event as support—however fleeting—this belongs to śāktopāya. The shock is not the goal; it is the crack through which awareness sees itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 89 transforms accident into awakening. We spend life avoiding pain, yet here, the <em>first flash</em> of impact becomes a doorway. The key is precision: not the lingering ache, but the initial spark—when nerve meets resistance and awareness flares before interpretation.</p>
<p>This is not masochism. It is radical presence. In the split second before “I am hurt” arises, there is only pure sensation—vivid, unowned, and free. That instant is <em>advaya śūnya</em>: non-dual emptiness, not as absence, but as the open space where all experience arises.</p>
<p>“Self shines forth” means the sense of separation dissolves. The toe, the stone, the sensation—they are not “mine.” They are ripples in the ocean of awareness, momentarily taking form. To rest in that ripple’s origin is to touch the ocean itself.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice meets life as it is—clumsy, abrupt, embodied—and reveals that even a stubbed toe can be a guru. The world constantly offers these cracks; most ignore them. The yogi leans in, and in that lean, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the space between impact and reaction.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>90</h1>
<h3>Silent Intoning Of Primal "A"</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अबिन्दुमविसर्गं चाकारं जपतो महान्।<br>
उदेति देवि सहसा ज्ञानौघः परमेश्वरः॥९०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
abindum avisargaṃ cākāraṃ japato mahān |<br>
udeti devi sahasā jñānaughaḥ parameśvaraḥ ||90||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>If one silently intones the primal “a”—<br />
free of bindu and visarga—<br />
O Goddess, in that very instant<br />
the Supreme Lord,<br />
a flood of wisdom,<br />
arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana centers on the pure, unadorned vowel “a”—the first and most fundamental of all sounds, the seed of manifestation. One does not utter “am” (with bindu, the dot signifying nasalization) or “aḥ” (with visarga, the colon-like release), but the naked “a” alone—short, abrupt, and unsupported.</p>
<p>This is not verbal repetition, but a sudden, internal release of soundless vibration. In that instant, the mouth opens spontaneously into <em>cakita-mudrā</em>—the gesture of awe: jaw dropped, eyes wide, breath suspended. This is not theatrical; it is the body’s natural response to the shock of pure presence.</p>
<p>In that gesture, duality collapses. The “flood of wisdom” (<em>jñānaughaḥ</em>)—Paramaśiva himself—arises not from effort, but from the gap created by the unqualified “a.” Though it begins with a trace of āṇavopāya (the intentional utterance), it swiftly becomes śāktopāya: awareness alone, ignited by the primal sound.</p>
<p>Spontaneous <em>cakita-mudrā</em>—as when one sees a lion and is struck with real fear—is śāmbhavopāya. But here, the mudrā is evoked through the sound “a,” making it śāktopāya. Both converge on the same truth: the moment the mind stops, Bhairava appears.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 90 distills mantra to its essence. The letter “a” is not a phoneme; it is the pulse of origination—the first breath of existence before division. To intone it “without bindu or visarga” is to strip away all elaboration, leaving only the raw potential from which all language, all form, emerges.</p>
<p>The resulting <em>cakita-mudrā</em>—mouth open in astonishment—is not performed; it <em>happens</em>. Like the gasp at the edge of a canyon or the pause when lightning splits the sky, it is the body’s surrender to immediacy. In that surrender, the thinker vanishes, and what remains is naked awareness: vast, unfiltered, and free.</p>
<p>“Flood of wisdom” (<em>jñānaughaḥ</em>) is not knowledge <em>about</em> reality, but reality knowing itself. It is the lion not as threat, but as pure presence—so real it stops the mind. The text’s example is precise: when you truly see the lion, there is no “me” to be afraid. Only seeing. And in that seeing, the lion walks away—not because you are fearless, but because there is no one left for it to threaten.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about sound, but about silence <em>through</em> sound. The “a” is the spark that burns away the veil of separation. And in the ashes, Bhairava stands—not as other, but as the open space where sound, silence, and seeing are one.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>91</h1>
<h3>The End Of Visarga Sound</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
वर्णस्य सविसर्गस्य विसर्गान्तं चितिं कुरु।<br>
निराधारेण चित्तेन स्पृशेद्ब्रह्म सनातनम्॥९१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
varṇasya savisargasya visargāntaṃ ctiṃ kuru |<br>
nirādhāreṇa cittena spṛśed brahma sanātanam ||91||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>At the end of a letter bearing visarga,<br />
fix awareness—<br />
with a mind utterly supportless—<br />
and touch the eternal Brahman.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice directs attention to the very end of a visarga sound—such as the "ḥ" in "namaḥ" or the final release in "oṃ." One does not cling to the syllable itself, but to the subtle after-pulse that follows its utterance: the vanishing point where sound dissolves into silence.</p>
<p>Crucially, the mind must be <em>nirādhāra</em>—without any prop, free of expectation, memory, or even the trace of "I am meditating." In that bare attention, the boundary between sound and silence collapses. One does not "achieve" contact with Brahman; one <em>recognizes</em> that the silence at the edge of sound was never separate from the eternal (<em>sanātana brahma</em>).</p>
<p>Though it begins with mantra recitation (āṇavopāya), the moment awareness rests in the unsupported gap after the visarga, it becomes śāktopāya. True śāmbhavopāya is the spontaneous recognition of silence—whether following a carefully intoned mantra or a sudden bird's cry—where no distinction remains between manufactured and natural sound. All sounds are Śiva's play when heard with uncontrived awareness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice centers on a single, precise threshold: the vanishing point where sound dissolves into silence. When intoning a syllable with visarga—the soft "ḥ" release at the end of "oṃ" or "namaḥ"—attention does not linger on the vibration itself, but rests precisely where it fades. This is not a temporal moment between sounds, but the living edge where manifestation returns to source.</p>
<p>The phrase "with a mind utterly supportless" (nirādhāreṇa cittena) is the core instruction. It means no anchor in technique, no expectation of result, no subtle identity as "one who meditates." Even the trace of "I am experiencing silence" must dissolve. In that groundless attention, the boundary between knower and known, sound and silence, collapses—not as achievement, but as recognition of what was never divided.</p>
<p>Lakshmanjoo's reference to "sounds that cannot be recited" serves not to establish hierarchy between sounds, but to illuminate the nature of pure awareness. The critical distinction is not between "manufactured" and "natural" sounds, but between attention that clings and attention that releases. Whether the sound is a carefully intoned mantra or a sudden birdcall, the practice remains the same: rest where it vanishes, with no foothold for the mind.</p>
<p>This is why the visarga—literally a "release"—is the perfect vehicle. It is not the sound itself that matters, but the surrender it embodies. In that surrender, Brahman is not "touched" as an object; the illusion of separation dissolves, revealing what never departed.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>92</h1>
<h3>The Form Of Boundless Sky</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
व्योमाकारं स्वमात्मानं ध्यायेद्दिग्भिरनावृतम्।<br>
निराश्रया चिच्छक्तिः स्वरूपं दर्शयेत्तदा॥९२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vyomākāraṃ svam ātmānaṃ dhyāyed digbhir anāvṛtam |<br>
nirāśrayā cic-chaktiḥ svarūpaṃ darśayet tadā ||92||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Meditate on your own body<br />
as the form of boundless sky—<br />
uncontained by any direction.<br />
Then, the Power of Consciousness,<br />
free from all support,<br />
reveals her true nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Here, <em>svam ātmānam</em> refers not to the individual soul, but to the physical body—reimagined not as flesh and bone, but as <em>vyomākāra</em>, the very shape of space itself. One does not merely think “my body is empty,” but perceives it as utterly void—without edges, without center, without east or west (<em>digbhir anāvṛtam</em>).</p>
<p>In this contemplation, the body ceases to be a localized object and becomes indistinguishable from the infinite <em>ākāśa</em>. As this perception stabilizes, the <em>cic-chakti</em>—the dynamic power of consciousness—no longer leans on form, direction, or identity. It becomes <em>nirāśrayā</em>, supportless. And in that unbound state, it <em>darśayet svarūpam</em>: reveals its own essence—not as an object seen, but as the self-luminous ground of all perception.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya: it begins with intentional visualization, yet its fulfillment is the collapse of the very framework it uses. The body-as-sky is not a metaphor to cling to, but a raft to be abandoned once the shoreless ocean is recognized.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 92 dissolves the body from within. The instruction is radical: not “lighten” the body, but <em>unmake</em> it as a container. “No sides” (<em>digbhir anāvṛtam</em>) means no reference points—no front or back, no inside or out. The body becomes a transparent field, as open as the sky at dawn.</p>
<p>This is not denial of embodiment, but liberation from its illusion of solidity. When you see your hand not as a thing, but as space appearing as hand, the contraction of “mine” relaxes. The heart is no longer a pump in a chest, but a rhythm in the vast.</p>
<p>“Power of Consciousness reveals her true nature” (<em>cic-chaktiḥ svarūpam darśayet</em>) when it stops pretending to be limited. Śakti is not <em>in</em> the body; the body is a ripple <em>in</em> Śakti. When that ripple is seen as empty, Śakti is no longer a force to be wielded—she is the open field in which all forces arise and vanish.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about becoming sky. It is about recognizing that you never were anything else. The body remains, but the prison of “here” has dissolved into the boundless.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>93</h1>
<h3>The Point Of Sharp Sensation</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
किंचिदङ्गं विभिद्यादौ तीक्ष्णसूच्यादिना ततः।<br>
तत्रैव चेतनां युक्त्वा भैरवे निर्मला गतिः॥९३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kiṃcid-aṅgaṃ vibhidyādau tīkṣṇa-sūcyādinā tataḥ |<br>
tatraiva cetanāṃ yuktvā bhairave nirmalā gatiḥ ||93||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Pierce any limb<br />
with a sharp needle or instrument—<br />
then, fixing awareness precisely on that point,<br />
one enters the pure state of Bhairava.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses acute, localized sensation as a gateway to non-dual awareness. One pricks the flesh—finger, arm, any point—with a sharp needle (<em>tīkṣṇa-sūcyādinā</em>). The pain is not avoided or analyzed; instead, attention is gathered instantly and wholly into the raw, pre-conceptual sensation itself (<em>tatraiva cetanāṃ yuktvā</em>).</p>
<p>In that pinpoint focus, the usual sense of body as a unified whole collapses. There is no “my arm,” only the luminous intensity of the prick—so vivid it burns away the subject-object split. In that gap, <em>nirmalā gatiḥ</em>—the stainless path—opens: direct entry into Bhairava’s state, unmediated by thought or identity.</p>
<p>Though the act of piercing belongs to āṇavopāya (physical intervention), the immediate, non-conceptual focus on the raw sensation—before the mind labels it “pain” or “me”—functions as a threshold that <em>anticipates</em> the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya. The pain is not the goal; it is the lens that focuses consciousness to a singularity, beyond which lies pure subjectivity.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 93 is not advocacy of pain, but a radical harnessing of immediacy. The needle’s prick is chosen for its precision: it creates a single, undeniable point of sensation that cannot be ignored. Unlike dull ache or diffuse tension, this sharpness demands total presence.</p>
<p>“Fixing awareness on that point” (<em>tatraiva cetanāṃ yuktvā</em>) is not endurance—it is surrender to the sensation before the mind names it “pain” or “me.” In that pre-reflective instant, the body is forgotten; only awareness remains, wide awake in the flash of intensity.</p>
<p>This is <em>sthūla-paśyantī</em>: the level where sensory experience still has form (<em>sthūla</em>), yet is perceived with the directness of intuition (<em>paśyantī</em>). It is the threshold where śāmbhavopāya can flash forth even through physical means. The purity (<em>nirmalā gatiḥ</em>) arises not from the needle, but from the mind’s total non-resistance to what is.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice meets suffering not as obstacle, but as revelation. The prick is merely a catalyst; the real alchemy is the mind’s willingness to rest in raw experience without flinching. And in that rest, Bhairava is not found in relief from pain—but in the space where pain, self, and separation all dissolve into luminous presence.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>94</h1>
<h3>Denying Inner Instruments</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
चित्ताद्यन्तःकरणं नास्ति ममान्तर्भावयेदिति।<br>
विकल्पानामभावेन विकल्पैरुज्झितो भवेत्॥९४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
cittādy-antaḥkaraṇaṃ nāsti mamāntar bhāvayed iti |<br>
vikalpānām abhāvena vikalpair ujjhito bhavet ||94||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Hold the firm conviction:<br />
“There is no inner instrument—<br />
mind, intellect, or ego—within me.”<br />
Through this absence of conceptualization,<br />
one becomes utterly free from all thoughts.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana employs radical negation as a means to still the inner machinery of identification. The practitioner does not suppress the mind, but <em>denies its very existence</em> as a real, substantial entity within. “There is no <em>antaḥkaraṇa</em>—no mind (<em>citta</em>), intellect (<em>buddhi</em>), or ego (<em>ahaṃkāra</em>) inside me.” This is not denial of experience, but of the presumed <em>owner</em> of experience.</p>
<p>By holding this conviction (<em>bhāvayed iti</em>) with unwavering focus, the habitual functioning of these three faculties ceases—not through force, but through lack of belief. Without the foundational assumption “I have a mind,” thoughts lose their anchor. In that groundlessness, <em>vikalpaiḥ ujjhitaḥ</em>—freed from all mental constructs—one abides in the natural state of śāmbhava, thought-free and self-luminous.</p>
<p>Though it begins as deliberate imagination (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the inner organs are seen as non-existent, the practice reveals its true nature: the spontaneous recognition of awareness prior to all inner division.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 94 cuts to the root of suffering: the belief in a separate, internal “me” that thinks, chooses, and suffers. The <em>antaḥkaraṇa</em>—the triad of mind, intellect, ego—is not rejected as unreal, but <em>disowned</em>. You do not say “my mind is quiet”; you say “there is no mind here at all.”</p>
<p>This is not nihilism. It is liberation through deconstruction. When you stop feeding the idea that “I am the thinker,” thoughts may still arise, but they float like clouds in empty sky—unclaimed, unowned, powerless to bind. The “super-mind” (as the commentary calls it) is not a higher intellect, but awareness itself—the silent witness that was never part of the inner apparatus.</p>
<p>“Absence of conceptualization” (<em>vikalpānām abhāvena</em>) is key. It is not blankness, but the end of the mind’s tyranny—the cessation of its endless commentary, its need to label, judge, and possess. Thus, this practice is not about emptying the mind, but about seeing through the illusion that there ever was a container to empty. And in that seeing, Bhairava is not attained. He is recognized as the space in which all notions of “me” and “my mind” arise—and vanish—like mist in morning sun.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>95</h1>
<h3>Veils Of Limitation</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
माया विमोहिनी नाम कलायाः कलनां स्थितम्।<br>
इत्यादि धर्मं तत्त्वानां कलयन्न पृथग्भवेत्॥९५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
māyā vimohinī nāma kalāyāḥ kalanaṃ sthitam |<br>
ityādi dharmaṃ tattvānāṃ kalayan na pṛthag bhavet ||95||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>“Māyā is the power of delusion;<br />
the function of Kalā is fragmentation”—<br />
contemplating the inherent nature<br />
of each limiting principle,<br />
one remains undivided from the whole.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana works through discriminative insight into the <em>kañcukas</em>—the five constrictive veils (Kalā, Vidyā, Rāga, Kāla, Niyati) and Māyā, the sixth, which binds the infinite Self into the illusion of finitude. The practitioner reflects: “Māyā’s nature is to delude; Kalā’s is to fragment; Vidyā limits knowledge; Rāga binds through desire; Kāla confines in time; Niyati enforces causality. These are their intrinsic functions—but they have nothing to do with <em>me</em>.”</p>
<p>By firmly holding this understanding (<em>kalayan</em>), one does not reject these tattvas, but refuses identification with them. In that non-attachment, the sense of separation (<em>pṛthag bhavet</em>) dissolves—not because the veils vanish, but because the Self is recognized as untouched by them. This is śāktopāya: the power of discernment itself becomes the liberating force.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 95 is a masterstroke of non-dual deconstruction. It does not ask you to destroy Māyā or the <em>kañcukas</em>—impossible, for they are divine functions of Śiva’s freedom. Instead, it invites you to <em>disidentify</em>.</p>
<p>Māyā is not evil; it is the creative power that makes the One appear as many. Kalā is not a flaw; it is the grace that allows the infinite to play at being finite. But the error is not in their existence—it is in the belief “I am this limited being shaped by them.”</p>
<p>The phrase “contemplating the inherent nature” (<em>ityādi dharmaṃ tattvānāṃ kalayan</em>) is precise. It means seeing these principles <em>as they are</em>: impersonal, automatic, and transient. Like weather passing through sky, they move through awareness but cannot stain it.</p>
<p>“One remains undivided” (<em>na pṛthag bhavet</em>) is the fruit. When you stop claiming Māyā’s dream as your reality, the dream loses its power to bind. You are not <em>in</em> the veils—you are the space in which they arise.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not renunciation, but reclamation. By seeing through the machinery of limitation, you reclaim your native freedom: Bhairava, ever whole, ever free—playing in the world, yet never touched by it.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>96</h1>
<h3>Desire's Point Of Arising</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
जाग्रतीच्छां समुत्पन्नामवलोक्य शमं नयेत्।<br>
यत एव समुद्भूता ततस्तत्रैव लीयते॥९६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
jāgrat-icchāṃ samutpannām avalokya śamaṃ nayet |<br>
yata eva samudbhūtā tatas tatraiva līyate ||96||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When a desire arises while awake,<br />
observe it the moment it springs—<br />
then gently dissolve it.<br />
From the very point of its arising,<br />
there it merges back into stillness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana addresses desire not through suppression or indulgence, but through immediate, non-reactive awareness. The moment a desire (<em>icchā</em>) stirs in the mind—before it gathers momentum, before it names its object—one <em>avalokya</em> (looks directly at it) without judgment, without following its pull. In that clear seeing, one <em>nayet śamaṃ</em>—guides it into quietude, not by force, but by withdrawing the attention that feeds it.</p>
<p>Because the desire is met at its source—<em>yata eva samudbhūtā</em>—it dissolves back into the awareness from which it arose (<em>tatraiva līyate</em>). No residue remains. This is not repression; it is alchemical reversal: the energy of desire is returned to its origin before it crystallizes into thought or action.</p>
<p>Though it involves an act of witnessing (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment awareness rests at the threshold of emergence—before vikalpa arises—it becomes śāmbhavopāya. This is the essence of <em>icchopāya</em>: mastery through the instantaneous recognition of will before it becomes willful.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 96 is a precise surgical strike at the root of bondage. Desire is not the enemy; unconscious identification with it is. The instruction is not “stop wanting,” but “see wanting as it is born.”</p>
<p>The critical instant is <em>jāgrat-icchāṃ samutpannām</em>—“a desire arising while awake.” This is not dream or fantasy, but the live spark of impulse in ordinary awareness. Most miss it, already lost in the narrative of fulfillment or denial. The yogi, however, intercepts it <em>at the threshold</em>—the split second between potential and actual.</p>
<p>“Dissolve it” (<em>śamaṃ nayet</em>) does not mean crush it. It means <em>cease to energize it</em>. Like a flame deprived of oxygen, desire extinguishes itself when not fed by attention. And because it never left its source, it returns there seamlessly—like a wave realizing it is ocean.</p>
<p>This is śāmbhavopāya in action: no mantra, no breath, no visualization—only the lightning clarity of presence. The state that arises is not emptiness, but <em>icchā-śūnya</em>: desirelessness not as absence, but as the fullness of uncontracted will.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about becoming desireless, but about becoming so utterly present that desire loses its power to divide. In that presence, Bhairava is not opposed to desire—he is the silent space in which desire arises, dances, and dissolves, untouched.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>97</h1>
<h3>Prior To Will And Knowledge</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यदा ममेच्छा नोत्पन्ना ज्ञानं वा कस्तदास्मि वै।<br>
तत्त्वतोऽहं तथा भूतस्तल्लीनस्तन्मना भवेत्॥९७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yadā mam-icchā nōtpannā jñānaṃ vā kas-tadāsmi vai |<br>
tattvato ’haṃ tathā bhūtas tal-līnas tan-manā bhavet ||97||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>“When neither my will nor my knowing has arisen—<br />
then, who am I?<br />
In truth, I am this!”<br />
Abiding as such, one dissolves into it,<br />
and the mind becomes one with that.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana unfolds as an inquiry into the ground of being prior to all activity. The practitioner asks: <em>When desire (icchā) and cognition (jñāna) are absent—before the mind names, chooses, or seeks—who is present?</em> This is not a philosophical question, but a direct turning of awareness toward its own source.</p>
<p>The answer is not conceptual: <em>“In truth, I am this!”</em> (<em>tattvato ’haṃ tathā bhūtaḥ</em>)—“this” being the silent, unqualified presence that remains when will and knowledge subside. One then <em>abides as that</em> (<em>tal-līnaḥ</em>), allowing the mind to merge completely into this pre-reflective awareness.</p>
<p>Though it begins with self-inquiry (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment attention rests in the “who” before “I,” the method reveals its true nature: śāmbhavopāya. The dissolution of individuality (<em>asmi</em>) is not annihilation, but the recognition that the “I” was never separate from the whole.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 97 strips identity to its naked core. It does not deny desire or knowledge as functions—they arise and vanish in awareness, like waves on ocean. But it exposes the error of identification: the belief that “I am the one who wants” or “I am the knower.”</p>
<p>The question <em>“kas-tadāsmi?”</em>—“then, who am I?”—is not seeking an answer. It is a gesture of surrender: stopping all projection and resting in the open space before self-definition. In that space, there is no “me” composed of wants, beliefs, or memories—only awareness, unmodified and free.</p>
<p>“Dissolves into it” (<em>tal-līnaḥ</em>) means the boundary between inquirer and inquiry vanishes. The mind does not “go” to stillness; it recognizes itself as stillness. What is called “individual consciousness” was only a ripple of thought mistaken for the ocean.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about achieving a state beyond desire and knowledge. It is about seeing that you were never bound by them. The “I” that sought liberation was the very veil—and in the instant it is seen through, Bhairava shines not as other, but as the silent “I am” beneath all names.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>98</h1>
<h3>Emergence Of Will Or Knowledge</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
इच्छयामथवा ज्ञाने जाते चित्तं निवेशयेत्।<br>
आत्मबुद्ध्यनन्यचेतस्ततस्तत्त्वार्थदर्शनम्॥९८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
icchayām athavā jñāne jāte cittaṃ niveśayet |<br>
ātma-buddhy-ananya-cetas tatas tattvārtha-darśanam ||98||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>But when will or knowledge arises,<br />
fix the mind precisely at their point of emergence—<br />
with undivided awareness rooted in the Self.<br />
Then, the true essence of Reality is revealed.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana complements the previous verse by addressing the moment <em>after</em> desire or cognition has stirred. One does not wait for full manifestation, but meets the impulse at its very inception—<em>jāte</em>, “as it arises.” Attention is placed not on the content of desire or thought, but on the <em>point of origin</em> itself: the threshold where potential becomes actual.</p>
<p>This focus must be <em>ātma-buddhy-ananya-cetaḥ</em>—with awareness that is one-pointed, unfragmented, and anchored in the Self (<em>ātma-buddhi</em>). There is no observer separate from the observed; awareness rests in the flash of arising without commentary. In that non-dual seeing, <em>tattvārtha-darśanam</em>—direct vision of reality’s essence—dawns not as concept, but as self-evident presence.</p>
<p>Though it uses a focal point (placing it in śāktopāya), the immediacy of attending to the <em>first spark</em> of icchā or jñāna—before vikalpa crystallizes—makes it brush against śāmbhavopāya. The practitioner chooses <em>one</em> stream—desire <em>or</em> knowledge—not both, to avoid fragmentation of attention. This is <em>prathama-bhāsa</em>, the first glimmer before full manifestation.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 98 is the art of interception. While verse 97 rests in the silence <em>before</em> will and knowledge, this verse engages the instant they <em>begin</em> to emerge. The key is timing: not after the thought has unfolded, but at the live wire of its birth.</p>
<p>“Fix the mind at their point of emergence” means attending to the <em>energy</em> of arising, not the object it seeks. When desire stirs, you do not ask “What do I want?” but feel the raw pulse of wanting itself. When cognition flickers, you do not follow the thought, but rest in the luminosity that precedes it.</p>
<p>This is not analysis—it is <em>witnessing without distance</em>. The phrase “undivided awareness rooted in the Self” (<em>ātma-buddhy-ananya-cetaḥ</em>) clarifies: awareness does not stand apart from the impulse; it <em>is</em> the space in which the impulse appears. Like a mirror reflecting a flame without being burned, consciousness holds the spark of icchā or jñāna without being claimed by it.</p>
<p>“True essence of Reality” (<em>tattvārtha-darśanam</em>) is not a new insight. It is the collapse of the illusion that will and knowledge are separate from awareness. In that collapse, Bhairava is not found beyond desire or thought—he is recognized as the unchanging ground from which they arise, play, and vanish.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about controlling the mind, but about becoming so intimate with its movements that the boundary between seer and seen dissolves—and what remains is freedom, even in the midst of wanting or knowing.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>99</h1>
<h3>Knowing As Rootless Illusion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
निर्निमित्तं भवेज्ज्ञानं निराधारं भ्रमात्मकम्।<br>
तत्त्वतः कस्यचिन्नैतदेवम्भूतः शिवः प्रिये॥९९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nirnimittaṃ bhavej jñānaṃ nirādhāraṃ bhramātmakam |<br>
tattvataḥ kasyacin naitad evaṃbhūtaḥ śivaḥ priye ||99||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>All knowing arises without cause,<br />
without support, and is illusion.<br />
In truth, it belongs to no one—<br />
thus abiding, O Beloved, one becomes Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana exposes the rootless nature of ordinary cognition (<em>jñāna</em>). The knowing of objects—of “this” and “that”—is <em>nirnimittaṃ</em> (causeless), <em>nirādhāraṃ</em> (supportless), and <em>bhramātmakaṃ</em> (deceptive). It appears to arise, yet has no true origin; it seems real, yet rests on nothing.</p>
<p>The illusion stems from the false subject: the limited “I” (<em>ahaṃ</em>) that believes it knows. In truth, there is no knower and no known—only the play of awareness projecting and reabsorbing forms. When one recognizes that <em>this knowing belongs to no one</em> (<em>kasyacin na etat</em>), the entire edifice of objectivity collapses.</p>
<p>In that collapse, one <em>becomes Śiva</em> (<em>evaṃbhūtaḥ śivaḥ</em>)—not by attainment, but by the recognition that one was never separate from the unconditioned awareness in which all apparent knowing floats like foam on ocean.</p>
<p>Though it begins with discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the illusory nature of objective knowledge is seen through, the practitioner rests in the natural state where Śiva and Śakti are one.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 99 is a direct dismantling of epistemology. Ordinary “knowledge”—the cognition of objects, thoughts, sensations—is revealed as a mirage. It has no inherent cause (<em>nirnimittaṃ</em>): no thought arises because it “should,” no perception exists because it is “true.” It is <em>nirādhāraṃ</em>: unsupported by any ultimate reality, like a dream within a dream. And thus, it is <em>bhramātmakaṃ</em>: fundamentally mistaken, mistaking appearance for essence.</p>
<p>The crucial insight is <em>“it belongs to no one.”</em> The belief “I know this” is the knot of bondage. But who is this “I”? The limited ego has no substance; it is a ripple in awareness, mistakenly claiming ownership of the waves. When that claim is seen as empty, knowing does not cease—yet it is no longer “mine.” It simply <em>is</em>, like sunlight on water: vivid, transient, unowned.</p>
<p>This is not Vedāntic negation of the world. Here, even the <em>subject</em>—the “I” that Vedānta often retains as witness—is dissolved into universal I-consciousness (<em>aham-vimarsa</em>), the dynamic self-recognition of Śiva. The world is not unreal; it is <em>real as Śiva</em>, not as separate object.</p>
<p>“Thus abiding” (<em>evaṃbhūtaḥ</em>) means living in this recognition—not as a state to maintain, but as the natural condition when illusion is seen through. The marketplace, the breath, the thought—all are transparent to the one reality: Bhairava, ever free, ever whole.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya because it uses discernment as a tool. But the moment discernment reveals its own groundlessness, the tool drops—and what remains is Śiva, not as other, but as your own unbroken heart.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>100</h1>
<h3>Consciousness Pervading All Bodies</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
चिद्धर्मा सर्वदेहेषु विशेषो नास्ति कुत्रचित्।<br>
अतश्च तन्मयं सर्वं भावयन्भवजिज्जनः॥१००॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
cid-dharmā sarva-deheṣu viśeṣo nāsti kutracit |<br>
ataś ca tan-mayaṃ sarvaṃ bhāvayan bhavajij janaḥ ||100||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Consciousness alone pervades all bodies—<br />
in insect, human, or cosmic form—<br />
without distinction anywhere.<br />
Thus, one who contemplates<br />
that all is filled with That<br />
conquers the cycle of becoming.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals the non-dual ground of all embodiment. The essential nature (<em>dharma</em>) of every being—from the tiniest insect to Brahmā, the cosmic architect—is <em>cit</em>, pure Consciousness. There is no hierarchy, no gradation: the same luminous awareness shines in a blade of grass and in the sun.</p>
<p>When the practitioner <em>bhāvayan</em>—holds the firm conviction—that all forms are saturated with this one Consciousness (<em>tan-mayaṃ sarvaṃ</em>), the illusion of separation collapses. The world of duality (<em>bhava</em>)—the realm of birth, death, and otherness—is not destroyed, but seen through. In that seeing, one <em>bhavajit</em>—conquers becoming—not by escaping it, but by recognizing it as the play of the One.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya: the power of recognition (<em>bhāvanā</em>) alone dissolves the veil of multiplicity. The method uses the perception of unity as a support, but its fruit is the spontaneous abidance where all is Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 100 is the heart of advaitic vision. It does not say “all beings have consciousness,” but “all <em>are</em> consciousness”—not as attribute, but as essence. The ant does not “possess” awareness; it <em>is</em> awareness appearing as ant. Brahmā is not “filled with” cit; he <em>is</em> cit appearing as cosmic order.</p>
<p>“Without distinction anywhere” (<em>viśeṣo nāsti kutracit</em>) is absolute. No form is closer to truth than another. The sacred and profane, pure and impure, are equal expressions of the same ground. This is not egalitarianism—it is the collapse of comparison itself.</p>
<p>To “contemplate all as That” (<em>tan-mayaṃ sarvaṃ bhāvayan</em>) is not idealism. It is lived perception: seeing the street dog and the sage as equally luminous, not because they are the same in form, but because form is transparent to the awareness that animates both.</p>
<p>“Conquers the world of becoming” (<em>bhavajij janaḥ</em>) means liberation within life. The cycle of <em>bhava</em>—the endless chase for fulfillment in form—ends not when forms vanish, but when they are seen as expressions of the formless. The yogi walks in the world, but the world no longer binds, for every face is recognized as the face of Bhairava.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not withdrawal, but total inclusion. In the ordinary act of seeing—truly seeing—the world becomes a mirror of the infinite. And in that mirror, there is no “me” apart from the all—only the one taste of reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>101</h1>
<h3>Unwavering Stillness Amid Turbulence</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
कामक्रोधलोभमोहमदमात्सर्यगोचरे।<br>
बुद्धिं निस्तिमितां कृत्वा तत्तत्त्वमवशिष्यते॥१०१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kāma-krodha-lobha-moha-mada-mātsarya-gocare |<br>
buddhiṃ nistimitāṃ kṛtvā tat tattvam avaśiṣyate ||101||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When desire, anger, greed, delusion,<br />
arrogance, or envy arise,<br />
steady the intellect in unwavering stillness—<br />
and only That Reality remains.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana confronts the most turbulent states of mind not by suppression or escape, but by immediate, unshaken presence. When kāma (desire), krodha (anger), lobha (greed), moha (delusion), mada (arrogance), or mātsarya (envy) surge—precisely at their incipient rise—one does not resist, analyze, or identify with them. Instead, the buddhi (intellect, or discerning awareness) is held <em>nistimitām</em>—motionless, like a calm sea beneath storm winds.</p>
<p>This is not passivity, but radical non-participation. The emotion may rage, but awareness remains untouched, anchored in its own nature. In that stillness, the emotion loses its power to bind, for it is no longer fed by the sense of “I am angry” or “I am envious.” Stripped of ownership, it reveals itself as raw energy—nothing but the pulsation of Consciousness itself.</p>
<p>Thus, <em>tat tattvam avaśiṣyate</em>—only That Reality remains: Śiva, unmodified, ever free. Because this practice occurs at the very threshold of arising—before vikalpa crystallizes into narrative—it belongs to śāmbhavopāya: instantaneous, objectless, and thought-free.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>It does not ask the yogi to avoid emotional storms, but to stand in their eye. The key is <em>timing</em>: the moment anger <em>begins</em> to rise—not after it has possessed the mind. Like a skilled sailor who senses the wind shift before the wave forms, the practitioner meets the impulse at its source.</p>
<p>“Steady the intellect in unwavering stillness” (<em>buddhiṃ nistimitāṃ kṛtvā</em>) means no inner commentary: no “This is wrong,” no “I shouldn’t feel this,” no strategizing. Just pure, open witnessing—like space holding thunder. In that holding, the emotion is not denied; it is <em>seen through</em>.</p>
<p>The six afflictions—kāma, krodha, lobha, moha, mada, mātsarya—are not enemies. They are Shakti in disguise: the very energy of the absolute appearing as disturbance. When met with total presence, their heat becomes the fire of wisdom.</p>
<p>“This is śāmbhavopāya” because no support is used—not breath, not mantra, not visualization. Only naked awareness, before thought divides “me” from “my anger.” In that split-second of pure seeing, the storm reveals its true nature: not chaos, but the dance of Bhairava.</p>
<p>Thus, liberation is not freedom <em>from</em> emotion, but freedom <em>within</em> it. The ocean is not troubled by its waves; the saint is not diminished by passion. And in that unbroken stillness, even wrath becomes a doorway—because what remains, always, is the one Reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>102</h1>
<h3>Divine Illusion Or Ceaseless Flow</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
इन्द्रजालमयं विश्वं न्यस्तं वा चित्रकर्मवत्।<br>
भ्रमद्वा ध्यायतः सर्वं पश्यतस्त सुखोद्गमः॥१०२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
indrajālamayaṃ viśvaṃ nyastaṃ vā citrakarmavat |<br>
bhramad vā dhyāyataḥ sarvaṃ paśyatasta sukho udgamaḥ ||102||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Contemplate the entire universe<br />
as a magician’s illusion,<br />
or as a masterfully painted scene,<br />
or as a ceaseless flow of movement—<br />
gazing thus, bliss spontaneously arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals the world not as illusion to be rejected, but as divine play to be seen through. The universe is <em>indrajālamayam</em>—like a magician’s trick—where the Great Magician, Bhairava himself, projects multiplicity from unity. It is <em>nyastaṃ citrakarmavat</em>—a painting placed before us—not as deception, but as the spontaneous expression (<em>vikāsa</em>) of Śiva’s sovereign freedom (<em>svātantrya</em>). Or it is <em>bhramat</em>—perpetually in motion—like a cinematic flow where beings shift from childhood to death, birth to rebirth, without ever ceasing to be the one reality.</p>
<p>When one <em>dhyāyataḥ</em> (contemplates) and <em>paśyataḥ</em> (sees) the world thus—not as solid and separate, but as transparent play—the heart opens into <em>sukho udgamaḥ</em>, the spontaneous upwelling of bliss. This belongs to śāktopāya because it begins with deliberate contemplation (<em>dhyāna</em>) before blossoming into direct perception (<em>sākṣātkāra</em>). True bliss arises not from denying the world, but from recognizing its divine nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 102 transforms perception itself into liberation. The "magician’s illusion" is not a metaphor for falsehood, but for transparency. Just as a painting holds no substance apart from the canvas, the world holds no reality apart from consciousness. When you see a sunset, you do not mistake the colors for independent entities—you know them as light playing on atmosphere. So too, all forms—mountains, emotions, galaxies—are luminous vibrations in the one field of awareness.</p>
<p>Ceaseless motion (<em>bhramat</em>) reveals impermanence not as loss, but as the very rhythm of freedom. Nothing is fixed: the child becomes the elder, the seed becomes the tree, the thought becomes the silence. This ceaseless flow is not chaos; it is the rhythm of the absolute dancing in time. To see this is to relax the grip of "mine" and "not mine"—the root of sorrow.</p>
<p>"Bliss arises" (<em>sukho udgamaḥ</em>) not because suffering vanishes, but because its sting dissolves in recognition. When anger arises, it is not "my anger," but energy appearing as anger. When joy comes, it is not "my joy," but the universe celebrating itself. This is not detachment—it is full participation without ownership.</p>
<p>The practice is simple: look at anything—a crowded street, a stormy sky, your own breath—and see it as <em>indrajāla</em>: shimmering, insubstantial, yet vividly alive. In that seeing, the world does not disappear. It becomes a sacrament. And in that sacrament, Bhairava is not hidden. He is the very aliveness of the dance.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>103</h1>
<h3>Between Suffering And Pleasure</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
न चित्तं निक्षिपेद्दुःखे न सुखे वा परिक्षिपेत्।<br>
भैरवि ज्ञायतां मध्ये किं तत्त्वमवशिष्यते॥१०३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
na cittaṃ nikṣiped duḥkhe na sukhe vā parikṣipet |<br>
bhairavi jñāyatāṃ madhye kiṃ tattvam avaśiṣyate ||103||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Let the mind not settle in suffering,<br />
nor scatter itself in pleasure.<br />
O Bhairavī, know the middle—<br />
what Reality then remains?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana instructs the practitioner to avoid the dual extremes of pain (<em>duḥkha</em>) and pleasure (<em>sukha</em>). The mind habitually clings to one or recoils from the other, but both are movements within duality. True freedom lies in the <em>madhye</em>—the neutral axis between them.</p>
<p>This “middle” is not a midpoint in time or sensation, but the gap where neither pain nor pleasure holds sway—where the mind is neither contracted in aversion nor expanded in attachment. It is the still point between the push and pull of experience.</p>
<p>By turning awareness to this center (<em>jñāyatāṃ madhye</em>), the practitioner does not suppress feeling, but ceases to identify with it. In that non-identification, the question arises: <em>What remains when both pleasure and pain are seen through?</em> And in that question, <em>kiṃ tattvam avaśiṣyate</em>—only That Reality is left: Śiva, unmodified by affect.</p>
<p>Though it begins with intentional focus (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment awareness rests in the gap, the method reveals its true nature: the spontaneous abidance of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 103 cuts through the cycle of emotional reactivity. We spend life chasing pleasure and avoiding pain, yet both bind equally—pleasure through hope, pain through fear. The “middle” is not indifference; it is full presence without ownership.</p>
<p>When pain arises, do not become the sufferer. When joy comes, do not become the enjoyer. Rest instead in the space <em>between</em> identification and rejection—the silent witness that holds both without being stained. This is not passivity, but radical freedom: the ability to feel fully without being defined by what is felt.</p>
<p>“Know the middle” (<em>jñāyatāṃ madhye</em>) is a call to constant vigilance. It is not a state to achieve, but a stance to embody—again and again, as the commentary insists, even across lifetimes. The moment you notice the mind leaning toward sorrow or elation, gently return to the axis.</p>
<p>“What Reality remains?” is not a riddle. It is an invitation to discover that beneath all emotion, there is only awareness—luminous, boundless, and free. In that discovery, Bhairava is not found beyond feeling. He is the stillness within which all feeling arises, dances, and dissolves.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>104</h1>
<h3>Beyond Body Identification</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
विहाय निजदेहस्थं सर्वत्रास्मीति भावयन्।<br>
दृढेन मनसा दृष्ट्या नान्यैक्षिण्य सुखी भवेत्॥१०४॥<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
