<p>After relinquishing identification with the body,<br />
firmly hold the conviction: “I am everywhere.”<br />
With unwavering mind and undivided gaze,<br />
one becomes bliss itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 104 invites direct collapse of the boundary between self and world. It does not ask you to imagine “I am everywhere” as poetic fancy, but to <em>unlearn</em> the contraction that places awareness inside a skin-bound form.</p>
<p>“Relinquishing identification with the body” (<em>vihāya nija-dehastham</em>) is not rejection of embodiment; it is the simple seeing that awareness was never <em>contained</em> by form. The body remains, but the belief “I am this” drops away—not through effort, but through the clarity that no sensation, no limb, no breath was ever truly “mine.”</p>
<p>“I am everywhere” (<em>sarvatrāsmi</em>) is not inflation of ego, but dissolution of it. It is the recognition that the same luminosity seeing through your eyes is seeing through all eyes—that the awareness reading these words is the very life of the cosmos.</p>
<p>“Undivided gaze” (<em>nānyai-kṣiṇyā dṛṣṭyā</em>) means no split between seer and seen, inside and out. When perception is no longer claimed by a center, the world is not “out there”—it is the pulsation of your own being.</p>
<p>In that unbroken continuity, bliss is not an emotion—it is the natural resonance of awareness free from the friction of separation. And in that freedom, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the space that holds all bodies as one.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>105</h1>
<h3>Not Confined Within But In All Things</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
घटादौ यच्च विज्ञानमिच्छाद्यं वा ममान्तरे।<br>
नैव सर्वगतं जातं भावयन्निति सर्वगः॥१०५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ghaṭādau yac ca vijñānam icchādyaṃ vā mamāntare |<br>
naiva sarvagataṃ jātaṃ bhāvayann iti sarvagaḥ ||105||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>“Knowledge, will, and such<br />
are not confined within me—<br />
they dwell even in jars and all things.”<br />
Contemplating thus the omnipresent Reality,<br />
one becomes all-pervading.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana dissolves the boundary between subject and object. The practitioner reflects: <em>The awareness that perceives this pot—is it truly “mine”? The desire to use it—is it located only in my mind?</em> In truth, consciousness is not a private possession. It is universal—already present in the pot, the bottle, the sky.</p>
<p>If consciousness were only “inside,” how could the pot appear in a dream when the physical object is absent? Its appearance proves that both object and subject arise within one field. The separation is illusion (<em>indrajāla</em>)—the divine play of the Magician.</p>
<p>By firmly holding (<em>bhāvayan</em>) this insight—that cognition and will are not personal but omnipresent (<em>sarvagataṃ</em>)—the sense of being a localized self collapses. One does not “attain” pervasion; one <em>recognizes</em> oneself as <em>sarvagaḥ</em>: all-pervading, like space.</p>
<p>Though it uses reflective contemplation (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment duality between “me” and “the pot” is seen through, the practice reveals the non-dual truth of Śaivism: <em>eka pramātā viśva-pramātā</em>—the one knower is the universal knower.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 105 is a direct strike at the root of alienation. The belief “I am here, the world is out there” is the primal contraction. This verse undoes it by revealing that the pot is not <em>outside</em> awareness—it <em>is</em> awareness appearing as pot.</p>
<p>The example of dreaming is key. In sleep, the pot reappears without its physical counterpart. This shows the pot was never truly “external”—it was always a modulation of consciousness. The waking world is no different: a dream with shared rules.</p>
<p>“Spectacles are the bottle, the bottle is the spectacles” is not metaphor. It is ontological equality: all forms are transient expressions of the same ground. The name and function differ, but the substance—cit—does not.</p>
<p>To “become all-pervading” (<em>sarvagaḥ</em>) is not to expand the self, but to see that the self was never small. When you no longer claim awareness as “mine,” you discover it as the open field in which galaxies and grains of sand arise equally.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not philosophy—it is lived deconstruction. In the ordinary act of seeing a jar, the yogi sees the universe. And in that seeing, Bhairava is not other. He is the space that holds both seer and seen.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>106</h1>
<h3>Unwavering Presence</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
ग्राह्यग्राहकसंवित्तिः सामान्या सर्वदेहिनाम्।<br>
योगिनां तु विशेषोऽयं संबन्धे सावधानता॥१०६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
grāhya-grāhaka-saṃvittiḥ sāmānyā sarva-dehinām |<br>
yogināṃ tu viśeṣo ’yaṃ saṃbandhe sāvadhānatā ||106||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The awareness of object and subject<br />
is common to all embodied beings.<br />
But the yogi’s distinction lies here:<br />
in every encounter, unwavering presence.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>All beings experience the duality of <em>grāhya</em> (object) and <em>grāhaka</em> (subject)—the known and the knower. A child, a merchant, a sage—all perceive a flower as “this” (object) and “I” (subject). This dualistic cognition (<em>saṃvittiḥ</em>) is universal.</p>
<p>What sets the yogi apart is not the <em>structure</em> of perception, but its <em>quality</em>: <em>sāvadhānatā</em>—constant, luminous awareness within the act of relating. While ordinary beings are lost in the content of experience—reacting with desire to a friend, aversion to an enemy—the yogi remains fully present in the <em>contact itself</em> (<em>saṃbandhe</em>).</p>
<p>For the yogi, the meeting of subject and object is not a trigger for contraction or expansion of the ego, but a transparent revelation of Consciousness playing as both sides of the encounter. This is the “divine contact”: not withdrawal from relationship, but total immersion without identification.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 106 reframes enlightenment not as escape from duality, but as mastery within it. The world of subject and object is not denied—it is the very field of realization. The difference is not <em>what</em> is seen, but <em>how</em> it is held.</p>
<p>When an ordinary person sees an enemy, the contact (<em>saṃbandha</em>) becomes a knot: “I am threatened.” When the yogi sees the same face, the contact becomes a mirror: “Here is Bhairava appearing as opposition.” The object hasn’t changed; the depth of seeing has.</p>
<p>“Unwavering presence” (<em>sāvadhānatā</em>) is the yogi’s secret. It is not mindfulness as technique, but as natural state—the continuous hum of awareness beneath all reactions. This presence transforms every interaction into <em>darśana</em>, direct seeing.</p>
<p>Thus, the yogi does not transcend the world of relationships. They sanctify it. In the handshake, the argument, the glance—each moment becomes an altar where subject and object dissolve into the one reality. And in that dissolution, Bhairava is not beyond the encounter. He is the encounter itself—luminous, boundless.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>107</h1>
<h3>Another's Body As One's Own</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
स्ववदन्यशरीरेऽपि संवित्तिमनुभावयेत्।<br>
आपेक्षां स्वशरीरस्य त्यक्त्वा व्यापी दिनैर्भवेत्॥१०७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
svavad-anya-śarīre ’pi saṃvittim anubhāvayet |<br>
āpekṣāṃ sva-śarīrasya tyaktvā vyāpī dinair bhavet ||107||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Feel awareness in another’s body<br />
as you feel it in your own.<br />
Relinquishing concern for your form alone,<br />
within days, you become all-pervading.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana cultivates direct, non-conceptual empathy as a path to non-duality. One does not merely <em>imagine</em> another’s pain or joy, but <em>feels it as one’s own</em>—as if the needle pricking another’s flesh were piercing one’s own. This is not emotional sympathy, but the dissolution of the boundary between “my body” and “your body” at the level of sensation itself.</p>
<p>By repeatedly placing awareness (<em>saṃvitti</em>) in the other’s form (<em>anya-śarīre</em>) with the intimacy of self-recognition (<em>svavat</em>), the habitual fixation on one’s own body (<em>āpekṣāṃ sva-śarīrasya</em>) softens. The sense of being a localized entity confined to a single organism begins to dissolve.</p>
<p>As this practice deepens—<em>dinair bhavet</em>, “within days”—the practitioner naturally expands into <em>vyāpī</em>, the all-pervading state. The “fruit” is not virtue, but the collapse of separation: when another weeps, it is not “their” sorrow, but the universe feeling itself.</p>
<p>This belongs to śāktopāya: the power of conscious identification alone becomes the vehicle to universal awareness.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 107 transforms empathy from moral duty to ontological truth. The ordinary mind says, “Your pain is yours; my pain is mine.” The yogi discovers: <em>There is only pain—and awareness holding it.</em></p>
<p>The instruction is visceral: when you see a beggar’s blistered feet, do not pity—<em>feel the raw skin as your own</em>. When a child laughs, do not observe—<em>let the joy vibrate in your chest as if it were your own breath</em>. This is not projection; it is deconstruction of the illusion that consciousness is owned.</p>
<p>“Relinquishing concern for your form” (<em>āpekṣāṃ sva-śarīrasya tyaktvā</em>) does not mean neglecting the body. It means ceasing to believe the body is the seat of “me.” The body remains, but awareness no longer contracts around it. Like a river that stops calling itself “this stream,” it flows as the ocean.</p>
<p>“Within days” (<em>dinair bhavet</em>) is not hyperbole. When identification with the separate self is pierced daily, the shift is rapid. The practitioner stops asking, “How do I feel?” and begins to feel <em>through</em> all beings—because all beings are the same openness appearing as form.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about becoming compassionate. It is about recognizing that compassion was never absent—only obscured by the fiction of separation. And in that recognition, Bhairava is not “in” all beings. He is the beating heart of all beings.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>108</h1>
<h3>Beyond All Thought</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
निराधारं मनः कृत्वा विकल्पान्न विकल्पयेत्।<br>
तदात्मपरमात्मत्वे भैरवो मृगलोचने॥१०८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nirādhāraṃ manaḥ kṛtvā vikalpān na vikalpayet |<br>
tadātma-paramātmatve bhairavo mṛgalocane ||108||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Render the mind supportless,<br />
and do not even entertain a single thought.<br />
Then, O gazelle-eyed one,<br />
when the self merges into the Supreme Self,<br />
Bhairava is realized.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is pure śāmbhavopāya—immediate, objectless, and free from all method. One does not meditate <em>on</em> anything, not even on emptiness. Instead, the mind is made <em>nirādhāra</em>—deprived of every foothold: no breath, no image, no mantra, no sensation, not even the subtle support of “I am aware.”</p>
<p>In that groundlessness, one <em>does not allow any thought to arise</em> (<em>vikalpān na vikalpayet</em>)—not by suppression, but by refusing to grant attention to the very possibility of thought. Without support, the mind has no place to stand, no fuel to burn. In that stillness, the individual self (<em>ātman</em>) dissolves into the Supreme Self (<em>paramātman</em>), and Bhairava—the non-dual reality—shines forth not as an attainment, but as what was always already the case.</p>
<p>There is no sequence, no progression, no “practice” in the conventional sense. It is the abrupt cessation of all doing, leaving only being.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice dissolves the very architecture of seeking. To "render the mind supportless" (nirādhāraṃ manaḥ kṛtvā) is not to empty an already-existing container, but to recognize that awareness was never confined to the notion of "mind" in the first place. The instruction "do not entertain a single thought" (vikalpān na vikalpayet) is not suppression, but the natural clarity that arises when attention ceases its habitual grasping at the edges of experience.</p>
<p>Thoughts may still come and go like clouds in the sky, but what is called for here is the absence of inner commentary—the subtle momentum that transforms a passing sensation into "my thought," "my feeling," "my experience." This is not achieved through will, but through recognition: the moment one stops reinforcing the ground on which thoughts stand, their power to define reality dissolves.</p>
<p>In that dissolution—where no foothold for the separate self remains—what emerges is not emptiness but fullness. The phrase "when the self merges into the Supreme Self" (tadātma-paramātmatve) is not a journey of union, but the collapse of the illusion that separation ever existed. Bhairava is not realized as an object of attainment; he is recognized as the ground already present when all supports vanish—not as void, but as the luminous fullness that was never obscured.</p>
<p>This is śāmbhavopāya in its purest expression: not a practice to perfect, but the cessation of all doing. There is no "me" becoming supportless; there is only supportlessness appearing as wakefulness. No technique bridges this gap, because no gap exists. The mind does not "achieve" groundlessness; it recognizes itself as that which was never bound to begin with.</p>
<p>Most crucially, this recognition is not delayed. "Then" (tadā) does not imply sequence—it is the timeless now where contraction meets its end. Like a clenched fist relaxing into open palm, what appears as realization is simply the body returning to its natural state. Bhairava does not "arrive" when thoughts cease; he is recognized as the space in which all arising and subsiding appears—a space that was never diminished by the presence of thoughts, nor enhanced by their absence.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>109</h1>
<h3>Identity With Supreme</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सर्वज्ञः सर्वकर्ता च व्यापकः परमेश्वरः।<br>
स एवाहं शैवधर्म इति दार्ढ्याद्भवेच्छिवः॥१०९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sarvajñaḥ sarva-kartā ca vyāpakaḥ parameśvaraḥ |<br>
sa evāhaṃ śaiva-dharma iti dārḍhyād bhavet śivaḥ ||109||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>“The Supreme Lord is all-knowing,<br />
all-acting, and all-pervading—<br />
I am none other than He.”<br />
By unwavering conviction in this truth,<br />
one becomes Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana unfolds in two movements. First, one contemplates the nature of Paramaśvara—<em>sarvajña</em> (omniscient), <em>sarva-kartā</em> (omnipotent), <em>vyāpaka</em> (all-pervading)—not as a distant deity, but as the living reality of consciousness itself. Then, one <em>transfers</em> that awareness inward, recognizing: <em>“This very I-am-ness is that Lord.”</em></p>
<p>The phrase <em>śaiva-dharma</em>—“the essential nature of Śiva”—is key. It is not a declaration of identity with a form, but the affirmation that one’s own awareness <em>is</em> the infinite, dynamic intelligence that knows, acts, and pervades all. When this is held with <em>dārḍhyāt</em>—unshakable steadiness—identification with limitation dissolves, and one <em>becomes Śiva</em> (<em>bhavet śivaḥ</em>) not through attainment, but through recognition.</p>
<p>Though it begins with contemplation and affirmation (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the conviction becomes total, the boundary between meditator and meditated vanishes, revealing the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 109 is the culmination of non-dual affirmation. It does not say “I am like Śiva” or “I will become Śiva.” It declares: <em>“I am He—here, now, as this very awareness.”</em></p>
<p>The three attributes are not theological claims, but experiential truths:<br />
- <em>Sarvajña</em>: Awareness knows without effort—thoughts, sensations, worlds arise within it, already known.<br />
- <em>Sarva-kartā</em>: All action flows through it—breathing, speaking, dying—none separate from its spontaneous play.<br />
- <em>Vyāpaka</em>: It is not located—present in the ant’s step and the galaxy’s spin, equally.</p>
<p>To “firmly establish” (<em>dārḍhyāt</em>) this is not mental repetition, but living from it. When anger arises, it is Śiva’s energy appearing as anger. When stillness comes, it is Śiva resting as stillness. There is no “me” apart from this flow.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya because it uses affirmation as a bridge. But the bridge burns away in the crossing. The moment “I am Śiva” is not a thought but the ground of being, practice ends—and what remains is Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>110</h1>
<h3>All Forms Emanate From The Self</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
जलस्येवोर्मयो वह्नेर्ज्वालाभार्गयः प्रभारवेः।<br>
ममैव भैरवस्यैता विश्वभङ्ग्यो विभेदिताः॥११०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
jalasyevormayo vahner jvālā-bhārgayaḥ prabhāraveḥ |<br>
mamaiva bhairavasyaitā viśva-bhaṅgyo vibhedditāḥ ||110||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Just as waves rise from water,<br />
flames from fire,<br />
and rays from the sun—<br />
so all forms of the universe<br />
emanate from me,<br />
who am none other than Bhairava.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals the non-dual source of all manifestation through natural metaphors. Waves do not exist apart from water; flames are not separate from fire; rays are not distinct from the sun. Likewise, every form of the universe—mountains, thoughts, stars—is a modulation of the one reality: <em>mamaiva bhairavasya</em>, “I, who am Bhairava.”</p>
<p>The practitioner does not merely understand this intellectually, but <em>sees</em> it with open eyes (<em>unmīlana</em>). In the marketplace, in the storm, in the breath—every phenomenon is recognized as a ripple in the infinite ocean of awareness.</p>
<p>Though it begins with contemplation (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment this seeing becomes spontaneous—when duality collapses in direct perception—the method matures into śāmbhavopāya. The universe is not denied; it is seen as the living body of Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 110 is a hymn of immanence. It does not place Bhairava beyond the world, but declares: <em>The world is Bhairava in motion.</em></p>
<p>The metaphors are precise:<br />
- <strong>Waves</strong> (<em>ūrmayaḥ</em>) arise and vanish, yet water remains unchanged. So too, forms appear and disappear, but awareness is untouched.<br />
- <strong>Flames</strong> (<em>jvālāḥ</em>) dance with heat and light, yet are never separate from fire. So too, all action and energy are Śakti, inseparable from Śiva.<br />
- <strong>Rays</strong> (<em>bhārgayaḥ</em>) radiate outward, yet are nothing but the sun’s own luminosity. So too, every perception is the self-expression of consciousness.</p>
<p>“From me” (<em>mamaiva</em>) is not the boast of an ego, but the declaration of the absolute: <em>There is no other.</em> The “I” here is not the limited self, but the universal “I” (<em>aham-vimarśa</em>) that is the heart of all beings.</p>
<p>This is <em>unmīlana</em> practice—eyes open, world embraced—not as escape, but as recognition. When you see a leaf fall, you do not see “a leaf.” You see Bhairava falling as a leaf.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not poetry. It is instruction. In every moment, look—and see the wave, the flame, the ray—and know: <em>This is me. This is Bhairava. There is nothing else.</em> And in that knowing, the universe does not vanish. It becomes a mirror—luminous.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>111</h1>
<h3>Sudden Falling As Gateway</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
भ्रान्त्वा भ्रान्त्वा शरीरेण त्वरितं भुवि पतनात्।<br>
क्षोभशक्तिविरामेण परा संज्ञायते दशा॥१११॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
bhrāntvā bhrāntvā śarīreṇa tvaritaṃ bhuvi patanāt |<br>
kṣobha-śakti-virāmeṇa parā saṃjñāyate daśā ||111||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Wandering long with the body,<br />
then suddenly falling to the earth—<br />
when the agitated energy ceases,<br />
the supreme state is revealed.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses physical exhaustion as a direct path to stillness. One wanders tirelessly—walking for hours without rest—until the body is saturated with motion (<em>bhrāntvā bhrāntvā</em>). At the peak of fatigue, one collapses abruptly onto the ground (<em>tvaritaṃ bhuvi patanāt</em>), surrendering all effort.</p>
<p>In that sudden cessation, the <em>kṣobha-śakti</em>—the energy of agitation that animated the journey—naturally subsides (<em>virāmeṇa</em>). With no residual will to move or think, the mind, exhausted of its kinetic habit, settles into its source. In that stillness, <em>parā daśā</em>, the supreme state, dawns not as an achievement, but as the natural condition beneath all motion.</p>
<p>Though it begins with action (placing it in āṇavopāya), the moment agitation ceases and awareness rests without support, the method becomes pure śāmbhavopāya—the effortless recognition of what is already whole.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 111 reveals liberation through total surrender—not philosophical, but somatic. The wandering is not aimless; it is the deliberate exhaustion of the will to control. Like a spinning top that collapses into stillness only when its momentum ends, the yogi moves until movement can no longer be sustained.</p>
<p>The "sudden fall" (<em>tvaritaṃ patanāt</em>) is key. It is not a planned rest, but an involuntary collapse—total letting go. In that surrender, the body becomes a corpse, and the mind, deprived of its habitual anchors (goal, direction, rhythm), has no choice but to return to its origin.</p>
<p>“Agitated energy ceases” (<em>kṣobha-śakti-virāmeṇa</em>) does not mean the energy vanishes. It is reabsorbed. Like waves dissolving into ocean, the kinetic force of wandering returns to the stillness from which it arose. In that reabsorption, what remains is not emptiness, but fullness: the unbroken presence of Bhairava.</p>
<p>This is śāmbhavopāya because no technique is applied. The yogi does not meditate on breath, void, or mantra—only collapses into the earth, and in that collapse, the illusion of separation ends. The ground is not beneath the body; it is the body’s true nature.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about pilgrimage, but about the journey to the end of seeking. When the legs can carry you no further, and you fall into the earth—there, in that surrender, you discover you were never separate from the ground of all being.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>112</h1>
<h3>When Mental Capacity Ceases</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अधारेष्वथवासक्त्या ज्ञानाच्चित्तलयेन वा।<br>
जातशक्तिसमावेशक्षोभान्ते भैरवं वपुः॥११२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
adhāreṣv athavāsaktyā-jñānāt citta-layena vā |<br>
jāta-śakti-samāveśa-kṣobhānte bhairavaṃ vapuḥ ||112||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When, due to inability to grasp objects<br />
through the senses,<br />
or through the mind’s dissolution,<br />
the agitation of absorbed energy ceases—<br />
then the form of Bhairava manifests.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals that even incapacity can be a gateway. When the senses (<em>adhāra</em>—bases of perception: eyes, ears, skin, etc.) fail to comprehend their objects—due to blindness, ignorance, or mental exhaustion (<em>asaktyā jñānāt</em>)—the usual stream of cognition halts. Alternatively, when the mind itself dissolves (<em>citta-layena</em>), all interpretive activity stops.</p>
<p>In that gap—where curiosity remains but understanding fails—the energy of knowing (<em>śakti</em>) has nowhere to go outward. It turns inward, not as frustration, but as spontaneous self-absorption (<em>samāveśa</em>). The resulting inner turbulence (<em>kṣobha</em>) soon settles, and in its stillness, <em>bhairavaṃ vapuḥ</em>—the essential nature of Bhairava—shines forth not as a vision, but as the ground of being itself.</p>
<p>Though it arises from limitation (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the energy reverses into its source, the method reveals the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse illuminates the transparency of apparent limitation. When sensory perception fails—not through defect, but through the natural boundary of all knowing—or when mental constructs spontaneously dissolve, what remains is not deficiency, but openness. The "agitation of absorbed energy" (jāta-śakti-samāveśa-kṣobha) is not the turbulence of failure, but the luminous vibration of awareness no longer contracted around objects. Its "ceasing" is not collapse, but completion: the natural settling of energy into its source.</p>
<p>Lakshmanjoo's Transmission is precise: this is not a technique to manufacture states of incapacity, but recognition when they arise spontaneously. The moment conceptual understanding reaches its natural limit—whether in complex philosophy or simple daily life—the mind's habitual grasping relaxes not through effort, but through its own exhaustion. In that relaxation, there is no "gap to be filled," no "problem to solve," and certainly no "cracks" through which Bhairava must enter. There is only the recognition of what remains when the mind stops projecting its maps onto reality.</p>
<p>The "form of Bhairava" (bhairavaṃ vapuḥ) is not a new experience arising from incapacity. It is the ever-present ground—like the depth of the ocean that remains undisturbed when surface waves subside. The energy that appeared as agitation was never separate from this ground; it was the ground itself in motion. What we call "inability" is simply the moment the illusion of separation thins enough to see through—not to something beyond, but to what has always been here.</p>
<p>This is not śāktopāya becoming śāmbhavopāya; it is the immediate recognition that both were always one. The "energy of knowing" (śakti) does not "reverse course" as if it had ever departed. It simply reveals itself as what it always was: the pulsation of awareness itself, never bound by the appearances it animates.</p>
<p>Most crucially, this is not about attaining knowledge but recognizing the space where all knowing arises. When the mind surrenders its need to comprehend—not through resignation but through the natural exhaustion of all conceptual frameworks—what remains is not ignorance but the luminous fullness that was never obscured. The "curiosity without understanding" is not a doorway to cross, but the very nature of awareness prior to all division.</p>
<p>In its essence, this verse does not sanctify failure. It reveals that what we call failure was never separate from freedom. When the last prop of conceptual understanding dissolves—not as loss but as homecoming—Bhairava does not "arrive." He is recognized as the space in which all arrivals and departures appear.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>113-114</h1>
<h3>Steady Gaze And Soundless Resonance</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सम्प्रदायमिमं देवि शृणु संयग्वदाम्यहम्।<br>
कैवल्यं जायते सद्यो नेत्रयोः स्थब्धमात्रयोः॥११३॥<br>
संकोचं कर्णयोः कृत्वा ह्यधोद्वारे तथैव च।<br>
अनाक्षमहलं ध्यायन्विशेद्ब्रह्म सनातनम्॥११४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sampradāyam imaṃ devi śṛṇu saṃyag vadāmy aham |<br>
kaivalyaṃ jāyate sadyo netrayoḥ stabdha-mātrayoḥ ||113||<br>
saṃkocaṃ karṇayoḥ kṛtvā hy adho-dvāre tathaiva ca |<br>
anākṣam ahalaṃ dhyāyan viśed brahma sanātanam ||114||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Goddess, hear this sacred transmission—<br />
I reveal it to you in full:<br />
The instant the eyes are held steady,<br />
without a blink,<br />
liberation dawns.</p>
<p>Close the ears,<br />
and likewise seal the lower gate;<br />
meditate on the sound<br />
that is neither vowel nor consonant—<br />
and merge into the eternal Brahman.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These two verses present direct methods of śāmbhavopāya—immediate, objectless, and free from sequential technique.</p>
<p><strong>Verse 113</strong>: The practitioner fixes the gaze (<em>netrayoḥ stabdha-mātrayoḥ</em>) with eyes wide open, yet without grasping any object. This is not staring, but <em>seeing without seeing</em>—a total suspension of perceptual grasping. In that unwavering stillness, the subject-object split collapses, and <em>kaivalya</em>—absolute aloneness, the state of Śiva—arises instantly (<em>sadyaḥ</em>).</p>
<p><strong>Verse 114</strong>: One simultaneously closes the ears (<em>saṃkocaṃ karṇayoḥ</em>) and contracts the perineum (<em>adho-dvāra</em>, the root center), sealing the lower aperture. In this internalized stillness, attention turns to <em>anākṣam ahalaṃ</em>—the unstruck sound beyond vowels and consonants, the silent vibration of pure awareness. By resting in this inaudible resonance, one enters <em>brahma sanātanam</em>, the eternal ground of being.</p>
<p>Both methods bypass breath, mantra, and visualization. They are not “practices” in the conventional sense, but sudden alignments with the natural state—hence pure śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses distill realization to its essence: <strong>non-grasping</strong>.</p>
<p>In <strong>verse 113</strong>, the eyes remain open, yet perception is unanchored. You do not look at anything; you abide as the open space in which seeing appears. This is not trance—it is hyper-awake presence. The moment the mind stops labeling “this is a wall,” “that is a sky,” the world becomes transparent, and only luminous emptiness remains. <em>Kaivalya</em>—“aloneness”—is not isolation, but the end of separation.</p>
<p>In <strong>verse 114</strong>, the sealing of ears and root center is not muscular effort, but the natural gathering of awareness when attention withdraws from the senses. The “sound without vowel or consonant” (<em>anākṣam ahalaṃ</em>) is not heard with ears—it is the hum of existence itself, the <em>nāda</em> that underlies all sound. To “meditate” on it is to become it: silent, boundless, self-luminous.</p>
<p>Both methods converge on the same truth: liberation is not <em>attained</em>—it is <em>recognized</em> when the mind stops reaching. The “eternal Brahman” is not a place to enter; it is the space that remains when all exits—through eye, ear, and body—are closed not by force, but by the simple, total absence of seeking.</p>
<p>Thus, these are not techniques to perfect, but thresholds to cross—<em>now</em>, in this breath, in this blinkless gaze, in this silent hum. And in that crossing, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the stillness beneath the blink, the silence beneath the sound.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>115-116</h1>
<h3>Abyss Gazing And Mind's Pervasion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
कूपादिके महागर्ते स्थित्वोपरि निरीक्षणात्।<br>
अविकल्पमतेः सम्यक्सद्यश्चित्तलयः स्फुटम्॥११५॥<br>
यत्र यत्र मनो याति बाह्ये वाभ्यन्तरेऽपि वा।<br>
तत्र तत्र शिवावस्था व्यापकत्वात्क्व यास्यति॥११६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kūpādike mahāgarte sthitvopari nirīkṣaṇāt |<br>
avikalpa-mateḥ saṃyak sadyaś citta-layaḥ sphuṭam ||115||<br>
yatra yatra mano yāti bāhye vābhyantare ’pi vā |<br>
tatra tatra śivāvasthā vyāpakatvāt kva yāsyati ||116||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Standing above a deep well or abyss,<br />
gazing steadily downward—<br />
with a thought-free mind,<br />
the dissolution of mind<br />
arises instantly and unmistakably.</p>
<p>Wherever the mind goes—<br />
outward or inward—<br />
there is the state of Śiva.<br />
Since He is all-pervading,<br />
where else could it go?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p><strong>Verse 115</strong> uses the abyss as a mirror for the mind’s depth. One stands at the edge of a deep well or chasm and gazes fixedly into its bottomless dark. The sheer verticality and emptiness arrest ordinary perception. When the mind is held <em>avikalpa</em>—free from conceptual overlay—in that unbroken downward gaze, thought collapses (<em>citta-layaḥ</em>) not through effort, but through awe. This belongs to śāktopāya, as it uses an external support to induce stillness.</p>
<p><strong>Verse 116</strong> transcends method entirely. One does not direct the mind but <em>releases</em> it—allowing it to wander freely in outer objects (sights, sounds) or inner states (joy, sorrow, desire). In that uncontrolled flow, the practitioner recognizes: every movement of mind arises within the one reality. Since Śiva is <em>vyāpaka</em> (all-pervading), there is no place the mind can go that is not already His state (<em>śivāvasthā</em>). This is pure śāmbhavopāya: effortless, non-dual recognition.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses present two complementary doorways to recognition—one through depth, the other through breadth—but both reveal the same truth: awareness is never separate from what it perceives.</p>
<p>In verse 115, the well is not a technique but a threshold where ordinary perception naturally collapses. When standing at the edge of boundless depth, awareness does not "gaze downward" as an act of will; it is undone by the sheer immensity before it. The abyss does not reflect back an image—it swallows the very sense of being a separate observer. What dissolves is not "mind" as an object to be destroyed, but the contraction that maintained the illusion of a center looking "out there" at something separate. This dissolution is instantaneous not because of effort, but because awareness recognizes itself as the depth. The body may stand at the well's edge, but the one who believed in edges has vanished into the boundless.</p>
<p>Verse 116 completes this realization by addressing the apparent opposite: when awareness flows rather than sinks. The phrase "wherever the mind goes" risks implying a journey from here to there. But when awareness is fully present—whether in the marketplace or in the depths of emotion—there is no "it" traveling anywhere. The mind does not "go" to Śiva's state; it is already saturated with that state. When thoughts arise about outer objects or inner feelings, they are not visitors arriving at awareness; they are ripples appearing within the ocean of what is already whole.</p>
<p>This is not freedom for the mind, but freedom from the mind as a separate entity. Śiva is not "all-pervading" as a quality he possesses—he is pervasion itself. The question "where else could it go?" does not seek an answer; it dissolves the questioner. In that dissolution, even the distinction between well-gazing and mind-wandering collapses. The abyss and the marketplace are equally mirrors of the one reality that never departed. All apparent movement happens within what never moved; all apparent depth exists within what was always whole.</p>
<p>The true teaching is not about gazing or releasing, but about seeing through the veil of separation that made gazing and releasing seem necessary. When standing at the well's edge or lost in thought, what remains when all technique falls away is not an experience to have, but the ever-present ground in which all experiences arise and dissolve—unbroken, unchanging, and already free.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>117</h1>
<h3>Revealed Through Any Sense Channel</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यत्र यत्राक्षमार्गेण चैतन्यं व्यज्यते विभोः।<br>
तस्य तन्मात्रधर्मित्वाच्चिल्लयाद्भरितात्मता॥११७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yatra yatrākṣa-mārgeṇa caitanyaṃ vyajyate vibhoḥ |<br>
tasya tan-mātra-dharmitvāc cit-layād bharitātmatā ||117||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Wherever, through any sense channel,<br />
the Consciousness of the All-Pervading One<br />
is revealed—<br />
since its essence is That alone,<br />
dissolving into pure Awareness,<br />
one becomes utterly full.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals that every sense perception is a direct manifestation of divine Consciousness. Whether seeing form, hearing sound, or feeling touch, the awareness that <em>knows</em> the object is not personal—it is the very essence of the Lord (<em>vibhoḥ caitanyam</em>).</p>
<p>The practitioner does not withdraw from perception, but dives into its source. At the very moment a pencil is seen or a sound is heard, one recognizes: <em>This knowing itself is Śiva.</em> Since the sense organ and its object are both expressions of the same Consciousness (<em>tan-mātra-dharmitvāt</em>), absorption (<em>cit-layāt</em>) into that knowing—not the known—leads to <em>bharitātmatā</em>: the state of being utterly full, without lack or boundary.</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya: using the immediacy of sensory experience as a portal to non-dual recognition. The senses are not obstacles; they are eyes of the absolute looking upon itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 117 transforms ordinary perception into revelation. It does not ask you to close your eyes or silence sound. It says: <em>Look—but see the seeing. Listen—but hear the hearing.</em></p>
<p>The “sense channel” (<em>akṣa-mārgeṇa</em>) is any aperture of awareness: the eye seeing color, the ear hearing music, the skin feeling wind. In each case, the object is transient, but the <em>awareness of</em> the object is eternal. That awareness is not yours—it is Bhairava’s, appearing as yours.</p>
<p>“Dissolving into pure Awareness” (<em>cit-layāt</em>) means ceasing to identify with the content of experience and resting as the space that holds it. When you see a flower, don’t linger on its petals—dive into the luminosity that allows it to be seen. In that dive, the boundary between seer and seen evaporates, and what remains is not emptiness, but <em>bharitātmatā</em>: the fullness of being that needs nothing added.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not withdrawal from the world, but total immersion in its true nature. Every sight, sound, and sensation becomes a doorway—not because it is sacred, but because it is transparent to the one reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>118</h1>
<h3>Ordinary Moments As Gateways</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
क्षुताद्यन्ते भये शोके गह्वरे वा रणद्रुते।<br>
कुतूहले क्षुधाद्यन्ते ब्रह्मसत्ता समीपगः॥११८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kṣutādyante bhaye śoke gahvare vā raṇa-dṛte |<br>
kutūhale kṣudhādyante brahma-sattā samīpagaḥ ||118||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>At the beginning and end of sneezing, in a state of fear or sorrow,<br />
(standing) on top of an abyss or while fleeing from a battlefield,<br />
at the moment of intense curiosity, at the beginning or end of hunger—<br />
in such moments, the reality of Brahman is immediately present.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse points to sudden, involuntary thresholds where ordinary mind halts and pure awareness shines through. These are not chosen states, but moments of extreme physiological or emotional intensity—sneezing, terror, despair, disorientation, flight, hunger, or wonder—where the habitual flow of thought is abruptly suspended.</p>
<p>In that split-second gap—between the gasp before a sneeze and its release, between the surge of fear and its interpretation—the sense of “I” vanishes. In that vanishing, <em>brahma-sattā</em>—the living presence of Brahman—is not attained, but <em>recognized as already present</em>.</p>
<p>Because these moments arise spontaneously, without method or support, they belong to śāmbhavopāya. The practitioner does not create these states, but learns to <em>recognize</em> them as gateways. When fear grips, don’t resist—<em>see the space within the grip</em>. When curiosity flares, don’t chase the object—<em>rest in the flare itself</em>.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 118 reveals that liberation is not confined to meditation cushions—it erupts in life’s raw edges. The “beginning and end of sneezing” (<em>kṣutādyante</em>) is a microcosm: a natural rupture in breath and thought where awareness flashes unmediated. Similarly, in terror—like the dread of a falling sky lab—the mind stops projecting into the future and is thrust into the naked now.</p>
<p>“On the edge of an abyss” (<em>gahvare</em>) or “fleeing battle” (<em>raṇa-dṛte</em>) are moments of total vulnerability, where control collapses. In that collapse, the illusion of a separate self—the one who plans, fears, and survives—dissolves. What remains is not absence, but the fullness of being prior to story.</p>
<p>“Burning curiosity” (<em>kutūhale</em>) is especially potent. It is not intellectual inquiry, but the mind’s sudden halt before mystery—<em>What is this?</em>—before it races to answer. In that suspension, awareness is pure, uncolored by knowing.</p>
<p>“Brahman draws near” (<em>brahma-sattā samīpagaḥ</em>) does not mean grace descends. It means the veil thins. These moments are not “spiritual”—they are human. And in their raw immediacy, the door to recognition swings open.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not a technique to practice, but an invitation to <em>wake up at the edges</em>. In life’s ruptures—sneeze, terror, hunger, wonder—the ordinary mind cracks—and through that crack, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the stillness within the storm.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>119</h1>
<h3>Recalled Places</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
वस्तुषु स्मर्यमाणेषु दृष्टे देशे मनस्त्यजेत्।<br>
स्वशरीरं निराधारं कृत्वा प्रसरति प्रभुः॥११९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vastuṣu smaryamāṇeṣu dṛṣṭe deśe manas-tyajet |<br>
sva-śarīraṃ nirādhāraṃ kṛtvā prasarati prabhuḥ ||119||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When recalling past objects,<br />
fix the mind fully on that seen place—<br />
render the present body supportless,<br />
and the Lord unfolds.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses vivid recollection as a gateway to presence. One does not merely remember a past event, but fully re-inhabits it—reconstructing the sights, sounds, and atmosphere of that time and place (<em>dṛṣṭe deśe</em>) with such intensity that attention is wholly withdrawn from the present body and its current location.</p>
<p>In that total immersion in the past, the present body becomes <em>nirādhāra</em>—devoid of support, as if it no longer exists. The sense of “I am here now” dissolves, and with it, the usual anchors of identity. In that groundlessness, <em>prabhuḥ prasarati</em>—the Lord, the unbounded awareness that underlies all experience, spontaneously unfolds.</p>
<p>Though it uses memory as a support (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the present body is rendered unreal through total mental absorption, the practice reveals the timeless nature of consciousness beyond past and present.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 119 reveals liberation through total immersion in remembered experience. It does not ask you to forget the past, but to <em>re-enter it so completely</em> that present awareness dissolves into that moment. This is not daydreaming; it is deliberate re-creation of sensory and spatial details—like standing again in the garden of childhood or the street of a past city.</p>
<p>When awareness flows entirely into that remembered space (<em>manas-tyajet</em>—"release the mind into it"), the present body becomes <em>nirādhāra</em>: without support, like a phantom. The sense of "I am here now" dissolves. In that dissolution, what expands (<em>prasarati</em>) is not a new state, but recognition of the timeless ground beneath all experience—where past, present and future arise as transient patterns in unchanging awareness.</p>
<p>This practice is not about time travel, but about seeing through time itself. When the knot of linear perception loosens in total recollection, what remains is not memory, but the ever-present awareness in which all moments appear and vanish—unbroken.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>120</h1>
<h3>Withdrawing Gaze From Object</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
क्वचिद्वस्तुनि विन्यस्य सनैर्दृष्टिं निवर्तयेत्।<br>
तज्ज्ञानं चित्तसहितं देवि शून्यालयो भवेत्॥१२०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kvacid-vastuni vinyasya sanair dṛṣṭiṃ nivartayet |<br>
taj-jñānaṃ citta-sahitaṃ devi śūnyālayo bhavet ||120||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Fix your gaze on any object,<br />
then slowly withdraw the seeing—<br />
along with the knowing of it,<br />
and the mind that holds it.<br />
O Goddess, thus you become<br />
the abode of the void.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana traces the reverse path of perception. One begins by fixing the gaze steadily on a chosen object (<em>kvacid vastuni</em>). Then, with utmost slowness (<em>sanaiḥ</em>), one withdraws attention—not abruptly, but as if gently retracting a thread—first from the object’s form, then from the mental impression of it (<em>taj-jñānaṃ</em>), and finally from the very sense of “I am seeing” (<em>citta-sahitam</em>).</p>
<p>In this inward retraction, awareness is not scattered but gathered. As the object fades and the knower dissolves, what remains is not absence, but <em>śūnyālaya</em>—the abode of the void: the open, luminous space of pure subjectivity, uncolored by content.</p>
<p>Since it uses a physical object as the starting point and requires deliberate withdrawal, this belongs to śāktopāya. Yet its culmination—the dissolution of subject and object—is the threshold of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 120 is the art of unseeing. It does not deny the world, but reveals it as a temporary knot in awareness. The instruction “slowly withdraw the gaze” is not physical—it is the softening of attention’s grip. You do not look away; you let the act of seeing unravel from within.</p>
<p>“Withdraw the knowing of the object along with the mind” means dissolving the entire perceptual chain: the sight (form), the memory (name), and the sense of ownership (“my perception”). Like a wave sinking back into ocean, each layer returns to its source.</p>
<p>“The abode of the void” (<em>śūnyālaya</em>) is not emptiness as lack, but as fullness without boundary. It is the space where all objects arise and vanish—never stained by their presence, never diminished by their absence.</p>
<p>This practice can be done with anything—a flower, a flame, a stone. The object is irrelevant; the movement of awareness is everything. In the gentle release of seeing, the seer vanishes—and what remains is Bhairava: the silent, boundless ground of all appearance.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>121</h1>
<h3>Detached Devotion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
भक्त्युद्रेकाद्विरक्तस्य यदृशी जायते मतिः।<br>
सा शक्तिः संकरी नित्यं भावयेत्तां ततः शिवः॥१२१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
bhakty-udrekād viraktasya yadṛśī jāyate matiḥ |<br>
sā śaktiḥ saṃkarī nityaṃ bhāvayet tāṃ tataḥ śivaḥ ||121||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>In one who is detached,<br />
the insight born of intense devotion—<br />
that very awareness is Śaṅkara’s eternal Power.<br />
Meditate on that,<br />
and thereby become Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals that supreme devotion (<em>bhakti-udreka</em>)—when utterly purified by detachment (<em>viraktasya</em>)—is not emotional longing, but the spontaneous upsurge of divine Energy itself. In such a state, the intellect (<em>matiḥ</em>) is no longer a faculty of discrimination, but a luminous mirror of pure awareness.</p>
<p>This awakened insight is not personal; it is <em>sā śaktiḥ saṃkarī</em>—the very Power of Śaṅkara (Śiva), self-luminous and unconditioned. The practitioner does not generate this state; it arises when worldly attachment falls away and love for the absolute becomes all-consuming. One then <em>bhāvayet tām</em>—holds that insight as the sole reality—and in that holding, <em>tataḥ śivaḥ</em>, becomes Śiva.</p>
<p>Though it arises from devotion (often associated with dualistic paths), the moment detachment is total and insight is non-conceptual, it becomes śāmbhavopāya: the direct, effortless abidance in the Self.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse describes a precise threshold where emotion transforms into insight. The devotion (bhakti-udrekā) referenced is not ordinary religious sentiment, but an all-consuming intensity that—only when paired with complete detachment (viraktasya)—gives rise to a particular quality of awareness (matiḥ). This awareness is not merely emotional warmth, but the very power (śakti) of Śaṅkara himself.</p>
<p>Crucially, Lakshmanjoo positions this practice within a sequence of refinement. Detached devotion is not an end state, but a transitional condition where the heart's intensity begins to dismantle the structures of conceptual thinking. The resulting insight is not a merger of bhakti and jñāna, but bhakti's natural culmination when purified beyond its dualistic nature.</p>
<p>When Lakshmanjoo notes this becomes śāmbhavopāya "the moment detachment is total and insight is non-conceptual," he maintains important distinctions. Traditional bhakti begins in duality—the devotee and the Beloved are distinct. Only through relentless refinement does this path cross into non-dual recognition. The verse does not collapse devotion into wisdom; it shows devotion's ultimate refinement yields wisdom.</p>
<p>The phrase "meditate on that" (bhāvayet tām) is not identification with emotion. It is the conscious stabilization of awareness in the clarity that emerges when attachment dissolves. This is not the heart overtaking the mind, but the heart refining the mind until both subside into their source. What arises is not "love as reality," but reality revealed through the alchemy of purified love.</p>
<p>This nuance matters: the verse does not spiritualize ordinary devotion. It honors its transformative potential while maintaining the rigor that distinguishes authentic path from mere sentiment. Only when devotion burns away all residues of self-reference does it become the doorway to Śiva. Until then, it remains a powerful, but provisional, support. The culmination is not "love becoming Bhairava," but love's complete surrender revealing what was always present beneath the devotion itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>122</h1>
<h3>Dissolution Of Previous Objects</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
वस्त्वन्तरे वेद्यमाने सनैर्वस्तुषु शून्यता।<br>
तमेव मनसा ध्यात्वा विदितोऽपि प्रशाम्यति॥१२२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vastv-antare vedyamāne sanair vastuṣu śūnyatā |<br>
tam eva manasā dhyātvā vidito ’pi praśāmyati ||122||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>When one object is perceived,<br />
all others gradually dissolve into void.<br />
Meditating on that emptiness in the mind,<br />
even while perception continues,<br />
peace arises.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana works through the natural sequence of perception. As one object is actively perceived (<em>vastv-antare vedyamāne</em>—“another object being known”), all previously perceived objects recede into the background. The practitioner does not suppress this process but attends to the <em>dissolution itself</em>—the way each object, once no longer the focus, becomes insubstantial (<em>sanair vastuṣu śūnyatā</em>).</p>
<p>By turning awareness toward this vanishing (<em>tam eva manasā dhyātvā</em>), the mind ceases to cling to any form, past or present. Even while the current object remains in view, the field of perception is seen as permeated by emptiness. In that recognition, <em>vidito ’pi praśāmyati</em>—“even while knowing, one is appeased”—the agitation of cognition subsides, and peace (<em>praśānti</em>) dawns not as absence of perception, but as its transparent ground.</p>
<p>This belongs to śāktopāya: it uses the ordinary flow of attention as support, yet reveals the non-dual space within which all objects arise and vanish.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 122 reveals that emptiness is not beyond perception—it is perception’s true nature. When you look at a tree, the book you held a moment ago doesn’t vanish; it simply ceases to demand attention. In that fading, it becomes <em>śūnya</em>: not nonexistent, but ungraspable, like a dream remembered upon waking.</p>
<p>The key is <em>not following the new object</em>, but <em>resting in the space left by the old</em>. As each perception yields to the next, a trail of emptiness is revealed. By meditating on this trail—not as void in opposition to form, but as form’s self-release—the mind stops accumulating experience.</p>
<p>“Even while knowing, one is appeased” (<em>vidito ’pi praśāmyati</em>) is the hallmark of the practice. The world continues to appear, but it no longer binds. You see the pencil, yet know it as emptiness appearing as pencil. You hear sound, yet rest in the silence that holds it.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not withdrawal, but deconstruction through attention. In the natural rhythm of perception—now this, now that—the yogi discovers that all objects are passing shadows on the screen of awareness. And in that seeing, Bhairava is not beyond the screen. He is the light itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>123</h1>
<h3>Beyond Purity And Impurity</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
किंचिज्ज्ञैर्या स्मृता शुद्धिः सा शुद्धिः संभुदर्शने।<br>
न शुचिर्नाशुचिस्तस्मान्निर्विकल्पः सुखी भवेत्॥१२३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
kiṃcid-jñair yā smṛtā śuddhiḥ sā śuddhiḥ saṃbhu-darśane |<br>
na śucir nāśucis tasmān nirvikalpaḥ sukhī bhavet ||123||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The purity proclaimed by those of limited understanding<br />
is not purity in the vision of Saṃbhu.<br />
Here, nothing is pure, nothing impure—<br />
thus, free from such thoughts, one abides in bliss.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse dismantles the dualistic framework of purity and impurity (<em>śuci/āśuci</em>) imposed by conventional spiritual systems. The <em>kiṃcid-jñaiḥ</em>—“those of partial knowledge”—prescribe rituals, avoidances, and moral codes based on the belief that certain acts, substances, or states defile while others sanctify.</p>
<p>But in the non-dual vision of Saṃbhu (Śiva), all such distinctions are <em>vikalpa</em>—mental constructs with no basis in reality. The absolute is beyond contamination and sanctification; it pervades all equally. To cling to ideas of purity is to fragment the One.</p>
<p>Thus, the practitioner who sees through this duality—<em>nirvikalpaḥ</em>, free from conceptual division—dwells in <em>sukha</em>, the natural bliss of awakening. This is śāktopāya, for it uses discernment (<em>viveka</em>) to dissolve the very framework of discernment itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 1 23 is a radical declaration of freedom. It does not say “everything is pure,” but “the categories of pure and impure do not apply.” The absolute is not sanctified by ritual or stained by transgression—it is the unchanging ground in which both arise.</p>
<p>This is not license for indulgence, but liberation from judgment. The yogi eats, speaks, and acts without inner division—no “clean” or “unclean,” only presence. A funeral pyre and a temple altar are equally luminous; excrement and nectar are equally appearances of Śakti.</p>
<p>“Free from such thoughts” (<em>nirvikalpaḥ</em>) means the mind no longer fragments reality into opposites. In that non-dual seeing, action flows spontaneously from wisdom, not from fear of contamination or hope for reward.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not rejection of ethics, but transcendence of duality. When the last trace of “I must purify myself” vanishes, what remains is not moral chaos, but the effortless harmony of one who has recognized: <em>All is Bhairava.</em></p>
</div>

---
<h1>124</h1>
<h3>Even In Ordinary Beings</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सर्वत्र भैरवो भावः सामान्येष्वपि गोचरः।<br>
न च तद्व्यतिरेकेण परोऽस्त्यद्वया गतिः॥१२४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
sarvatra bhairavo bhāvaḥ sāmānyeṣv api gocaraḥ |<br>
na ca tad-vyatirekeṇa paro ’sty advayā gatiḥ ||124||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The state of Bhairava is present everywhere—<br />
even in ordinary beings it is directly perceived.<br />
Nothing exists apart from That;<br />
thus, the non-dual path is revealed.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana affirms that Bhairava is not hidden in temples or attainable only by sages. His presence (<em>bhāvaḥ</em>) is <em>gocaraḥ</em>—immediately evident—even in the mundane actions of ordinary people (<em>sāmānyeṣu</em>): in their speech, in their labor, in their daily exchanges. When a woman draws water from a well, when two friends meet on the street, when a merchant weighs goods—each act unfolds within the luminous field of divine awareness.</p>
<p>The practitioner recognizes: <em>There is no reality apart from this.</em> (<em>na ca tad-vyatirekeṇa paraḥ asti</em>). The very notion of separation—of “world” versus “God”—is illusion. To see this is to walk the <em>advayā gatiḥ</em>, the non-dual path, where every being, every act, is recognized as the living expression of Śiva.</p>
<p>Though it begins with discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment this seeing becomes total, duality collapses—not as philosophy, but as lived truth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 124 shatters the hierarchy of sacred and profane. It does not say the ordinary <em>leads to</em> the divine; it declares the ordinary <em>is</em> the divine in motion. The Vedāntic verse cited—<em>“God is seen by all”</em>—echoes this: the water-carrier knows Bhairava not through scripture, but through the weight of the vessel in her hands, the rhythm of her steps, the sky above.</p>
<p>“Directly perceived” (<em>gocaraḥ</em>) is precise. Bhairava is not inferred; He is the very space in which perception occurs. The market, the field, the kitchen—all are His body. To miss this is not ignorance of doctrine, but inattention to life itself.</p>
<p>“Nothing exists apart from That” is the final undoing of otherness. When you see a stranger’s face, you do not see “other”—you see Bhairava appearing as stranger. When you hear gossip, you do not hear distraction—you hear the absolute vibrating as sound.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not meditation apart from life, but life as meditation. In the unguarded moment—when a child laughs, when a bell rings, when breath flows—the door is open. And through it, Bhairava is not found elsewhere. He is the ordinary, seen with extraordinary clarity.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>125</h1>
<h3>Equanimity</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
समः शत्रौ च मित्रे च समो मानावमानयोः।<br>
ब्रह्मणः परिपूर्णत्वादिति ज्ञात्वा सुखी भवेत्॥१२५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
samaḥ śatrau ca mitre ca samo mānāvamānayoḥ |<br>
brahmaṇaḥ paripūrṇatvād iti jñātvā sukhī bhavet ||125||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Be the same toward friend and foe,<br />
unmoved by honor or disgrace.<br />
Knowing that Brahman is ever full,<br />
one abides in bliss.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals equanimity not as emotional suppression, but as the natural expression of non-dual realization. The practitioner remains <em>samaḥ</em>—undisturbed, unwavering—whether facing love or hatred, praise or blame. This is not indifference, but the recognition that all dualities arise within the one reality: <em>brahmaṇaḥ paripūrṇatvāt</em>, “because Brahman is ever full.”</p>
<p>When fulfillment is not contingent on external conditions, the mind no longer contracts in aversion or expands in attachment. The enemy’s arrow and the friend’s embrace are both seen as ripples in the same ocean of awareness. In that seeing, <em>sukhī bhavet</em>—one dwells in spontaneous joy, untouched by circumstance.</p>
<p>This belongs to śāktopāya: the power of discernment (<em>jñātvā</em>) alone dissolves the illusion that happiness depends on the world’s approval.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse does not ask you to <em>pretend</em> indifference, but to <em>see through</em> duality. The “fullness of Brahman” (<em>paripūrṇatvam</em>) is not a state to achieve—it is the ground of being, always already whole.</p>
<p>When you know this, honor cannot inflate you, nor disgrace diminish you. The cross and the crown are equally luminous. Utpaladeva’s verse—<em>“If victorious, they laugh; if defeated, they laugh”</em>—captures this freedom: devotion so complete that it transmutes all experience into celebration.</p>
<p>“Be the same” (<em>samaḥ</em>) is not stoicism. It is the relaxation of the ego’s desperate need to control outcomes. The yogi acts with full engagement—defending, loving, working—but without inner division. The arrow comes; awareness holds it as play. The embrace comes; awareness holds it as play.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not withdrawal from relationship, but total immersion without ownership. In the fire of duality, the diamond of non-duality shines—not because conditions are perfect, but because perfection was never absent. And in that knowing, Bhairava is not beyond the battlefield or the banquet. He is the unshaken heart of both.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>126</h1>
<h3>Attachment And Aversion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
न द्वेषं भावयेत्क्वापि न रागं भावयेत्क्वचित्।<br>
रागद्वेषविनिर्मुक्तौ मध्ये ब्रह्म प्रसर्पति॥१२६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
na dveṣaṃ bhāvayet kvāpi na rāgaṃ bhāvayet kvacit |<br>
rāga-dveṣa-vinirmuktau madhye brahma prasarpati ||126||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Cultivate neither hatred toward anyone,<br />
nor attachment to anything.<br />
Free from both aversion and clinging,<br />
in the center, Brahman unfolds.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana prescribes a middle path that transcends the dual impulses of <em>rāga</em> (attachment) and <em>dveṣa</em> (aversion). The practitioner does not suppress emotion, but refuses to feed it with identification. One does not deny the arising of attraction or repulsion, but does not <em>bhāvayet</em>—nourish, dwell in, or act from—that impulse.</p>
<p>In that non-identification, one abides <em>madhye</em>—in the neutral center between the opposing pulls of like and dislike. This is not indifference, but the space of pure awareness where emotions arise and pass without leaving residue. In that space, <em>brahma prasarpati</em>—Brahman, the boundless reality, spontaneously expands and reveals itself.</p>
<p>Though it points to the immediacy of the center, the instruction to “not cultivate” still involves discernment and restraint, placing it in śāktopāya. True śāmbhavopāya would be the spontaneous recognition of the center without any injunction.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 126 is not moral instruction, but ontological clarity. <em>Rāga</em> and <em>dveṣa</em> are not sins to avoid; they are contractions of awareness that obscure the whole. When you cling to a friend, you fragment reality into “mine” and “not mine.” When you reject an enemy, you deny the unity of being.</p>
<p>“Free from both” (<em>rāga-dveṣa-vinirmuktau</em>) means the mind no longer leans. It is not that feelings cease—the heart may still flutter or ache—but the inner narrative of “I want this” or “I hate that” dissolves. In that dissolution, the center (<em>madhye</em>) is not a point between two poles; it is the ground where poles never arose.</p>
<p>“Brahman unfolds” (<em>brahma prasarpati</em>) is not an event. It is the natural state when the mind stops splitting the world. In that wholeness, even hatred and love are seen as waves of the same ocean—energies of Śakti playing within Śiva.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about becoming emotionless. It is about becoming so utterly present that emotion no longer binds. And in that freedom, Bhairava is not beyond duality. He is the silent center in which all dualities arise, play, and vanish.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>127</h1>
<h3>Contemplating The Unknowable</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यदवेद्यं यदग्राह्यं यच्छून्यं यदभावगम्।<br>
तत्सर्वं भैरवं भाव्यं तदन्ते बोधसंभवः॥१२७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yad avedyaṃ yad agrāhyaṃ ya cchūnyaṃ yad abhāvagam |<br>
tat sarvaṃ bhairavaṃ bhāvyaṃ tad ante bodha-sambhavaḥ ||127||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>That which is unknowable,<br />
ungraspable,<br />
void,<br />
and even beyond non-existence—<br />
contemplate all this as Bhairava.<br />
At its culmination,<br />
awakening dawns.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse points directly to śāmbhavopāya—the highest means, where no object, no support, and no mental operation remains. One is to contemplate (<em>bhāvyam</em>) not on forms, not on qualities, but on that which lies beyond all designation: <em>avedya</em> (unknowable as object), <em>agrāhya</em> (ungraspable by any faculty), <em>śūnya</em> (void of all content), and <em>abhāvagam</em> (even beyond the notion of non-existence).</p>
<p>This is not negation, but the recognition of the ground prior to all that can be known or unknown. Since there is no object to hold, no attribute to affirm or deny, the mind has nothing to do—<em>it simply ceases</em>. In that cessation, <em>bodha-sambhavaḥ</em>—awakening as spontaneous self-recognition—arises not as an event in time, but as the ever-present reality of Bhairava.</p>
<p>This is pure śāmbhavopāya: the path of immediacy, where even the trace of “meditation” dissolves into the self-luminous silence that is one’s true nature.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 127 is the razor’s edge of non-duality. It does not ask you to meditate <em>on</em> emptiness, but to rest where emptiness itself is seen as provisional. The four terms map the collapse of all reference points:<br />
- <strong>Avedya</strong>: Not an object of knowledge—not hidden, but prior to the subject-object split.<br />
- <strong>Agrāhya</strong>: Not perceivable—not because it is subtle, but because perception arises within it.<br />
- <strong>Śūnya</strong>: Void—not as absence, but as the open space in which presence and absence play.<br />
- <strong>Abhāvagam</strong>: Beyond non-existence—not annihilation, but the ground in which even “nothing” appears as concept.</p>
<p>To “contemplate all this as Bhairava” is not to imagine a void, but to <em>be</em> the unlocatable space in which all imagining arises. The instruction “see the absence of the spectacles” is precise: don’t see the object—see the <em>not-object</em>, the luminous openness that holds the appearance without being stained by it.</p>
<p>“At its culmination” (<em>tad ante</em>) does not imply progress. It is the moment attention stops seeking—even seeking the void—and collapses into itself. In that collapse, “awakening” (<em>bodha-sambhavaḥ</em>) is not new knowledge. It is the self-evident fact that you were never in ignorance.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not a practice to perform, but a mirror held up to the mind. When you look for the unknowable, you find only the looker—and in that finding, the looker vanishes. What remains is Bhairava: not as other, but as your own unbroken heart.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>128</h1>
<h3>Spaceless Space</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
नित्ये निराश्रये शून्ये व्यापके कलनोज्झिते।<br>
बाह्याकाशे मनः कृत्वा निराकाशं समाविशेत्॥१२८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nitye nirāśraye śūnye vyāpake kalanojjhite |<br>
bāhyākāśe manaḥ kṛtvā nirākāśaṃ samāviśet ||128||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Fix the mind on the outer sky—<br />
eternal, supportless, empty,<br />
all-pervading, beyond all limitation—<br />
and enter the spaceless space.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana begins with śāktopāya and culminates in śāmbhavopāya. One gazes at the external sky (<em>bāhyākāśa</em>)—not as a colored dome, but as a symbol of boundlessness: <em>nitya</em> (eternal), <em>nirāśraya</em> (without support), <em>śūnya</em> (void of content), <em>vyāpaka</em> (pervading all), and <em>kalana-ujjhita</em> (free from all conceptual division).</p>
<p>Initially, the sky serves as a support—a tangible image of openness. But as attention deepens, even the subtle perception of “blueness” or “vastness” dissolves. The sky is no longer an object “out there,” but the mirror of one’s own nature. In that dissolution, one enters <em>nirākāśa</em>—the spaceless space, the non-dual reality beyond all spatial metaphor.</p>
<p>This final absorption (<em>samāviśet</em>) is śāmbhavopāya: the spontaneous recognition that awareness was never confined, never localized, never other than the infinite.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This practice reveals the sky not as symbol but as mirror. When you look at the vast expanse overhead—not as object but as pure seeing—the boundary between inner and outer dissolves not as achievement, but as recognition of what has always been so. The five descriptors are not attributes to mentally superimpose, but signposts pointing back to the awareness that perceives them.</p>
<p>The sky is not "eternal" as a quality it possesses, but reveals the timeless nature of seeing itself. It is not "supportless" as a characteristic, but mirrors how awareness needs no foundation. When you stop conceptualizing the blue dome as "something out there," the sky becomes transparent to the space within which both seer and seen arise.</p>
<p>In that transparency, "entering the spaceless space" is not movement toward a destination. There is no journey from the outer sky to inner emptiness. When conceptual overlay falls away, the sky is recognized not as object, but as the open field of awareness itself—where the one who gazes and the expanse gazed upon collapse into a single, undivided reality.</p>
<p>What we call "nirākāśa" is not a state achieved after contemplating the sky. It is the collapse of the very notion that there was ever separation to begin with. The sky doesn't lead to emptiness—it reveals that emptiness was never absent, never separate, never other than the luminous ground of all appearing.</p>
<p>In this recognition, there is no "entering," no crossing thresholds, no attainment of what was absent. There is only the end of the illusion that awareness was ever confined to a center looking outward. When that illusion dissolves, the sky is not seen as Brahman—it is seen as the mirror in which Brahman recognizes itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>129</h1>
<h3>Non-Abidance As Path</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यत्र यत्र मनो याति तत्तत्तेनैव तत्क्षणम्।<br>
परित्यज्यानवस्थित्या निस्तरङ्गस्ततो भवेत्॥१२९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yatra yatra mano yāti tat tat tenaiva tat-kṣaṇam |<br>
parityajyānavasthitayā nis-taraṅgas tato bhavet ||129||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Wherever the mind goes,<br />
at that very instant,<br />
let it go—and do not let it rest.<br />
By this constant non-abidance,<br />
one becomes waveless.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses dynamic non-attachment as a path to stillness. The practitioner allows the mind complete freedom to wander—toward sights, memories, desires—but the moment it alights on any object, one <em>parityajya</em>—releases it immediately, without allowing fixation. This is not suppression, but a gentle, vigilant redirection: not “stop thinking,” but “don’t settle.”</p>
<p>Through this ceaseless letting-go (<em>anavasthitayā</em>), the mind is denied every foothold. Without support, it cannot generate the “waves” (<em>taraṅga</em>) of thought, emotion, or identification. In that groundlessness, <em>nis-taraṅgaḥ</em>—the waveless state of pure awareness—naturally dawns.</p>
<p>Though it begins with an act of discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment the mind becomes utterly supportless, the method reveals the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya: the natural abidance in the still ocean beneath all ripples.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 129 is the art of conscious restlessness. It does not still the mind by force, but by <em>refusing to let it land</em>. Like a bird denied the branch, the mind eventually tires of flight and dissolves into the sky.</p>
<p>The key is <em>timing</em>: “at that very instant” (<em>tat-kṣaṇam</em>). Not after analysis, not before impulse—but in the split second the mind grasps an object, release it. See a flower? Don’t linger—let it go. Remember a song? Don’t hum—let it go. This is not distraction, but de-identification in real time.</p>
<p>“Waveless” (<em>nis-taraṅgaḥ</em>) is not absence of movement, but the end of turbulence. Thoughts may still arise, but they leave no wake—no residue of “me” and “mine.” The mind becomes like a mirror: images pass through, but nothing sticks.</p>
<p>This practice is especially potent because it meets the mind as it is—fickle, roaming, restless—and turns its very nature into the path. In the constant motion of non-abidance, the illusion of a fixed self evaporates.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not about controlling the mind, but about outmaneuvering it. In the space between one object and the next, Bhairava is not found. He is the space itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>130</h1>
<h3>Unceasing Utterance</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
भया सर्वं रवयति सर्वगो व्यापकोऽखिले।<br>
इति भैरवशब्दस्य सन्ततोच्चारणाच्छिवः॥१३०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
bhayā sarvaṃ ravayati sarvago vyāpako'khile |<br>
iti bhairava-śabdasya santatoccaranāc chivaḥ ||130||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Bhairava—He who, through fear,<br />
makes all cry out,<br />
who is present in all as the All-Pervader—<br />
by unceasing utterance of this name,<br />
one becomes Śiva.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals the power of the sacred syllable <em>Bhairava</em> through its esoteric etymology. The name is not merely a label, but a vibrational key:<br />
- <strong>Bha</strong> signifies <em>bhaya</em>—primordial fear, the shock of confrontation with the unknown;<br />
- <strong>Ra</strong> signifies <em>rava</em>—the cry or resonance that arises from that fear, the raw outpouring of being;<br />
- <strong>Va</strong> signifies <em>vyāpaka</em>—the all-pervading reality that is both the source and substance of that cry.</p>
<p>Thus, Bhairava is the divine presence that <em>elicits</em> the cry through terror and <em>is</em> the cry itself—simultaneously the threat and the refuge. When one recites this name continuously (<em>santatoccaranāt</em>), not as mechanical repetition but as an invocation embodying its full meaning, the practitioner does not “attain” Bhairava—he <em>becomes</em> Śiva, for the name and its essence are one.</p>
<p>This belongs to śāktopāya: the power of the mantra, understood and intoned with full awareness, awakens the reality it signifies.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 130 transforms fear from bondage into awakening. The cry of terror—“Bhairava!”—is not a plea to a distant god, but the spontaneous recognition that the source of fear <em>is</em> the absolute itself. In extreme crisis, when the mind shatters and the cry erupts unbidden, duality collapses: the one who fears and the one who is feared are revealed as one.</p>
<p>“Unceasing utterance” (<em>santatoccaranāt</em>) does not mean endless vocalization. It is the continuous resonance of that primal cry within awareness—holding the tension of <em>bhaya</em> and <em>vyāpaka</em> together until the knot of separation burns away. When you understand that the scream <em>is</em> the silence that holds it, fear transmutes into awe.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not about calming fear, but diving into its core. In the heart of terror, Bhairava is not found as protector. He is recognized as the very ground of the trembling—a presence so total that even fear becomes a hymn. And in that recognition, the one who cried vanishes—and only Śiva remains.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>131</h1>
<h3>Self-Referential Thoughts</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अहं ममेदमित्यादि प्रतिपत्तिप्रसङ्गतः।<br>
निराधारे मनो याति तद्ध्यानप्रेरणाच्छमिः॥१३१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ahaṃ mam-edam ityādi pratipatti-prasaṅgataḥ |<br>
nirādhāre mano yāti tad-dhyāna-preraṇāc chamiḥ ||131||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>In the very midst of assertions like<br />
“I am,” “this is mine,” and so on,<br />
the mind turns toward the supportless.<br />
Spontaneously inspired by this,<br />
one becomes utterly peaceful.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana works within the ordinary stream of self-referential thought. As the mind naturally engages in worldly relationships—“I am Lakshmanjoo,” “this is my disciple,” “that is my son”—one does not reject these thoughts, but uses them as a trigger for insight.</p>
<p>In the very act of saying “I am this” or “this is mine,” one notices the <em>groundlessness</em> of such claims. The “I” has no substance; the “mine” has no inherent owner. In that recognition, the mind (<em>manas</em>) naturally turns toward <em>nirādhāra</em>—the supportless reality beyond all identification.</p>
<p>This shift is not forced; it arises <em>tad-dhyāna-preraṇāt</em>—spontaneously, as if inspired by the meditation itself. In that unforced turning, <em>śamiḥ</em>—perfect peace—dawns, not as absence of thought, but as the natural clarity of non-attachment.</p>
<p>Though it begins with attention to ordinary mental activity (śāktopāya), the moment the mind rests in the supportless, it reveals the immediacy of śāmbhavopāaya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 131 is a masterstroke of integration: liberation is found not by silencing the ego, but by seeing through it in real time. The phrases “I am,” “this is mine” are not errors to correct—they are doorways.</p>
