<p>When you say “I am a teacher,” notice: who is this “I”? When you think “this is my body,” feel: where is the boundary between “mine” and “not mine”? The moment you look, the claim dissolves into open awareness.</p>
<p>“Supportless” (<em>nirādhāra</em>) does not mean the thoughts vanish. It means they float like clouds in sky—unclaimed, unowned. The relationships remain—disciple, son, friend—but the inner grasping that turns them into chains falls away.</p>
<p>“Spontaneously inspired” (<em>preraṇāt</em>) is key. This is not a technique you <em>do</em>; it is a seeing that <em>happens</em>. In the gap between “I am” and the object of identification, awareness wakes up to itself.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice meets you exactly as you are—in the thick of life, tangled in “I” and “mine”—and asks only: <em>Can you let go, even while holding?</em> In that letting go, peace is not achieved. It is recognized as what was always already here: Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>132</h1>
<h3>Five Attributes Of The Absolute</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
नित्यो विभुर्निराधारो व्यापकश्चाखिलाधिपः।<br>
शब्दान्प्रतिक्षणं ध्यायन्कृतार्थोऽर्थानुरूपतः॥१३२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nityo vibhur nirādhāro vyāpakaś cākhilādhipaḥ |<br>
śabdān pratikṣaṇaṃ dhyāyan kṛtārtho ’rthānurūpataḥ ||132||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>“Eternal, all-pervading,<br />
supportless,<br />
omnipresent,<br />
Lord of all”—<br />
meditating on these words<br />
every moment,<br />
one is fulfilled<br />
in exact accordance with their meaning.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana uses the power of sacred affirmation as a means to realization. The practitioner continuously reflects on the five attributes of the absolute:<br />
- <strong>Nitya</strong>: eternal, beyond time’s rise and fall;<br />
- <strong>Vibhu</strong>: all-pervading, without boundary or center;<br />
- <strong>Nirādhāra</strong>: supportless, resting on nothing, dependent on no cause;<br />
- <strong>Vyāpaka</strong>: permeating all, equally present in the atom and the cosmos;<br />
- <strong>Akhilādhipa</strong>: sovereign Lord of all, the one reality in which every phenomenon arises.</p>
<p>This is not mere repetition, but deep <em>dhyāna</em>—holding each word as a lens through which to see reality. By dwelling on these truths <em>pratikṣaṇam</em>—at every instant—the mind aligns with their living essence. In that alignment, the practitioner becomes <em>kṛtārthaḥ</em>—“one whose aim is fulfilled”—not by acquiring something new, but by embodying the very nature described.</p>
<p>Since it relies on conceptual contemplation, this belongs to śāktopāya. Yet as the words dissolve into direct experience, the method ripens into śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 132 transforms metaphysics into lived presence. These five epithets are not theological abstractions; they are mirrors held up to awareness itself.</p>
<ul>
<li>When you feel time passing, remember: <em>nitya</em>—you are the timeless in which time flows.</li>
<li>When you feel confined, remember: <em>vibhu</em>—your awareness is not in the body; the body is in your awareness.</li>
<li>When you seek security, remember: <em>nirādhāra</em>—freedom is not found in supports, but in the courage to stand without them.</li>
<li>When you see separation, remember: <em>vyāpaka</em>—the same light shines in friend and foe, leaf and star.</li>
<li>When you feel powerless, remember: <em>akhilādhipa</em>—you are not a subject ruled by the world; you are the sovereign space in which the world appears.</li>
</ul>
<p>“Fulfilled in accordance with their meaning” (<em>artha-anurūpataḥ</em>) means the words become flesh. You do not “believe” in eternity—you <em>are</em> eternity appearing as this breath. You do not “accept” omnipresence—you <em>live</em> it, walking through the world as the unbroken field of consciousness.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not affirmation as self-hypnosis, but as self-recognition. In the echo of these words, Bhairava is not invoked. He is remembered—and in that remembering, the seeker vanishes into the sought.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>133</h1>
<h3>Universe As Magical Illusion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अतत्त्वमिन्द्रजालाभमिदं सर्वमवस्थितम्।<br>
किं तत्त्वमिन्द्रजालस्येति दृढ्यच्छममाव्रजेत्॥१३३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
atattvam indrajālābham idaṃ sarvam avasthitam |<br>
kiṃ tattvam indrajālasyeti dṛḍhyac chamaṁ āvrajeta ||133||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>All this universe is unreal,<br />
like a magician’s illusion—<br />
for what truth can there be in a magic show?<br />
Holding this insight with unwavering certainty,<br />
one enters perfect peace.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse declares the entire manifest cosmos (<em>idaṃ sarvam</em>) to be <em>atattvam</em>—devoid of inherent reality—and compares it to <em>indrajāla</em>, the net of illusion cast by the cosmic magician. Whether the 118 worlds, the body, or personal history, all are appearances without substance, like figures in a dream or actors in a play.</p>
<p>The practitioner is not asked to deny the world’s appearance, but to see through its insubstantiality. The question <em>“kiṃ tattvam indrajālasya?”</em>—“What reality is there in illusion?”—is not rhetorical; it is an invitation to direct inquiry. When one firmly recognizes that all phenomena are empty of independent existence, the mind stops clinging. In that non-grasping, <em>śamaṁ āvrajeta</em>—perfect peace arises, not as a state to achieve, but as the natural condition of awareness freed from delusion.</p>
<p>Though it uses discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment this seeing becomes total and effortless, it reveals the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 133 is the culmination of non-dual deconstruction. The “magic show” (<em>indrajāla</em>) is not a metaphor for falsehood, but for transparency. Just as a magician’s rope appears as a snake to the deluded but remains rope to the wise, the world appears as solid only to the mind that believes in separation.</p>
<p>The dream analogy is precise: in sleep, the dream world feels utterly real—fear, joy, death—all seem binding. Yet upon waking, one laughs: <em>There was never any Yama, no register, no terror—only mind playing with itself.</em> The waking world is no different.</p>
<p>“Unwavering certainty” (<em>dṛḍhyat</em>) is not intellectual conviction, but the steady gaze that sees every form as fleeting, every identity as provisional. When you look at a mountain and know it as temporary crystallization of earth, or see your own thoughts as passing clouds, the world loses its power to bind.</p>
<p>“Perfect peace” (<em>śamaṁ</em>) is not tranquility in the absence of disturbance, but the unshakable stillness that holds all disturbance as play. In that peace, the universe is not rejected—it is embraced as the luminous dance of emptiness.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not nihilism. It is liberation through clarity. When the last vestige of “this is real” vanishes, what remains is not void, but Bhairava: the ever-present, ever-free reality behind the magic—and the Magician himself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>134</h1>
<h3>World As Void Dependent On Knowing</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
आत्मनो निर्विकारस्य क्व ज्ञानं क्व च वा क्रिया।<br>
ज्ञानयत्ता बहिर्भाव अतः शून्यमिदं जगत्॥१३४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ātmano nirvikārasya kva jñānaṃ kva ca vā kriyā |<br>
jñāna-yattā bahir-bhāva ataḥ śūnyam idaṃ jagat ||134||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>For the Self, ever changeless,<br />
where is knowledge? Where is action?<br />
All outer phenomena arise only in relation to knowing—<br />
thus, this world is void.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse cuts to the root of all identification. The true Self (<em>ātman</em>) is <em>nirvikāra</em>—absolutely immutable, untouched by modification. In that pure being, there is no room for <em>jñāna</em> (knowing) or <em>kriyā</em> (doing), for both imply change: a subject acquiring knowledge, an agent performing action.</p>
<p>Yet the world of objects (<em>bahir-bhāva</em>) appears only <em>because</em> of knowledge—“I see,” “I know,” “I act.” This dependency reveals its insubstantiality: the world has no independent reality; it is a reflection in the mirror of awareness. Since it exists only in relation to a knower, and the true Self is beyond knower and known, the universe is ultimately <em>śūnyam</em>—empty of inherent existence.</p>
<p>This is śāmbhavopāya: direct recognition that the Self was never the doer or knower, and the world was never truly “out there.”</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 134 is the final undoing of the doer. It does not deny experience—thoughts arise, hands move—but exposes the illusion that “I” am their source. The immutable Self is like space: sounds echo in it, but space neither hears nor produces them.</p>
<p>“Where is knowledge? Where is action?” is not a negation, but an invitation to look. When you say “I know this,” feel the “I” before the knowing. Is it changed by the knowledge? When you act, is there a “you” behind the movement—or only movement, luminous and free?</p>
<p>The peacock’s feather metaphor is precise: the vibrant colors arise from a colorless plasma. So too, all knowing and doing arise from the changeless ground. The colors are real as appearance, unreal as essence.</p>
<p>“Thus, this world is void” (<em>ataḥ śūnyam idaṃ jagat</em>) is not nihilism. It is freedom. The world remains, but it is seen as play—<em>līlā</em>—of the absolute. Not a solid reality to navigate, but a dance of light on water.</p>
<p>In that seeing, the last vestige of “I am the knower” dissolves. What remains is not emptiness, but the fullness of Bhairava.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>135</h1>
<h3>Beyond Bondage And Liberation</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
न मे बन्धो न मे मोक्षो जीवस्यैता विभीषिकाः।<br>
प्रतिबिम्बमिदं बुद्धेर्जलेष्विव विवस्वतः॥१३५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
na me bandho na me mokṣo jīvasyaitā vibhīṣikāḥ |<br>
pratibimbam idaṃ buddher jaleṣv iva vivasvataḥ ||135||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>For me, there is neither bondage nor liberation—<br />
these are mere phantoms that terrify the individual soul.<br />
This world is but a reflection in the mind,<br />
like the sun mirrored in rippling water.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse exposes the ultimate illusion: even the concepts of <em>bandha</em> (bondage) and <em>mokṣa</em> (liberation) are projections of the limited intellect (<em>buddhi</em>), not truths of the Self. The Self—ever free, ever whole—never fell into bondage, and thus never needs liberation.</p>
<p>The individual soul (<em>jīva</em>) trembles before these ideas like a child afraid of ghosts (<em>vibhīṣikāḥ</em>). But just as the sun remains untouched by its reflection in a stream—whether the water is still or turbulent—the absolute remains unaltered by the mind’s dualistic narratives.</p>
<p>When one sees that both bondage and liberation are <em>pratibimba</em>—reflections in the limited medium of <em>buddhi</em>—fear dissolves. True freedom is not the attainment of liberation, but the recognition that one was never bound.</p>
<p>Though it uses discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment this insight becomes total, it reveals śāmbhavopāya: the spontaneous abidance in the Self beyond all pairs.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 135 is the final release from spiritual ambition. The seeker who strives for liberation is still caught in the dream of separation. Bhairava declares: <em>“I am not liberated—because I was never bound.”</em></p>
<p>The metaphor of the sun in water is exact:<br />
- When the water is calm, the reflection is clear—this is the state of the “liberated” sage.<br />
- When the water churns, the reflection shatters—this is the “bound” soul in turmoil.<br />
But the sun itself? Unmoved. Unchanged. Ever one.</p>
<p>“Liberation” becomes another cage when sought as an achievement. The moment you say “I am free,” you have already created a “me” that was once unfree. True freedom is the silent ground before such distinctions arise.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse is not indifference—it is the highest clarity. In the marketplace, in sorrow, in joy, the yogi acts without inner division, knowing all states are reflections in the mirror of awareness. And in that knowing, Bhairava is not beyond the reflection. He is the light that makes reflection possible.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>136</h1>
<h3>Withdrawal From Senses To Abide In Self</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
इन्द्रियद्वारकं सर्वं सुखदुःखादिसङ्गमम्।<br>
इतीन्द्रियाणि संत्यज्य स्वस्थः स्वात्मनि वर्तते॥१३६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
indriya-dvārakaṃ sarvaṃ sukha-duḥkhādi-saṅgamam |<br>
itīndriyāṇi saṃtyajya svasthaḥ svātmani vartate ||136||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>All contact with pleasure, pain, and the like<br />
occurs through the gates of the senses.<br />
Therefore, withdraw from the senses,<br />
and abide, self-established, in your true Self.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This dharana reveals that the entire spectrum of worldly experience—pleasure (<em>sukha</em>), pain (<em>duḥkha</em>), excitement, sorrow—arises only through the channels of the senses (<em>indriya-dvārakam</em>). These are not inherent realities, but fleeting impressions conditioned by sensory contact.</p>
<p>The instruction is not to physically disable the senses, but to <em>saṃtyajya</em>—withdraw identification from their play. One does not reject sensation, but ceases to believe “I am the feeler.” In that non-identification, one becomes <em>svasthaḥ</em>—“self-established,” resting naturally in the <em>svātman</em>, the true Self, which is untouched by all dualities.</p>
<p>Though it begins with discernment (placing it in śāktopāya), the moment awareness abides in the Self without reference to sense experience, it matures into śāmbhavopāya—the spontaneous state.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 136 is not ascetic renunciation, but ontological clarity. The senses are not enemies; they are portals through which the world appears. But the error is not in seeing or feeling—it is in the inner narrative: <em>“This is mine. This affects me.”</em></p>
<p>“Withdraw from the senses” (<em>indriyāṇi saṃtyajya</em>) means refusing to let sensory input define your reality. When pain arises, do not become the sufferer. When joy comes, do not become the enjoyer. Let both pass through awareness like wind through sky—unclaimed, unowned.</p>
<p>“Self-established” (<em>svasthaḥ</em>) is the key. It is not a state you attain, but your native condition when you stop outsourcing your identity to transient experience. The Self is not <em>in</em> the body, reacting to stimuli; the body is <em>in</em> the Self, appearing as vibration.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is wisdom in action. It does not require special postures or mantras—only the constant return to the question: <em>Is awareness altered by what it witnesses?</em> The answer—never—is freedom.</p>
<p>In that freedom, Bhairava is not beyond the senses. He is the silent witness within which all sensing arises—and vanishes—ever free, ever whole.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>137</h1>
<h3>Knower Within Knowledge Itself</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
ज्ञानं प्रकाशकं लोके आत्मा चैव प्रकाशकः।<br>
अनयोरपृथग्भावाज्ज्ञानी ज्ञाने विभाव्यते॥१३७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
jñānaṃ prakāśakaṃ loke ātmā caiva prakāśakaḥ |<br>
anayor apṛthag-bhāvāj jñānī jñāne vibhāvyate ||137||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>In this world, knowledge illuminates all;<br />
the Self, too, is the illuminator.<br />
Since they are not two,<br />
the knower is to be seen within knowledge itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse resolves the apparent duality between <em>jñāna</em> (knowing) and <em>ātman</em> (the Self). Knowledge is that by which all objects are revealed; the Self is the pure subjectivity that makes knowing possible. Though they seem distinct—the light and the source of light—in truth they are <em>apṛthag-bhāva</em>: non-separate, inseparable.</p>
<p>The practitioner is instructed to <em>vibhāvyate</em>—discern, contemplate—not as separate entities, but as one reality. When one looks deeply, the knower (<em>jñānī</em>) is found not apart from knowing (<em>jñāna</em>), but as its very essence. There is no “I” behind awareness; awareness is the “I.”</p>
<p>This is śāktopāya: the method of wisdom, where discernment alone reveals non-duality. Yet in the moment of recognition, the distinction between knower and known collapses, revealing the immediacy of śāmbhavopāya.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 137 dismantles the last subtle division: the belief that awareness needs a “holder.” Knowledge is not a tool the Self uses; it is the Self in action. Like sunlight and the sun—can you separate the radiance from its source?</p>
<p>“Knowledge illuminates the world” (<em>jñānaṃ prakāśakaṃ loke</em>)—yes. But without the Self, there would be no one to whom the world appears. Yet the Self is not a silent witness <em>behind</em> knowledge; it is the luminous openness <em>within which</em> knowledge arises.</p>
<p>“Since they are not two” (<em>anayor apṛthag-bhāvāt</em>) is the pivot. When you see a tree, the seeing is not yours—it <em>is</em> you. The joy you feel is not possessed by you—it <em>is</em> you. The thought that passes is not observed by you—it <em>is</em> you. Awareness and its content are not subject and object, but one seamless flow.</p>
<p>“The knower is to be seen within knowledge” (<em>jñānī jñāne vibhāvyate</em>) means: don’t look for the Self apart from your present experience. In the very act of reading these words, the knower is already present—not as a ghost in the machine, but as the living space of knowing itself.</p>
<p>Thus, this practice is not meditation on an abstract principle. It is the simple, direct seeing that in every moment, awareness and its expression are one taste. And in that seeing, Bhairava is not beyond experience. He is experience, fully awake.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>138</h1>
<h3>Dissolution Of The Fourfold Contraction</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
मनसं चेतना शक्तिरात्मा चेति चतुष्टयम्।<br>
यदा प्रिये परिक्षीणं तदा तद्भैरवं वपुः॥१३८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
manasaṃ cetanā śaktir ātmā ceti catuṣṭayam |<br>
yadā priye parikṣīṇaṃ tadā tad bhairavaṃ vapuḥ ||138||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Beloved, when the four—<br />
mind, sentient awareness,<br />
vital energy, and limited self—<br />
have utterly dissolved,<br />
then the form of Bhairava shines forth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This is the culminating verse of the Vijñāna Bhairava’s 112 dharanas. It names the fourfold contraction that veils the Self:<br />
- <strong>Manas</strong>: the mind, source of fragmented thought (<em>vikalpa</em>);<br />
- <strong>Cetanā</strong>: individualized awareness, the sense of “I perceive”;<br />
- <strong>Śakti</strong>: the energy of breath and vital force (<em>prāṇa</em>), the pulsation of embodiment;<br />
- <strong>Ātmā</strong>: the limited ego, the sense of being a separate “I.”</p>
<p>These are not enemies to destroy, but veils to see through. When, through any of the preceding practices, these four dissolve—not by force, but by recognition of their root in pure awareness—what remains is not absence, but <em>tad bhairavaṃ vapuḥ</em>: the luminous body of Bhairava, the unconditioned reality.</p>
<p>This is not a new attainment. It is the end of the illusion that these four ever defined you.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 138 is the final unbinding. The “four” are the architecture of separation:<br />
- <strong>Mind</strong> divides the world into “this” and “that”;<br />
- <strong>Cetanā</strong> locates awareness in a body;<br />
- <strong>Śakti</strong> binds consciousness to breath and rhythm;<br />
- <strong>Ātmā</strong> claims ownership of it all.</p>
<p>When they “dissolve” (<em>parikṣīṇam</em>), it is not annihilation. It is the collapse of the illusion that they were ever solid. The mind does not vanish—it is seen as thought appearing in space. Breath does not stop—it is recognized as the pulsation of the absolute. The “I” does not die—it is revealed as the open field in which all identity arises and passes.</p>
<p>This is the fulfillment of all 112 methods—not as techniques to perfect, but as doorways to this one recognition. Whether through breath, sound, void, devotion, or fear, each path leads here: to the end of the seeker.</p>
<p>And in that end, Bhairava is not found as other. He is the ever-present ground—unmade, unmeasured, unbound—shining as your own true nature: ever free, ever whole.</p>
<p>Thus, the Vijñāna Bhairava does not conclude with instruction, but with revelation: <strong>You are already That.</strong></p>
</div>

---
<h1>139</h1>
<h3>Completion Of 112 Instructions</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
निस्तरङ्गोपदेशानां शतमुक्तं समाशतः।<br>
द्वादशाभ्यधिकं देवि यज्ज्ञात्वा ज्ञानविज्जनः॥१३९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nistaraṅgopadeśānāṃ śatam uktaṃ samāśataḥ |<br>
dvādaśābhyadhikaṃ devi yaj jñātvā jñānavijjanaḥ ||139||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Goddess, I have fully disclosed<br />
one hundred and twelve<br />
instructions for the waveless state—<br />
knowing which, one becomes rich in wisdom.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Here Bhairava concludes the revelation, affirming that the 112 dharanas (<em>nistaraṅga-upadeśānām</em>—“teachings for the tideless state”) have been completely imparted. The number is symbolic: 100 represents completeness, and 12 alludes to the full cycle of manifestation (as in the twelve <em>kalās</em> or the <em>dvādaśānta</em>).</p>
<p>These are not mere techniques, but direct portals to <em>nistaraṅga</em>—the ocean of awareness without waves, where thought, emotion, and identity subside into their source. The one who truly <em>knows</em> them—not intellectually, but through embodied recognition—becomes <em>jñānavijjanaḥ</em>: one whose life is saturated with living wisdom, free from the turbulence of duality.</p>
<p>This final verse is both seal and invitation: the transmission is complete, yet the path remains open to all who dare to see.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 139 is the quiet closure of a thunderous revelation. The “112 methods” are not prescriptions to follow sequentially, but mirrors held up to the many faces of your own awareness. Some are gentle (gazing at the sky), some fierce (the shock of a prick), some simple (listening to breath), some paradoxical (sneezing as awakening)—yet all converge on the same truth: <strong>you are already the waveless ocean</strong> free from mental turbulence.</p>
<p>“Knowing which” (<em>yaj jñātvā</em>) does not mean memorizing verses, but <em>becoming</em> the understanding. The wise one (<em>jñānavijjanaḥ</em>) is not the scholar, but the one in whom wisdom flows as spontaneous presence—unshaken by praise or blame, joy or sorrow.</p>
<p>Thus, the Vijñāna Bhairava does not end with a ritual or a command. It ends with a gift: the assurance that liberation is not distant. It is the very ground of every breath, every blink, every moment—already whole, already free.</p>
<p>And in that freedom, Bhairava and Bhairavī are not two.  They are the one taste of reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>140</h1>
<h3>Becoming Bhairava Through Devotion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अत्र चैकतमे युक्तो जायते भैरवः स्वयम्।<br>
वाचा करोति कर्माणि सापानुग्रहकारकः॥१४०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
atra caikatame yukto jāyate bhairavaḥ svayam |<br>
vācā karoti karmāṇi sāpānugraha-kārakaḥ ||140||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>If one is fully absorbed in even a single one of these,<br />
one becomes Bhairava himself.<br />
Such a one accomplishes all by speech alone—<br />
bestowing grace or curse through the power of the word.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse affirms the potency of singular devotion. Among the 112 dharanas, it is not necessary to master them all; deep immersion in <em>even one</em>—with total presence and unwavering focus—is enough to dissolve the sense of separation. When such a practitioner becomes <em>yuktaḥ</em>—united, established—in that one method, they do not “attain” Bhairava; they <em>are</em> Bhairava (<em>svayam</em>).</p>
<p>In that state, speech is no longer mere utterance, but <em>vāc</em>—the creative power of consciousness itself. Words carry the weight of reality: blessings manifest, curses dissolve, not through personal will, but because the speaker <em>is</em> the source from which all manifestation flows. This is not magic, but the natural expression of non-dual realization.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 140 is both promise and warning. It declares that authenticity matters more than quantity. The practitioner who truly abides in <em>one</em> doorway—whether through breath, void, devotion, or wonder—touches the whole. For the absolute is indivisible; to enter it through any gate is to be <em>it</em>.</p>
<p>“Accomplishes all by speech alone” (<em>vācā karoti karmāṇi</em>) does not mean supernatural command, but the effortless alignment of word and reality. When there is no gap between inner truth and outer expression, speech becomes <em>ṛta</em>—truth in action. A blessing is not a wish, but a revelation of what already is; a curse is not malice, but the clear naming of illusion.</p>
<p>Yet this power is not for display. It arises only when the “I” has vanished—when the speaker is no longer a doer, but the voice of the One. Thus, the verse is not an invitation to seek siddhis, but to recognize: <strong>true mastery is self-effacement.</strong></p>
<p>In that silence behind the word, Bhairava is not invoked.  He speaks.  And in that speaking, the world is made anew—  not as it should be,  but as it always was.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>141</h1>
<h3>Freedom From Aging And Death</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अजरामरतमेति सोऽणिमादिगुणान्वितः।<br>
योगिनीनां प्रियो देवि सर्वमेलापकाधिपः॥१४१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ajarāmaratam eti so 'ṇimādi-guṇānvitaḥ |<br>
yoginīnāṃ priyo devi sarva-melāpakādhipaḥ ||141||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Such a yogi attains freedom from aging and death,<br />
endowed with powers like aṇimā and beyond.<br />
O Goddess, he becomes beloved of the Yoginīs,<br />
lord over all sacred assemblies.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse describes the fruit of full realization. When one is established as Bhairava, the limitations of mortal existence dissolve. <em>Ajara-amara</em>—freedom from decay and death—is not physical immortality, but the recognition that the Self was never born, and thus never dies.</p>
<p>The <em>aṇimādi-guṇāḥ</em>—the eight great siddhis (aṇimā, mahimā, laghimā, etc.)—arise not as personal attainments, but as spontaneous expressions of unobstructed consciousness. They are not sought; they <em>adorn</em> the one who is free.</p>
<p>Being <em>yoginīnāṃ priyaḥ</em>—beloved of the Yoginīs—signifies harmonious resonance with the dynamic power of Śakti in all her liberated forms. The <em>sarva-melāpakādhipaḥ</em>—"lord of all gatherings"—does not rule over others, but presides as the embodiment of the unity in which all awakened beings naturally converge.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 141 is not a promise of supernatural feats, but a declaration of ontological freedom. The "end of aging and death" is the end of identification with the body-mind complex. The yogi may grow old, but awareness remains ever fresh; the body may fall, but the Self never perishes.</p>
<p>The siddhis are not magical powers, but the natural transparency of a mind free from contraction. Aṇimā (the power to become subtler than the subtle) is simply the ability to rest as pure awareness, prior to all form. Mahimā (to become vast) is the recognition that one's being <em>is</em> the cosmos.</p>
<p>To be "beloved of Yoginīs" is to be in seamless alignment with the spontaneous intelligence of reality. The Yoginīs are not external deities, but the living forces of perception, energy, and insight—now fully integrated. The "assemblies" (<em>melāpa</em>) are not celestial councils, but the inner harmony of all faculties functioning as one.</p>
<p>Thus, this verse does not glorify the yogi; it reveals the natural state of one who has remembered: <strong>I am not in the world. The world is in me.</strong> And in that knowing, every breath is liberation, every step is grace.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>142</h1>
<h3>Untouched While Acting</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
जीवन्नपि विमुक्तोऽसौ कुर्वन्नपि न लिप्यते॥१४२॥<br>
श्रीदेव्युवाच।<br>
इदम्यदि वपुर्देव परायाश्च महेश्वर।<br>
एवमुक्तव्यवस्थायां जप्यते को जपश्च कः॥१४२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
jīvann api vimukto 'sau kurvann api na lipyate<br>
śrī-devy uvāca:<br>
idam yadi vapur deva parāyāś ca maheśvara |<br>
evam-ukta-vyavasthāyāṃ japyate ko japaś ca kaḥ ||142||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Though living in this world,<br />
he is already liberated;<br />
though acting,<br />
he remains utterly untouched.</p>
<p>The Goddess said:</p>
<p>O Lord, if this—<br />
the essence of the Supreme—<br />
is as you have revealed,<br />
then, in this very state,<br />
who is there to recite,<br />
and what is there to be recited?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>The first part of this verse affirms the state of <em>jīvanmukti</em>—liberation while living. Though engaged in the world (<em>kurvan</em>), the realized being is <em>na lipyate</em>—untouched by action, like a lotus in water. His activity flows from fullness, not need; from play (<em>līlā</em>), not compulsion.</p>
<p>The second part marks a crucial turning point. Having heard the 112 dharanas and their culmination in the identity of the yogi with Bhairava, the Devi asks the ultimate question—not in doubt, but in consummate understanding. If reality is non-dual, if the Self is already whole, if speech itself is the vibration of the absolute—then where is the need for <em>japa</em> (recitation), for <em>japya</em> (the deity to whom it is offered)?</p>
<p>In the state fully established as Bhairava—there is no separation between devotee and divine, no gap between utterance and essence. Mantra, then, is not a means to reach the absolute; it <em>is</em> the absolute resonating as sound. But when even that distinction dissolves, what remains is silence: the space in which all words arise and vanish, unneeded, yet unbroken.</p>
<p>The Devi's question is the razor that cuts the last thread of method. It is not rejection of practice, but the recognition that in the highest state, <em>all practice is already fulfilled</em>.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 142 contains two profound teachings. First, the essence of jīvanmukti: "Though acting, he remains untouched." The yogi eats, speaks, works—but there is no "doer" behind the doing. Action arises like wind in space: dynamic, yet leaving no trace. In this, he is not extraordinary—<em>he is ordinary, seen without distortion.</em></p>
<p>Second, the final unbinding of spiritual technique. The Goddess, though herself the embodiment of Śakti, poses the question that every sincere seeker must confront: <em>If I am already That, why recite?</em></p>
<p>"Who will recite?" (<em>ko japaḥ</em>)—there is no "I" left to perform the act.<br />
"What is to be recited?" (<em>japaś ca kaḥ</em>)—there is no "other" to invoke.</p>
<p>This is not nihilism. It is the fullness of immanence. When the world is seen as the pulsation of consciousness itself, every breath is mantra, every step is ritual, every silence is prayer. The <em>japa</em> does not cease—it is recognized as the eternal hum of existence.</p>
<p>Yet in the absolute stillness beyond even that hum, there is nothing to do, nothing to say, no one to do it. This is not a state to attain, but the ground already present when all seeking ends.</p>
<p>Thus, the Devi's question is not a doubt, but a mirror. In it, Bhairava sees his own face—and smiles. For in that smile, all 112 methods, all mantras, all worlds dissolve into the one taste:<br />
<strong>Silence, awake to itself.</strong></p>
</div>

---
<h1>143</h1>
<h3>Collapse Of Ritual Framework</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
ध्यायते को महानाथ पूज्यते कश्च तृप्यति।<br>
हूयते कस्य वा होमो यागः कस्य च किं कथम्॥१४३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
dhyāyate ko mahānātha pūjyate kaśca tṛpyati |<br>
hūyate kasya vā homo yāgaḥ kasya ca kiṃ katham ||143||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Great Lord, who is there to meditate?<br />
Who is to be worshipped, and who will be satisfied?<br />
To whom is the oblation offered in the fire?<br />
Whose is the sacrifice, and how can it be performed?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>The Devi’s inquiry deepens, extending beyond mantra to the entire edifice of ritual and devotion. If the Self is non-dual—if Bhairava is all that is—then the very structure of spiritual practice collapses:<br />
- <strong>Meditation</strong> requires a meditator and an object;<br />
- <strong>Worship</strong> demands a worshipper and a deity;<br />
- <strong>Sacrifice</strong> implies an offerer, an offering, and a recipient.</p>
<p>There is no separation. The fire is not <em>other</em> than the offering; the worshipper is not <em>other</em> than the worshipped. The Devi’s questions are not skeptical—they are the radiant clarity of one who sees to the root.</p>
<p>Her inquiry prepares the ground for Bhairava’s final revelation: that all these acts, when performed with full recognition, are not means to realization, but expressions of it. Yet in the absolute, even expression dissolves into pure being.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses dismantle the scaffolding of religion itself. The Devi, in her supreme wisdom, points to the paradox at the heart of all spiritual life: <em>If God is everywhere, to whom do we pray? If truth is within, what are we seeking?</em></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>“Who will mediate?”</strong> — When awareness is already whole, there is no “me” to sit in meditation.</li>
<li><strong>“Who will be gratified?”</strong> — When there is no lack, worship becomes play, not petition.</li>
<li><strong>“Whose is the offering?”</strong> — When all is Śiva, the fire, the ghee, and the hand that pours are one vibration.</li>
</ul>
<p>This is not rejection of ritual. It is its consummation. The <em>yāga</em> (sacrifice) is not abandoned—it is seen as the eternal offering of form into formlessness, of wave into ocean. But in the final silence, even the notion of “offering” vanishes.</p>
<p>The Devi’s questions echo in every genuine seeker: <em>If I am already home, why walk the path?</em> The answer is not in words, but in the space between them—the same space Bhairava now reveals as the living heart of all practice and its utter transcendence.</p>
<p>Thus, her inquiry is not an end, but a threshold.  And on the other side—  only Bhairava remains,  not as object of worship,  but as the worship itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>144-145</h1>
<h3>Beyond External Ritual</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
श्रीभैरव उवाच।<br>
एषा त्वत्र प्रक्रिया बाह्या स्थूलेष्वेव मृगेक्षणे॥१४४॥<br>
भूयो भूयः परे भावे भावना भाव्यते हि या।<br>
जपः सोऽत्र स्वयं नादो मन्त्रात्मा जप्य इदृशः॥१४५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
śrī-bhairava uvāca:<br>
eṣā tv atra prakriyā bāhyā sthūleṣv eva mṛgekṣaṇe ||144||<br>
bhūyo bhūyaḥ pare bhāve bhāvanā bhāvyate hi yā |<br>
japaḥ so ’tra svayaṃ nādo mantrātmā japya idṛśaḥ ||145||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Śrī Bhairava replied:<br />
O gazelle-eyed one,<br />
this ritual tradition you speak of—<br />
mantra, worship, sacrifice—<br />
is external,<br />
meant only for those<br />
still abiding in the gross.</p>
<p>But the contemplation<br />
that flows without break<br />
in the supreme state—<br />
that is true recitation.<br />
Here, the spontaneous sound (<em>nāda</em>)<br />
is the mantra itself,<br />
and the Self, as universal “I,”<br />
is the one to be recited.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava distinguishes two levels of practice. The external (<em>bāhyā</em>) path—rituals, formal japa, puja—is prescribed for those whose awareness is still coarse (<em>sthūleṣu</em>), not yet refined enough to rest in non-dual immediacy. It is a preparatory discipline, not the ultimate truth.</p>
<p>The real japa is <em>bhāvanā bhāvyate hi yā</em>—the unbroken contemplation (<em>bhūyo bhūyaḥ</em>, “again and again” in the sense of seamless continuity, like a flame without flicker) in the supreme state (<em>pare bhāve</em>). In this, there is no separate “reciter” or “mantra.” The inner resonance—<em>svayaṃ nādaḥ</em>, the self-arising sound of awareness—is both the utterance and the essence.</p>
<p>The <em>japya</em>—the one for whom japa is done—is not an external deity, but <em>mantrātmā</em>: the Self as the very body of mantra, the universal “I” (<em>aham</em>) that pulses as all existence.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses are the turning point: from form to formlessness, from method to immediacy. Bhairava does not condemn ritual; he places it in its proper context—as a raft for those still on the shore of duality. But for the one ready to swim in the open ocean, the raft is left behind.</p>
<p>“Contemplation without pause” (<em>bhūyo bhūyaḥ</em>) is not repetition, but unbroken abidance. Like a candle flame that appears steady though made of countless flickers, true meditation is not a series of efforts, but a single, flowing presence. The moment you “try again,” you have left the stream.</p>
<p>“The spontaneous sound” (<em>svayaṃ nādaḥ</em>) is not heard with ears. It is the hum of being itself—the silent vibration behind thought, breath, and silence. To “recite” it is not to vocalize, but to <em>be</em> it.</p>
<p>And the “deity to be recited” (<em>japya</em>) is not other than you. It is the <em>mantrātmā</em>—the Self as the living essence of all mantra, all sound, all silence. When you realize this, every breath is japa, every step is puja, every moment is sacrifice—and yet, nothing is done, no one does it, and no one receives it.</p>
<p>Thus, Bhairava reveals:  <strong>True worship is recognition.  True recitation is being.  True sacrifice is abidance.</strong>  And in that abidance,  Bhairava is not invoked.  He simply is.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>146</h1>
<h3>Formless, Supportless Awareness</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
ध्यानं हि निश्चला बुद्धिर्निराकारा निराश्रया।<br>
न तु ध्यानं शरीराक्षि-मुखहस्तादिकल्पना॥१४६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
dhyānaṃ hi niścalā buddhir nirākārā nirāśrayā |<br>
na tu dhyānaṃ śarīrākṣi-mukha-hastādi-kalpanā ||146||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>True meditation is unwavering awareness—<br />
formless, supportless.<br />
It is not the visualization<br />
of a deity’s body, eyes, face, hands, or limbs.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava now defines <em>dhyāna</em> not as imaginative construction, but as the natural state of <em>niścalā buddhiḥ</em>—a mind utterly still, free from fluctuation. This awareness is <em>nirākārā</em> (without form) and <em>nirāśrayā</em> (without support): it does not rest on any object, internal or external—not the space between breaths, not the brow center, not even the void as a concept.</p>
<p>Mental imagery—whether of a personal deity’s form (<em>śarīra</em>), eyes (<em>akṣi</em>), face (<em>mukha</em>), or hands (<em>hasta</em>)—belongs to preparatory practice for the gross mind. True meditation is the direct abidance in pure subjectivity, where even the subtlest support has been relinquished.</p>
<p>This is not negation of form, but recognition that the ground of all form is formless awareness itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 146 strips meditation to its essence. The word <em>dhyāna</em> is often conflated with visualization, concentration on symbols, or inner imagery. But Bhairava declares: <strong>True meditation is what remains when all that is seen through.</strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Unwavering awareness</strong> (<em>niścalā buddhiḥ</em>) is not forced stillness, but the natural calm that arises when attention stops chasing.</li>
<li><strong>Formless</strong> (<em>nirākārā</em>) means no shape—divine or otherwise—is held as object. The luminous space of awareness needs no face to be recognized.</li>
<li><strong>Supportless</strong> (<em>nirāśrayā</em>) is the final release: even the subtle reliance on “the gap between breaths” or “the void” is dropped. Awareness rests in itself, not <em>on</em> anything.</li>
</ul>
<p>The rejection of deity visualization is not iconoclasm. It is clarity: such practices are skillful means for the mind still needing form. But when the mind matures, it no longer needs a mirror to see—it <em>is</em> the seeing.</p>
<p>Thus, true <em>dhyāna</em> is not an act. It is the cessation of all doing. In that cessation, Bhairava is not found as an object of meditation. He is the meditator, the meditation, and the silence in which both dissolve—ever free, ever whole.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>147</h1>
<h3>Merging Into Thought-Free Space</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
पूजा नाम न पुष्पाद्यैर्या मतिः क्रियते दृढा।<br>
निर्विकल्पे परे व्योम्नि सा पूजा ह्यदराल्लयः॥१४७॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
pūjā nāma na puṣpādyair yā matiḥ kriyate dṛḍhā |<br>
nirvikalpe pare vyomni sā pūjā hy adarāl-layaḥ ||147||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Worship is not flowers or offerings—<br />
but the mind’s unwavering abidance<br />
in the supreme, thought-free space.<br />
True worship is merging into that<br />
with reverence,<br />
as total dissolution.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava dismantles conventional ritual to reveal the essence of <em>pūjā</em>. External offerings—flowers, incense, ghee—are symbolic gestures for those still bound by form. But true worship (<em>pūjā</em>) is <em>yā matiḥ kriyate dṛḍhā</em>—the firm establishment of awareness in <em>nirvikalpa para vyoman</em>, the supreme void beyond thought, the boundless sky of pure consciousness (<em>cid-ākāśa</em>).</p>
<p>This is not passive contemplation, but <em>adarāl-layaḥ</em>—absorption born of profound reverence (<em>adarā</em>). It is not rejection of the world, but the total offering of the self into its source. In that surrender, worship is not an act performed <em>by</em> someone <em>to</em> someone else—it is the collapse of the very distinction between worshipper and worshipped.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 147 is the alchemy of devotion. It does not abolish ritual; it transmutes it. The flower on the altar is not discarded—it is seen as the offering of form back into formlessness. But the <em>real</em> offering is the mind itself, when it stops grasping and dissolves into the open sky of awareness.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>“Not flowers or offerings”</strong> (<em>na puṣpādyaiḥ</em>) means: no external object can bridge the gap between you and the divine—because there is no gap.</li>
<li><strong>“Firm abidance”</strong> (<em>dṛḍhā matiḥ</em>) is not effortful focus, but the natural steadiness of awareness resting in its own nature.</li>
<li><strong>“Supreme, thought-free space”</strong> (<em>nirvikalpe pare vyomni</em>) is not emptiness as absence, but the luminous fullness in which all phenomena arise and vanish unclaimed.</li>
<li><strong>“Merging with reverence”</strong> (<em>adarāl-layaḥ</em>) is key: this dissolution is not annihilation, but the highest honor—like a drop returning to the ocean, not in loss, but in homecoming.</li>
</ul>
<p>Thus, true <em>pūjā</em> is not something you <em>do</em>. It is what you <em>are</em> when the illusion of separation ends. In that being, every breath is an offering, every silence a hymn, and every moment the altar where Bhairava is not worshipped—<br />
but recognized as the very ground of worship itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>148</h1>
<h3>Ever-Full Presence</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अत्रैकतमयुक्तिस्थे योत्पद्येत दिनाद्दिनम्।<br>
भरिताकारता सत्र तृप्तिरत्यन्तपूर्णता॥१४८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
atraikatama-yukti-sthe yo tpadyeta dinād dinam |<br>
bharitākāratā satra tṛptir atyanta-pūrṇatā ||148||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>For one established in even a single one of these ways,<br />
day by day,<br />
what arises is ever-full presence—<br />
a satisfaction that is supreme, complete, unending.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse affirms the ripening of realization in daily life. The practitioner who is <em>yukti-sthaḥ</em>—steadfastly absorbed in any one of the 112 dharanas—does not experience sudden annihilation, but a gradual yet unbroken unfolding of fullness (<em>bharitākāratā</em>). Each day, experience itself becomes saturated with the presence of Bhairava—not as an added state, but as the very texture of ordinary life.</p>
<p>This is <em>satra tṛptiḥ</em>—the satisfaction of true worship—not a fleeting emotional high, but the deep contentment that arises when every moment is recognized as divine play. And because it stems from non-dual recognition, this fulfillment is <em>atyanta-pūrṇatā</em>—utterly complete, needing nothing beyond itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 148 is the quiet fruition of all prior teachings. It does not promise cosmic visions or miraculous powers, but something far more profound: <strong>the sanctification of the ordinary.</strong></p>
<ul>
<li><strong>“Day by day”</strong> (<em>dinād dinam</em>) reveals that awakening is not a single lightning strike, but a deepening clarity that colors each sunrise, each breath, each encounter.</li>
<li><strong>“Ever-full presence”</strong> (<em>bharitākāratā</em>) is not emotional plenitude, but the unshakable sense that nothing is missing—because awareness itself is the only reality, and it is never absent.</li>
<li><strong>“Supreme fullness”</strong> (<em>atyanta-pūrṇatā</em>) is not the end of desire, but the end of seeking. The world continues—joys, sorrows, duties—but none can diminish the inner wholeness that knows itself as Bhairava.</li>
</ul>
<p>This is the ultimate fruit: not escape from life, but life lived in its full depth, where even washing dishes or walking in rain becomes an act of worship—not because it is “holy,” but because the one who acts is no longer separate from the act.</p>
<p>Thus, the 112 methods are not paths to a distant goal.  They are mirrors held up to the truth that is already here—  and in that reflection,  every day becomes a hymn,  every moment a temple,  and every breath a silent “Amen”  to the one reality.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>149</h1>
<h3>Offering Into Awareness-Fire</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
महाशून्यालये वह्नौ भूताक्षविषयादिकम्।<br>
हूयते मनसा सार्धं स होमश्चेतनास्रुचा॥१४९॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
mahā-śūnyālaye vahnau bhūtākṣa-viṣayādikam |<br>
hūyate manasā sārdhaṃ sa homaś cetanā-srucā ||149||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Real sacrifice is the offering<br />
of the elements, senses, and sense-objects,<br />
along with the mind,<br />
into the fire that blazes in the Great Abode of Void—<br />
using awareness itself as the ladle.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava reveals the inner meaning of <em>homa</em> (sacrificial fire ritual). The true fire (<em>vahni</em>) is not physical, but <em>mahā-śūnyālaye</em>—the luminous awareness that dwells in the Great Void, the boundless space of pure consciousness. Into this fire, one offers not ghee or grains, but <em>bhūtākṣa-viṣayādikam</em>—the five elements (earth, water, fire, air, ether), the five senses, their corresponding objects (form, sound, etc.), and the mind itself.</p>
<p>The instrument of offering is not a bronze spoon, but <em>cetanā-srucā</em>—awareness as the sacrificial ladle. This is not symbolic; it is the actual dissolution of identification. As each aspect of experience is offered—not rejected, but released—into the fire of non-dual awareness, the illusion of separation burns away.</p>
<p>This is the true <em>yajña</em>: not an external ritual, but the continuous offering of the finite into the infinite, until only the flame remains.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 149 transmutes ritual into realization. The “Great Abode of Void” (<em>mahā-śūnyālaya</em>) is not emptiness as negation, but the radiant openness in which all phenomena arise. The “fire” is the self-consuming intelligence of awareness—burning all that is not itself, yet leaving nothing destroyed, only revealed.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Elements, senses, objects</strong> represent the entire cosmos of experience—both inner and outer. To “offer” them is not to renounce them, but to see them as transient expressions of the one reality.</li>
<li><strong>Mind included</strong> means even the sense of “I am offering” must be released. The last vestige of doership is consumed in the fire.</li>
<li><strong>Awareness as the ladle</strong> (<em>cetanā-srucā</em>) is precise: the same awareness that witnesses the offering <em>is</em> the means of offering. There is no separate “me” behind the act—only the fire offering itself to itself.</li>
</ul>
<p>Thus, true <em>homa</em> is not performed at an altar, but in the heart of every moment. When you see a tree, hear a bird, feel pain—each is an offering into the flame of presence. And in that offering, nothing is lost. Everything is transfigured into light.</p>
<p>Bhairava is not the recipient of sacrifice.  He is the fire, the offering, and the silence after the flame.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>150-151</h1>
<h3>Blissful Immersion In Rudra's Power</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यागोऽत्र परमेशानि तुष्टिरानन्दलक्षणा।<br>
क्षपयन्सर्वपापानां त्रायते सर्वस्य पार्वति॥१५०॥<br>
रुद्रशक्तिसमावेशस्तत्क्षेत्रं भावना परा।<br>
अन्यथा तस्य तत्त्वस्य का पूजा कश्च तृप्यति॥१५१॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yāgo ’tra parameśāni tuṣṭir ānanda-lakṣaṇā |<br>
kṣapayan sarva-pāpānāṃ trāyate sarvasya pārvati ||150||<br>
rudra-śakti-samāveśas tat-kṣetraṃ bhāvanā parā |<br>
anyathā tasya tattvasya kā pūjā kaśca tṛpyati ||151||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>O Supreme Goddess, here,<br />
true sacrifice is satisfaction—<br />
marked by bliss,<br />
destroying all impurities,<br />
and liberating all beings.</p>
<p>The sacred field (<em>kṣetra</em>) is<br />
supreme contemplation<br />
and immersion in the Power of Rudra.<br />
Otherwise, for That Reality,<br />
what worship could there be,<br />
and who could be fulfilled?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava now defines the highest <em>yāga</em> (sacrifice) not as external offering, but as <em>tuṣṭiḥ</em>—the self-subsisting contentment that arises from abidance in the Self. This bliss (<em>ānanda-lakṣaṇā</em>) is not emotional pleasure, but the natural fullness of non-dual awareness. In this state, “sins” (<em>pāpānām</em>)—not as moral transgressions, but as contractions of consciousness—are <em>kṣapayat</em>, dissolved by the light of recognition. And because all beings are expressions of that same awareness, all are <em>trāyate</em>—liberated, protected, restored to wholeness.</p>
<p>The true <em>kṣetra</em> (sacred field or pilgrimage site) is not a geographical location, but <em>parā bhāvanā</em>—unbroken contemplation—and <em>rudra-śakti-samāveśaḥ</em>, total absorption in the dynamic power of Rudra (Śiva). Conventional tīrthas—Kṣīra-Bhavānī, Jvālā—are symbolic; their power lies only insofar as they point the mind inward. The real pilgrimage is the journey into the heart of awareness itself.</p>
<p>The final question—<em>“Otherwise, what worship could there be?”</em>—is the seal of non-duality. If reality is one, there is no “other” to worship, no gap to bridge. True fulfillment (<em>tṛpti</em>) is the end of seeking itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verses 150–151 complete the transmutation of ritual into revelation. Every element of external religion is reclaimed as inner truth:<br />
- <strong>Sacrifice</strong> is not killing, but the surrender of separation.<br />
- <strong>Sacred site</strong> is not a temple, but the space of pure awareness where all contraction burns away.<br />
- <strong>Purification</strong> is not washing the body, but the dissolution of the illusion “I am impure.”</p>
<p>The etymology of <em>kṣetra</em>—<em>kṣa</em> (to destroy) and <em>tra</em> (to protect)—is exact: in the fire of non-dual awareness, the false self is destroyed, and the true Self is protected—not as something to be guarded, but as that which cannot be lost.</p>
<p>“Immersion in the Power of Rudra” (<em>rudra-śakti-samāveśaḥ</em>) is the culmination of all 112 dharanas. Whether through breath, void, devotion, or wonder, each path leads here: to the recognition that you are not separate from the pulsating energy of the absolute.</p>
<p>The final rhetorical question—<em>“What worship could there be?”</em>—is not skepticism, but the silence that follows all answers. When the seeker vanishes, worship becomes identity. There is no one left to bow, no one to receive the bow—only the one taste of reality, luminous and free.</p>
<p>Thus, the Vijñāna Bhairava does not lead to a shrine,  but to the shrine within: where every breath is sacrifice,  every moment is pilgrimage,  and every atom sings the name of Bhairava—  not as sound,  but as silence.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>152</h1>
<h3>Sacred Bath As Immersion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
स्वतन्त्रानन्दचिन्मात्र-सारः स्वात्मा हि सर्वतः।<br>
द्वेषाणां तत्स्वरूपे स्वात्मनः स्नानमीरितम्॥१५२॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
svatantra-ānanda-cin-mātra-sāraḥ svātmā hi sarvataḥ |<br>
dveṣāṇāṃ tat-svarūpe svātmanaḥ snānam īritam ||152||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The true Self is essence alone—<br />
freedom, bliss, and pure awareness—<br />
pervading all.<br />
The sacred bath is immersion<br />
in that very nature of one’s own Self.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava now reveals the inner meaning of <em>snāna</em> (sacred bath). External ablution—washing the body with water—is merely physical cleansing. True purification is <em>svātmanaḥ snānam</em>—dissolving the sense of limited selfhood into the recognition of one’s true nature: <em>svatantra-ānanda-cin-mātra-sāraḥ</em>, the essence that is nothing but absolute freedom (<em>svatantra</em>), bliss (<em>ānanda</em>), and consciousness (<em>cit</em>).</p>
<p>This Self is not confined to the body or mind; it is <em>sarvataḥ</em>—present everywhere, the universal ground of all existence. To “bathe” is to withdraw identification from the transient and rest fully in this ever-present reality. In that immersion, all impurity—understood as contraction, ignorance, and separation—is washed away not by water, but by the light of self-recognition.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 152 is the final purification: not of the body, but of identity. The “bath” is not an act performed <em>on</em> the self, but the self <em>remembering itself</em>.</p>
<ul>
<li><strong>Freedom</strong> (<em>svatantra</em>) means uncaused, unconditioned being—never bound, never in need of liberation.</li>
<li><strong>Bliss</strong> (<em>ānanda</em>) is not emotion, but the inherent fullness of awareness untouched by gain or loss.</li>
<li><strong>Consciousness</strong> (<em>cit</em>) is the luminous space in which all phenomena arise, yet which remains unmarked by them.</li>
</ul>
<p>Together, these are not qualities <em>of</em> the Self—they <em>are</em> the Self, <em>mātra-sāraḥ</em>—essence alone, without admixture.</p>
<p>The phrase “immersion in that very nature” (<em>tat-svarūpe svātmanaḥ snānam</em>) is precise. It is not a mental affirmation, but total abidance. When you cease to claim “I am this body,” “I am this mind,” and rest as the open awareness in which both appear, you have taken the true bath.</p>
<p>Thus, the sacred river is not Ganges or Saraswati—  it is the stream of your own awareness,  flowing through every moment,  cleansing not the skin,<br />
but the illusion of separation.</p>
<p>And in that purity,  Bhairava is not found as other.  He is the water, the bather, and the light upon the surface.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>153</h1>
<h3>Worshipper, Worshipped, And Worship</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
यैरेव पूज्यते द्रव्यैस्तर्प्यते वा परापरः।<br>
यश्चैव पूजकः सर्वः स एवैकः क्व पूजनम्॥१५३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
yair eva pūjyate dravyais tarpyate vā parāparaḥ |<br>
yaś caiva pūjakaḥ sarvaḥ sa evaikaḥ kva pūjanam ||153||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The very substances with which worship is offered,<br />
the Transcendent-and-Immanent who is worshipped,<br />
and the worshipper himself—<br />
all are one.<br />
Then, where is worship?</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse is the final collapse of all duality in devotion. The flowers, incense, and lamps offered in ritual (<em>dravyaiḥ</em>) are not separate from the Divine (<em>parāparaḥ</em>). The one who offers (<em>pūjakaḥ</em>) is not other than the One to whom the offering is made. All three—offering, offered, and offerer—are the one reality, <em>sa eva ekaḥ</em>—the singular essence of Bhairava.</p>
<p>In this non-dual recognition, the very notion of <em>pūjanam</em>—worship as a transaction between separate entities—vanishes. How can you offer to what you already are? How can you adore what never left you? The question <em>“kva pūjanam?”</em>—“where is worship?”—is not rhetorical doubt, but the radiant silence that follows the end of all seeking.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 153 is the culmination of the entire Vijñāna Bhairava: a single, luminous stroke that erases the last line between sacred and profane, self and other, means and end.</p>
<p>The garden’s flower is not a symbol <em>of</em> the divine—it <em>is</em> the divine appearing as petal and stem. The incense smoke is not a carrier of prayer—it <em>is</em> prayer made visible. The hands that arrange the offering are not instruments of devotion—they <em>are</em> devotion in motion.</p>
<p>“Transcendent-and-Immanent” (<em>parāparaḥ</em>) is key: Bhairava is not beyond the world, nor confined within it. He is the world’s very substance—both the form and the formless ground from which it arises.</p>
<p>Thus, worship does not cease—it is transfigured. Every act becomes <em>pūjā</em> not because it is directed outward, but because it flows from the recognition: <em>All is That.</em> The marketplace, the kitchen, the meditation cushion—all are altars. The breath, the thought, the heartbeat—all are offerings.</p>
<p>And in that offering, there is no “I” offering to “Thou.”  Only Bhairava,  offering himself to himself,  in the endless dance of love.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>154</h1>
<h3>Breath As Willful Motion</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
व्रजेत्प्राणो विशेज्जीव इच्छया कुटिलाकृतिः।<br>
दीर्घात्मा सा महादेवि परक्षेत्रं परापरा॥१५४॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
vrajeta prāṇo viśej jīva icchayā kuṭilākṛtiḥ |<br>
dīrghātmā sā mahādevi para-kṣetraṃ parāparā ||154||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>The outward breath departs,<br />
the inward breath enters—<br />
by will, in a coiled motion.<br />
When the Great Goddess stretches Herself fully,<br />
that is the supreme sacred space—<br />
Transcendent-and-Immanent.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse reveals the inner dynamics of breath as the play of divine will. Normally, prāṇa (outgoing breath) and jīva (incoming breath) move in a “crooked” (<em>kuṭilākṛtiḥ</em>) path—fragmented, unconscious, governed by habitual patterns. But when one breathes <em>icchayā</em>—by conscious will, not mechanically—the breath becomes an act of awareness.</p>
<p>In that intentional breathing, the Kuṇḍalinī Śakti—here called the Great Goddess (<em>mahādevi</em>)—uncoils from her habitual contraction and straightens into <em>dīrghātmā</em>: “long-breathed,” meaning slow, deep, extended in time and subtle in space. This is not mere prāṇāyāma; it is the alignment of individual will (<em>icchā</em>) with the divine pulsation.</p>
<p>In that alignment, the body itself becomes <em>para-kṣetra</em>—the supreme sacred field—not as a location, but as the living presence of <em>parāparā</em>, the reality that is both utterly transcendent and fully immanent.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 154 returns to the breath—not as technique, but as revelation. The “crooked” breath is the ordinary, unconscious breathing of the unawakened mind—scattered, reactive, bound to the past and future. The “straightened” breath (<em>dīrghātmā</em>) is the breath of presence: slow, deliberate, fully inhabited.</p>
<p>Three energies govern this shift:<br />
- <strong>Prāṇa-śakti</strong>: the automatic life-force, operating even in sleep;<br />
- <strong>Ātma-śakti</strong>: the power of individual will, by which one consciously directs the breath;<br />
- <strong>Prabhu-śakti</strong>: the grace of the Lord, which alone awakens the central channel (<em>madhya-nāḍī</em>).</p>
<p>Bhairava emphasizes <em>icchayā</em>—by will—as the practitioner’s role. You cannot command grace, nor stop the primal life-force—but you can bring awareness to the breath you <em>do</em> control. In that conscious breathing, the crooked path straightens.</p>
<p>“Supreme sacred space” (<em>para-kṣetra</em>) is not a temple or pilgrimage site. It is the body-mind when it becomes transparent to the absolute. The breath, once a sign of limitation, becomes the rhythm of the infinite.</p>
<p>Thus, the sacred is not elsewhere.  It is in the next inhale—  taken not by habit,  but by devotion to presence.  And in that breath,  Bhairava is not invoked.  He breathes.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>155</h1>
<h3>Continuous Recitation</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
अस्यामनुचरन्तिष्ठन्महानन्दमयेऽध्वरे।<br>
तया देव्या समाविष्टः परं भैरवमाप्नुयात्॥१५५॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
asyām anucaran tiṣṭhan mahānandamaye ’dhvare |<br>
tayā devyā samāviṣṭaḥ paraṃ bhairavam āpnuyāt ||155||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Abiding in continuous recitation of Her,<br />
established in the sacrificial rite<br />
that is overflowing with supreme bliss,<br />
one merges with the Goddess—<br />
and thereby attains the highest Bhairava.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>This verse reveals the culmination of the path: not as attainment, but as union. “Her” (<em>asyām</em>) refers to the Great Goddess—the dynamic power of consciousness, the very breath and will that animates all practice. <em>Anucaran tiṣṭhan</em>—“abiding in continuous recitation”—is not vocal repetition, but the unbroken awareness of Her presence in every breath, every pulse, every thought.</p>
<p>The <em>adhvara</em>—the supreme sacrifice—is not a fire ritual, but the living state of <em>mahānandamaya</em>—permeated by great bliss. In this rite, the practitioner does not offer ghee or grains, but offers the sense of separation itself.</p>
<p>To be <em>samāviṣṭaḥ</em>—“merged with the Goddess”—is not metaphorical. It is the total identification with Śakti, the energetic self-expression of Śiva. And in that union, <em>paraṃ bhairavam āpnuyāt</em>—the highest Bhairava is not “reached,” but recognized as one’s own nature. This is the true marriage: not of bodies, but of awareness with its own power.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 155 is the consummation of all 112 methods. The “recitation” is the silent rhythm of conscious breathing—slow, deep, extended in time (<em>dīrghātmā</em>, as in the previous verse), where each inhalation and exhalation becomes a mantra of presence.</p>
<p>The “sacrificial rite of great bliss” (<em>mahānandamaye ’dhvare</em>) is life itself, when lived without division. There is no altar apart from this moment; no offering apart from full attention. In this rite, the fire is awareness, the offering is the ego, and the priest is the Self.</p>
<p>“Merged with the Goddess” (<em>tayā devyā samāviṣṭaḥ</em>) signifies the end of duality between stillness and motion, Śiva and Śakti. The practitioner is no longer “doing” a practice—they <em>are</em> the practice: breath as devotion, body as temple, awareness as worship.</p>
<p>Thus, union with Bhairava is not a future event.  It is the natural state  when the illusion of separation  has been offered  into the fire of presence—  and what remains  is only one:  luminous, ecstatic.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>156</h1>
<h3>The 21,600 Breaths</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
सत्सष्टिं दिवरात्रौ सहस्रद्वयविंशतिः।<br>
जपो देव्याः समुद्दिष्टः सुलभो दुर्लभो जडैः॥१५६॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ṣaṭ-ṣaṣṭiṃ divārātrau sahasra-dvaya-viṃśatiḥ |<br>
japo devyāḥ samuddiṣṭaḥ sulabho durlabho jaḍaiḥ ||156||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Twenty-one thousand six hundred times<br />
in a day and night—<br />
this recitation of the Goddess<br />
is ever ongoing.<br />
Ordinary beings breathe it unconsciously;<br />
the wise breathe it with awareness.<br />
Thus, it is easy for all—<br />
yet difficult for the dull.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava reveals that every being, without exception, performs the <em>japa</em> of the Goddess—21,600 breaths in 24 hours—simply by living. This is the natural, automatic recitation (<em>samuddiṣṭaḥ</em>) of divine energy, the silent mantra of existence itself.</p>
<p>For the <em>jaḍa</em>—the spiritually inert, those asleep to their own nature—this remains mere biological function, yielding no awakening. But for the aware, the same breath becomes <em>sulabha</em>—effortlessly accessible as the highest practice—when infused with conscious will (<em>icchā</em>), as described in the prior verses: breathing slowly (<em>dīrghātmā</em>), reducing spatial extension (<em>tuti</em>), and extending temporal duration.</p>
<p>Thus, the japa is both universal and rare: universal in its occurrence, rare in its recognition. When breath is no longer mechanical but meditative—when each inhalation and exhalation is a full-hearted abidance in the present—the ordinary becomes the path to Bhairava.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verse 156 is a profound paradox: <strong>the most esoteric practice is the most ordinary act.</strong> Every human being, every animal, performs 21,600 “mantras” daily—not with lips, but with lungs. Yet only the awakened recognize this as <em>japa</em>.</p>
<p>The key lies not in <em>doing</em> more, but in <em>being present</em> to what is already being done. The “difficult” (<em>durlabha</em>) japa is not a different breath, but the same breath imbued with awareness—slowed, deepened, made spacious in time and subtle in space.</p>
<p>The reference to <em>tuṭi</em> (a unit of 2¼ finger-widths, with 16 such units spanning the traditional breath path) is not literal measurement, but symbolic refinement:<br />
- <strong>16 tuṭi</strong>: ordinary, scattered breathing;<br />
- <strong>15, 14...</strong>: conscious contraction of spatial dispersion, expansion of temporal presence.</p>
<p>As the breath lengthens inwardly, the mind gathers. After days or weeks of this subtle discipline, the practitioner is “born anew”—not through supernatural feat, but through the quiet death of unconsciousness.</p>
<p>Thus, the Vijñāna Bhairava does not demand extraordinary effort.  It asks only this: <strong>Breathe—and know that you breathe.</strong> In that knowing,<br />
the dog, the bear, the sage—  all recite the same mantra.  But only the aware  hear the silence between the breaths—  and in that silence,<br />
Bhairava is not found.  He is the breath itself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>157-158</h1>
<h3>Protection Of The Supreme Nectar</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
इत्येतत्कथितं देवि परमामृतमुत्तमम्।<br>
एतच्च नैव कस्यापि प्रकाश्यं तु कदाचन॥१५७॥<br>
परशिष्ये खले क्रूरे अभक्ते गुरुपादयोः।<br>
॥१५८॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
ity etat kathitaṃ devi paramāmṛtam uttamam |<br>
etac ca naiva kasyāpi prakāśyaṃ tu kadācana ||157||<br>
para-śiṣye khale krūre abhakte guru-pādayoḥ ||158||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Thus, O Goddess,<br />
I have revealed to you<br />
the supreme, immortal nectar—<br />
the highest of all.</p>
<p>Never disclose this<br />
to one who follows another tradition,<br />
who is deceitful or cruel,<br />
or who lacks devotion to the Guru's feet.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava concludes with a solemn injunction on the transmission of this highest wisdom. The <em>paramāmṛtam uttamam</em>—the supreme nectar—is not mere knowledge, but the living current of non-dual realization. It must be guarded not out of secrecy, but out of compassion: to reveal it to the unready is to invite misunderstanding, distortion, or misuse.</p>
<p>The unfit are described precisely:<br />
- <strong>Para-śiṣya</strong>: one rooted in another tradition, not yet matured beyond sectarian identity;<br />
- <strong>Khala</strong> and <strong>krūra</strong>: the deceitful and cruel, whose hearts are hardened by ego;<br />
- <strong>Abhakta guru-pādayoḥ</strong>: one without devotion to the Guru, thus lacking the humility that opens the heart to grace.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These verses are not exclusionary—they are protective. The Vijñāna Bhairava is not a text to be studied, but a mirror to be lived. To give it to one whose mind is still fragmented by doubt, sectarianism, or hardness of heart is like handing a sword to a child—it may be misused, or worse, turned against the giver.</p>
<p>The warning to withhold this wisdom is not elitism, but compassion. When truth is offered before the vessel is ready, it can become a weapon against itself. The unripe mind twists liberation into another form of bondage, non-duality into nihilism, and grace into arrogance.</p>
<p>True protection of the teaching means knowing when silence is the highest form of transmission. And in that silence, Bhairava is not hidden—he waits for the right moment to reveal himself.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>159-160</h1>
<h3>Transmission To The Worthy</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
निर्विकल्पमतीनां तु वीराणामुन्नतात्मनाम्॥१५९॥<br>
भक्तानां गुरुवर्गस्य दातव्यं निर्विशङ्कया।<br>
ग्रामो राज्यं पुरं देशः पुत्रदारकुटुम्बकम्॥१५९॥<br>
सर्वमेतत्परित्यज्य ग्राह्यमेतन्मृगेक्षणे।<br>
किमेभिरस्थिरैर्देवि स्थिरं परमिदं धनम्॥१६०॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
nirvikalpa-matīnāṃ tu vīrāṇām unnatātmanām ||159||<br>
bhaktānāṃ guru-vargasya dātavyaṃ nirviśaṅkayā |<br>
grāmo rājyaṃ puraṃ deśaḥ putra-dāra-kuṭumbakam ||159||<br>
sarvam etat parityajya grāhyam etan mṛgekṣaṇe |<br>
kim ebhir asthirair devi sthiraṃ param idaṃ dhanam ||160||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>But give it freely<br />
to those whose minds are free from doubt,<br />
to the heroic,<br />
and to those whose vision is vast.</p>
<p>To those devoted to the Guru's lineage,<br />
this nectar must be given without hesitation.</p>
<p>O gazelle-eyed one,<br />
renounce village, kingdom,<br />
city, country,<br />
son, wife, and family—<br />
and hold to this alone.</p>
<p>For what are these fleeting things, O Goddess?<br />
This alone is the imperishable wealth.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Having described who should not receive this teaching, Bhairava now clarifies the proper recipients: <em>nirvikalpa-matīnām</em>—those free from doubt and mental agitation; <em>vīrāṇām</em>—spiritual heroes, fearless in commitment to truth; <em>unnatātmanām</em>—those with expansive vision. To them, and especially to <em>bhaktānāṃ guru-vargasya</em>—those devoted not only to their immediate Master but to the entire lineage of awakened beings—the teaching must be imparted <em>nirviśaṅkayā</em>—without doubt or reservation, for their hearts are already open vessels.</p>
<p>He then issues a radical call: <em>sarvam etat parityajya</em>—"abandon all this"—not as literal command for every aspirant, but as a test of value. The true measure of one's readiness is whether one would, if necessary, forsake all worldly attachments—home, nation, family—for the sake of this truth.</p>
<p>For these—<em>grāma, rājya, pura, deśa, putra, dāra, kuṭumbakam</em>—are transient (<em>asthiraiḥ</em>). But this knowledge, this direct recognition of one's nature as Bhairava, is <em>sthiraṃ param idaṃ dhanam</em>—the supreme, unshakable treasure.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Verses 159-160 complete the teaching on transmission. The worthy disciple is not defined by external credentials but by inner qualities: freedom from doubt, spiritual courage, and expansive vision. Such a one doesn't merely "believe" the teaching—they recognize it as their own inner truth.</p>
<p>The call to renounce all attachments is not monastic instruction but metaphysical reordering of priorities. The list of what to "renounce" is comprehensive:<br />
- <strong>Grāma</strong> (village): local identity,<br />
- <strong>Rājya</strong> (kingdom): power and status,<br />
- <strong>Deśa</strong> (country): national allegiance,<br />
- <strong>Putra-dāra-kuṭumbakam</strong> (son, wife, family): emotional bonds.</p>
<p>All are sacred in their place—but none are ultimate. The teaching asks: <em>If you had to choose between the truth of your being and all these, which would you choose?</em></p>
<p>"Imperishable wealth" (<em>sthiraṃ param idaṃ dhanam</em>) is not metaphor. It is the only thing that cannot be stolen, lost, or destroyed—because it is your very self. Bank balances vanish. Bodies decay. Nations fall. But the recognition "I am Bhairava" stands untouched by time.</p>
<p>Thus, the final message is not ascetic renunciation, but <strong>inner non-attachment</strong>. Live in the world—but let your anchor be the nectar of recognition. Hold your loved ones—but know they are appearances in the one awareness. Serve your duties—but never confuse them with your true identity.</p>
<p>For in the end, all else slips through your fingers— but this truth you cannot lose, because you never possessed it.  You <em>are</em> it.</p>
</div>

---
<h1>161-163</h1>
<h3>Ultimate Fulfillment</h3>

<p class="sanskrit-block">
प्राणापि प्रदातव्यो न देयं परमामृतम्।<br>
श्रीदेव्युवाच।<br>
देव देव महादेव परितृप्तास्मि शङ्कर॥१६१॥<br>
रुद्रयामलतन्त्रस्य सारमद्यावबुध्यतम्।<br>
सर्वशक्तिप्रभेदानां हृदयं ज्ञातमद्य च॥१६२॥<br>
इत्युक्त्वानन्दिता देवी कण्ठे लग्ना शिवस्य तु॥१६३॥<br>
</p>

<p class="romanized-block">
prāṇāpi pradātavyo na deyaṃ paramāmṛtam |<br>
śrī-devy uvāca:<br>
deva deva mahādeva paritṛptāsmi śaṅkara ||161||<br>
rudrayāmala-tantrasya sāram adyāvabudhyatam |<br>
sarva-śakti-prabhedānāṃ hṛdayaṃ jñātam adya ca ||162||<br>
ity uktvānanditā devī kaṇṭhe lagnā śivasya tu ||163||<br>
</p>

<div class="translation">
<p>Even life may be surrendered—<br />
but never this supreme nectar.</p>
<p>The Goddess said:<br />
O God of gods, O Great God!<br />
I am utterly fulfilled, O Śaṅkara.</p>
<p>Today I have realized<br />
the essence of the Rudrayāmala Tantra—<br />
the heart of all powers and their divisions.</p>
<p>Having spoken thus,<br />
the bliss-filled Goddess<br />
merged into Śiva’s embrace.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Lakshmanjoo's Transmission</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>Bhairava’s final instruction—<em>na deyaṃ paramāmṛtam</em>—is not prohibition, but protection. The supreme nectar (<em>paramāmṛtam</em>) is the direct recognition of one’s nature as Bhairava. It is not a commodity to be traded, but the living core of existence itself. To “give it up” is to forget oneself; even life is secondary to this truth.</p>
<p>The Devi’s response—<em>paritṛptāsmi</em>—is the seal of realization. Her fulfillment is not emotional satisfaction, but the collapse of all seeking. She has not “learned” the essence of the Rudrayāmala; she has <em>become</em> it—<em>sāram avabudhyatam</em>, fully awakened to the core. The “heart of all powers” (<em>sarva-śakti-prabhedānāṃ hṛdayam</em>) is not a secret doctrine, but the pulsation of awareness itself.</p>
<p>Her embrace of Śiva (<em>kaṇṭhe lagnā śivasya</em>) is not physical union, but the dissolution of subject and object in non-dual being. There is no “she” merging into “him”—only the one reality, recognizing itself.</p>
</div>

<p class="center">Extended Commentary</p>
<div class="commentary">
<p>These final verses are not conclusion, but homecoming. Bhairava’s warning—<em>“Never give up this nectar”</em>—is the ultimate safeguard against spiritual materialism. This teaching is not a tool to be discarded when convenient; it is the ground of all being. To abandon it is to abandon life’s meaning—not because it is “holy,” but because it is <em>true</em>.</p>
<p>The Goddess’s declaration—<em>“I am fulfilled”</em>—is the natural state of seeing. No method remains, no path to walk. The 112 dharanas were not steps to climb, but mirrors reflecting what was always present. In that recognition, even “understanding” (<em>jñātam</em>) dissolves: she does not <em>possess</em> the truth; she <em>is</em> the truth.</p>
<p>The embrace is the final silence. No words remain. No “I” and “Thou.” Only the unbroken wholeness where Śakti and Śiva are not two. This is not union of separate beings, but the recognition that separation was never real.</p>
<p>Thus, the Vijñāna Bhairava does not end with a ritual or a command.  It ends as it began:  in the open sky of awareness,  where every breath is mantra, every step is pilgrimage, and every moment is Bhairava.</p>
<p><em>iti śrī-vijñāna-bhairave tantraṃ samāptam</em></p>
<p>Thus ends the sacred Vijñāna Bhairava Tantra.</p>
</div>

---
